26 Execuï؟½ve summary 27 The global context has shi ed dramaï؟½cally since 2019, with increased geopoliï؟½cal instability, soaring food 28 prices, and the COVID-19 pandemic exacerbaï؟½ng exisï؟½ng vulnerabiliï؟½es and creaï؟½ng new challenges. Yet 29 food systems remain squarely centred at the nexus of food security, human health, environmental 30 sustainability, social jusï؟½ce and the resilience of naï؟½ons. Acï؟½ons on food strongly impact the lives and 31 well-being of all and are necessary to progress toward criï؟½cal goals as arï؟½culated in the Sustainable 32 Development Goals, the Paris Climate Agreement, and the Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity 33 Framework, amongst others. While current food systems have largely kept pace with populaï؟½on growth, 34 ensuring sufficient caloric intake for many, they are the single most influenï؟½al driver of planetary 35 boundary transgression. More than half the world's populaï؟½on struggles to access healthy diets, leading 36 to devastaï؟½ng consequences for public health, social equity, and the environment. Although hunger has declined in certain regions, recent increases linked to expanding conflicts and emergent climate change

38 impacts have reversed this posiï؟½ve trend. Obesity rates conï؟½nue to rise globally, and the pressure exerted 39 by food systems on planetary boundaries shows no signs of abaï؟½ng. In this moment of increasing 40 instability, food systems sï؟½ll offer an unprecedented opportunity build the resilience of environmental, 41 health, economic and social systems and are uniquely placed enhance human well-being while 42 contribuï؟½ng massively to Earth system stability. 43 44 This updated analysis builds upon the 2019 EAT-Lancet Commission, expanding its scope and 45 strengthening its evidence base. The first Commission defined food group ranges for a healthy diet and 46 idenï؟½fied the food system shares of planetary boundaries. In this Commission, we add an analysis of the 47 social foundaï؟½ons for a just food system. It incorporates new data and perspecï؟½ves on distribuï؟½ve, 48 representaï؟½onal, and recogniï؟½onal jusï؟½ce, providing a global overview on equity in food systems. 49 Substanï؟½al improvements in modelling capacity and data analysis allow for the use of a mulï؟½-model 50 ensemble to project potenï؟½al outcomes of a transiï؟½on to healthy and sustainable food systems. 51 52 The Planetary Health Diet (PHD) remains a cornerstone of our recommendaï؟½ons and can be seen as a 53 framework within which diverse and culturally appropriate diets can exist. Robust updated evidence 54 reinforces a strong associaï؟½on with improved health outcomes, large reducï؟½ons in all-cause mortality, 55 and a substanï؟½al decrease in the incidence of major diet-related chronic diseases. The reference PHD 56 emphasises a balanced dietary paï؟½ern, predominantly plant-based, with moderate inclusion of animal- 57 source foods and limited consumpï؟½on of added sugars, saturated fats, and salt. The successful 58 implementaï؟½on of the PHD requires careful consideraï؟½on of cultural contexts and the promoï؟½on of 59 culturally appropriate and sustainable dietary tradiï؟½ons. This diversity of contexts, bounded by the PHDâ€™s 60 reference values, represents significant flexibility and choice across cultures, geographies and individual 61 preferences. However, confronted with todayâ€™s mulï؟½ple crises, transformaï؟½on will require urgent and 62 meaningful changes in our individual and collecï؟½ve behaviours and culture of unhealthy, unjust, and 63 unsustainable food producï؟½on and consumpï؟½on. 64 65 For the first ï؟½me we quanï؟½fy the global food system share of all nine planetary boundaries. These food 66 system boundaries (FSB) confirm that food is the single largest cause for transgression of the planetary 67 boundaries driving the transgression of five of the six breached boundaries. In addiï؟½on, food systems 68 exert a significant impact on the transgressed climate boundary and on the Ocean acidificaï؟½on boundary. 69 Food becomes the most significant driver of climate when its transportaï؟½on, and energy use contribuï؟½ons 70 are included. Unsustainable land conversion, parï؟½cularly deforestaï؟½on, remains a major driver of 71 biodiversity loss and climate change calling for zero conversion of remaining intact ecosystems. Food 72 systems account for the near totality of nitrogen and phosphorus boundary transgression, highlighï؟½ng a 73 criï؟½cal need for improvements in nutrient management, efficient nutrient redistribuï؟½on, and the 74 development of circular nutrient systems. The massive use of novel enï؟½ï؟½es in food producï؟½on, 75 processing, and packaging (ranging from plasï؟½cs to pesï؟½cides) remains of significant concern, yet 76 alarmingly understudied. 77 Our assessment of jusï؟½ce integrates three dimensionsâ€”distribuï؟½ve, representaï؟½onal, and recogniï؟½onalâ€”within a human rights framing that includes the rights to food, a healthy environment, and

80 decent work. Analyses reveal important inequiï؟½es in access to healthy diets, decent work condiï؟½ons, and 81 healthy environments, disproporï؟½onately affecï؟½ng marginalised groups in low-income regions. We 82 therefore propose nine social foundaï؟½ons that enable these rights to be met and assess their status. 83 Enabling access to, affordability of, and demand for healthy diets is paramount. Equally crucial is the right 84 to live and work within a healthy environment and a stable climate system, recognising the profound 85 impact of environmental degradaï؟½on on human health and well-being. Meanwhile, a living wage and 86 meaningful representaï؟½on would enable individuals to be recognized, heard, and parï؟½cipate in building 87 healthy, sustainable, and just food systems. However, nearly half of the world's populaï؟½on falls below 88 these foundaï؟½ons, undermining their ability to meet basic human rights. At the same ï؟½me, the dietary 89 paï؟½erns of most (6.9 billion people) of the world exert pressures that threaten to further transgress 90 planetary boundaries. The destabilising effect of unhealthy overconsumpï؟½on on the Earth's systems 91 highlights the criï؟½cal importance of viewing healthy diets not just as a human right, but also as a shared 92 responsibility. 93 94 A model intercomparison including ten integrated assessment models (IAMs) across mulï؟½ple scenarios 95 reveals the substanï؟½al potenï؟½al for reducing environmental and health impacts through dietary shi s, 96 improved agricultural producï؟½vity, and reducï؟½ons in food loss and waste. Creaï؟½ng demand for and 97 increasing adopï؟½on of diets that adhere to the PHD, coupled with ambiï؟½ous climate miï؟½gaï؟½on policies, 98 will result in significant reducï؟½ons in greenhouse gas emissions and land use. However, the results are 99 sobering, showing that even with the ambiï؟½ous transformaï؟½ons (sustainable intensificaï؟½on, reduced 100 food loss and waste, and transiï؟½on to eaï؟½ng within the PHD), the world is barely able to come back into 101 the safe space for freshwater use and climate change, while conï؟½nuing to transgress the biogeochemical 102 boundary for nitrogen and phosphorus loading-albeit with significant reduced pressure. 103 104 Deep-dive analyses focusing on sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on food producï؟½on pracï؟½ces, along 105 with more circular nutrient systems, suggest that widespread adopï؟½on of these pracï؟½ces could reduce 106 further greenhouse gas emissions , increasing carbon sequestraï؟½on , reducing the land area dedicated to 107 food producï؟½on, decreasing water footprints, and make substanï؟½al progress on addressing nitrogen and 108 phosphorus boundaries, even in the face of a growing global populaï؟½on and food consumpï؟½on. 109 110 To advance towards the goals of healthy (the PHD), sustainable (within the food system boundaries), and 111 just (above the social foundaï؟½ons) food systems by 2050, eight priority soluï؟½ons are proposed, each 112 accompanied by specific acï؟½ons and policy measures: (1) create food environment to increase demand 113 for healthy diets ensuring they are more accessible and affordable; (2) protect and promote healthy 114 tradiï؟½onal diets; (3) implement sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on pracï؟½ces; (4) apply strong 115 regulaï؟½ons to prevent loss of remaining intact ecosystems; (5) improve infrastructure, management, and 116 consumer behaviour change to reduce food loss and waste; (6) secure decent working condiï؟½ons; (7) 117 ensure meaningful voice and representaï؟½on; and (8) recognise and protect marginalised groups. These 118 proposed soluï؟½ons and acï؟½ons should be organized into coherent â€کbundlesâ€™ of intervenï؟½ons to enhance 119 poliï؟½cal feasibility and policy effecï؟½veness. The most suitable and effecï؟½ve bundles will vary by context and tailored to the specific challenges and opportuniï؟½es of each region and sector.

122 This Commission reinforces the urgent need for a â€œgreat food transformaï؟½onâ€‌. The targets of the EAT- 123 Lancet Commission for healthy people (PHD) on a healthy planet (FSB) with just food systems (social 124 foundaï؟½ons) can only be met through concerted global acï؟½on and unprecedented levels of transformaï؟½ve 125 change. The Commission calls for cross-sectoral coaliï؟½ons, that develop context-specific roadmaps, 126 aligning with exisï؟½ng and emerging global frameworks, such as the Paris Agreement, the Convenï؟½on on 127 Biological Diversity, and the post-2030 SDG agenda. These roadmaps include the seï؟½ng of science-based 128 targets with monitoring and accountability mechanisms in place. Mechanisms must be established to 129 insulate policymaking from undue corporate influence, while civil society and social movements play a 130 vital role in promoï؟½ng transparency and oversight. 131 132 Significant financial resources, esï؟½mated between US$200-500 billion per year, are needed to support the 133 transformaï؟½on to healthy, sustainable, and just food systems. However, there is good evidence that the 134 price of acï؟½on is much lower than the cost of inacï؟½on, and that the investments rapidly shi to economic 135 benefits, esï؟½mated at US$5 trillion per year. There is an opportunity to repurpose exisï؟½ng finance by 136 realigning incenï؟½ves with goals, such as shi ing exisï؟½ng support to producer communiï؟½es adopï؟½ng 137 sustainable ecological intensificaï؟½on pracï؟½ces or transiï؟½oning to the producï؟½on of currently under- 138 consumed food groups, while eliminaï؟½ng support to polluï؟½ng pracï؟½ces or to foods whose overproducï؟½on 139 drives poor health. 140 141 The Commission posiï؟½ons jusï؟½ce as both a goal and a driving force for food systems transformaï؟½on. Food 142 systems cannot be just without ensuring healthy diets that meet the PHD are affordable and accessible, 143 and without significantly reducing planetary boundaries transgressions. At the same ï؟½me, jusï؟½ce 144 is necessary to overcome the deeply entrenched structural barriers that currently impede transformaï؟½ve 145 change. In this sense, jusï؟½ce is not only an outcome of food systems transformaï؟½on, but a prerequisite 146 means for enabling it.

148 Introducï؟½on: Healthy, Sustainable, and Just Food Systems 149 The food system has an outsized impact on human wellbeing and planetary health. What we eat, and 150 where and how this food is produced and distributed strongly influences the length and quality of people's 151 lives, as well as our capacity to stay within planetary boundaries. How food systems are governed and 152 managed determine the extent to which people can parï؟½cipate and benefit. Since the 2019 publicaï؟½on of 1 153 Food in the Anthropocene: the EAT-Lancet Commission on healthy diets from sustainable food systems, 154 evidence from a growing number of sources show an alarming rise in stress to all natural systems on Earth, 155 escalaï؟½ng diet-related ill-health across the world, worrying inequaliï؟½es in food consumpï؟½on paï؟½erns 156 within and between countries, and disrupted food supplies. Given this context and the expected increase 157 in the global populaï؟½on to approximately 9.6 billion people in 2050, the need and urgency for a food 158 system transformaï؟½on to remain within 1.5 degrees C of global warming (Figure 1) and to meeï؟½ng a broad 159 range of global goals related to human health, environmental sustainability and social jusï؟½ce is clear.

Access to food is inequitable, with an insufficient access to healthy diets. 5 174 The food system produces enough calories to feed the worldâ€™s populaï؟½on, yet average diets trend to 3,6 175 low composiï؟½onal quality with global underconsumpï؟½on of whole grains, fruits, nuts, and vegetables. 1 176 Unhealthy overconsumpï؟½on, notably of red meat, is a leading driver of climate and land-use impact. Our 177 food supply is inherently sensiï؟½ve to water scarcity and climaï؟½c variability, and is increasingly sensiï؟½ve to 178 rapidly rising human climate change induced extreme events, like droughts, floods, heatwaves, or pest 179 and disease outbreaks. Prevailing food producï؟½on pracï؟½ces are driving biodiversity loss, water scarcity, 180 overuse of pesï؟½cides and overloading of ferï؟½lisers, climate change, and excessive use of anï؟½microbials. 181 Food prices do not reflect the true cost of unsustainable producï؟½on, while rising global poverty makes 7,8 182 healthy diets too expensive for billions of people. This is a major challenge, as it shows that the planet 183 is subsidizing cheap food as no environmental externaliï؟½es (e.g., biodiversity loss, nutrient overloading, 184 freshwater use, and GHG emissions) are paid for, while almost one billion people suffer from 185 undernutriï؟½on. The economic costs of these challenges are staggering, while global food system 186 generates US$10 trillion in value each year, the negaï؟½ve externaliï؟½es are esï؟½mated at US$15 trillion, with 9,10 the health sector contribuï؟½ng the most.

188 189 These trends drive criï؟½cal and unacceptable injusï؟½ces. The current socio-economic situaï؟½on for billions 190 of people in the world undermines fundamental human rights. Inadequate and inequitable access to safe, 191 sufficient, and nutriï؟½ous food for nearly 50% of the global populaï؟½on10 violates the right to food, 6 192 contribuï؟½ng to malnutriï؟½on, ill-health, and maternal and child mortality. The food system drives 193 transgression of mulï؟½ple planetary boundaries violaï؟½ng the right to a healthy environment and a stable 194 planet, resulï؟½ng in illness, and the loss of work, food, and life. Poor labour condiï؟½ons for many food 195 system workers, o en underpaid, and lacking social benefits, violate the right to decent work, resulï؟½ng in 11â€“13 196 exploitaï؟½on and low purchasing power. In addiï؟½on, the lack of representaï؟½on and freedom of 197 expression of many food system workers, alongside market concentraï؟½on, with a few large food system 198 firms influencing decision-making, policies, and prices, undermines the achievement of all of these 14â€“17 199 rights. 200 201 Growing aï؟½enï؟½on to food systems, but progress is slow 202 The global food agenda has made significant progress over the past five years. New evidence provides 203 stronger support for the importance of food for human health and planetary stability, and there is a rising 204 recogniï؟½on that food jusï؟½ce forms an integral part of successful food transformaï؟½ons. Scienï؟½fic aï؟½enï؟½on 205 to food systems has surged with new evidence emerging daily, including more than 10,500 citaï؟½ons of 206 the 2019 EAT-Lancet Commission alone. This evidence spans the development of planetary health diet 18,19 20,21 207 indices, naï؟½onal downscaling of dietary impacts on human health and environmental condiï؟½ons, 22â€“24 208 economic analyses of food systems transformaï؟½on assessments on novel technologies, and behaviour 209 change. Most of this evidence supports the 2019 findings, adding new insights and stronger support for 210 food policy, acï؟½on on public health, and raising public awareness, but it also signals need for addiï؟½onal 211 aï؟½enï؟½on to socioeconomic dimensions feasibility, and environmental impacts across all planetary 25 212 boundaries. 213 214 Complemenï؟½ng the growing aï؟½enï؟½on by the research community, a diversity of internaï؟½onal, naï؟½onal, 215 and local actors is driving a variety of commitments and iniï؟½aï؟½ves. A key moment came in 2021 with the 216 United Naï؟½ons Food Systems Summit (UNFSS). Since then, two thirds of countries have adopted naï؟½onal 217 food systems transformaï؟½on pathways and have commitments to integrate foods into their Naï؟½onally 218 Determined Contribuï؟½ons (NDCs) to the Paris Climate Agreement. More than 290 ciï؟½es, with a combined 219 populaï؟½on of 490 million, have signed onto the Milan Urban Food Policy Pact enacï؟½ng more than 620 26,27 220 specific food system acï؟½ons. Both the IPCC and IPBES have assessed food systems, noï؟½ng foodâ€™s 221 dependency, and impacts on biodiversity, climate, water, and health. The Kunming-Montreal Global 222 Biodiversity Framework signed in 2023 set specific food system targets. In these efforts, and other, food 223 is recognised as uniquely central in the nexus of human health, social jusï؟½ce, and environmental 28 224 sustainability. We are alarmed by recent efforts to undermine, hide, or obfuscate climate science, 225 environmental protecï؟½ons, and aï؟½enï؟½on to jusï؟½ce. Ignoring these trends aggravates, rather than resolves 226 the challenges they represent and their growing impact on human society. 227

# Criï؟½cal scienï؟½fic assessment

229 The growing aï؟½enï؟½on in science and pracï؟½ce is encouraging. Yet with few excepï؟½ons, emerging plans 29 230 have targets that are poorly arï؟½culated, non-specific with no accountability, insufficiently ambiï؟½ous, 30â€“32 231 include too few and siloed intervenï؟½ons, and lack appropriate financial support. The slow rate of 33,34 232 acï؟½on is unjust and dangerous to both current populaï؟½ons and future generaï؟½ons. Considering this 233 surge in evidence, and interest in food system acï؟½on, an updated assessment is criï؟½cally needed. Our 234 primary objecï؟½ve of this Commission is to provide that update. 235 236 First, we review new evidence published since 2019 on diet and health to provide the latest scienï؟½fic 237 update on the PHD paying specific aï؟½enï؟½on to research on dietary paï؟½erns and incorporaï؟½ng evidence 238 from a broader range of populaï؟½ons and world regions. We highlight long-established or tradiï؟½onal 239 dietary paï؟½erns that are in line with the PHD and emphasise the potenï؟½al of achieving culturally diverse 240 healthy diets. However, we also recognise that there is similarly ample evidence that â€کmodernâ€™ diets, 241 appropriately configured, can provide similar health benefits. The diversity of fruits, nuts, vegetables, and 242 whole grains available in many modern socieï؟½es, and the interest in ethnic cuisines from around the world 243 is unprecedented, as is the number of choices available to those desiring healthy opï؟½ons where they are 244 available. In addiï؟½on, we provide updated health impacts and nutriï؟½onal assessments of adopï؟½ng the 245 PHD and related dietary paï؟½erns by country and socio-demographic group. 246 247 Second, we review the evidence on the impact of food producï؟½on systems on the Earth system and assess 248 the current state of food systems across all nine planetary boundaries, updaï؟½ng the five quanï؟½fied in the 249 2019 EAT-Lancet Commission. For the first ï؟½me, we propose specific food system boundaries, as a food 250 system share of the safe operaï؟½ng space provided by the planetary boundaries, for use in target seï؟½ng 251 for food. We provide a novel assessment of how sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on food producï؟½on 252 pracï؟½ces can support a transformaï؟½on of food producï؟½on systems within safe food system boundaries. 253 254 Third, we establish social foundaï؟½ons of a just food system and assess where current food systems are 255 placed in relaï؟½on to the foundaï؟½ons. By adding a focus on jusï؟½ce, we place people at the centre of food 256 systems recognising that a just food system is one where human rights can be met. We assess the global 257 distribuï؟½on of injusï؟½ces focusing both on where unhealthy food consumpï؟½on exerts undue pressure on 258 planetary boundaries, and regions where social jusï؟½ce in relaï؟½on to the food system are not met. 259 260 Fourth we include a mulï؟½-model analysis to quanï؟½fy the consequences of a food systems transformaï؟½on 261 to healthy diets, sustainably produced by 2050. We include ten well-established agro-economic and 262 environmental models with socio-economic rebound effects to expand our understanding of the potenï؟½al 263 outcomes of the proposed food systems transformaï؟½on, including direct effects on food producï؟½on 264 quanï؟½ï؟½es and environmental impacts, and a greater consideraï؟½on of potenï؟½al socio-economic impacts. 265 266 Finally, based on the reviewed evidence, we propose soluï؟½ons and acï؟½ons to ensure access to, and 267 demand for, healthy, sustainable, and just food systems for all. While these soluï؟½ons are impacï؟½ul in 268 making healthy and sustainable diets available, accessible, and affordable, we emphasize the opportunity and need to ensure that foods offered must also be delicious and aspiraï؟½onal to ensure that demand

270 helps fuel their adopï؟½on. We discuss and suggest steps to develop roadmaps that can guide and 271 accelerate the urgent and systemic food systems transformaï؟½on that is needed. 272 273 While the challenges are daunï؟½ng, we find that achieving access to healthy diets for all, produced, 274 processed, distributed, and consumed fairly within planetary boundaries largely remains biophysically 275 possible. Important synergies between economic, social, and environmental outcomes exist, but are 276 currently missed in todayâ€™s socio-economic and socio-poliï؟½cal systems. These synergies should be beï؟½er 277 leveraged. Healthy diets are not only a fundamental right, but a shared responsibility. Major efforts are 278 needed to manage an increasingly urgent food systems transformaï؟½on that secures human and planetary 279 health, addressing the criï؟½cal lock-ins and poliï؟½cal economies inhibiï؟½ng progress on this necessary 280 transiï؟½on. The urgency to accelerate the â€œgreat food transformaï؟½onâ€‌ is undeniable. This Commission 281 provides clear quanï؟½taï؟½ve guidance on health, sustainability, and jusï؟½ce with the aim that this guidance 282 will assist food system actors to navigate this progress with greater confidence and impact. The â€œgreat 283 food transformaï؟½onâ€‌ needed to achieve healthy, sustainable, and just food systems is huge; it will require 284 the efforts of individuals, organisaï؟½ons and governments at levels from local to global and across 285 essenï؟½ally all sectors in society. 286 287 Treatment of Uncertainty 288 As with the 2019 Commission, we recognize that few decisions on dietary health, environmental 289 sustainability, and social jusï؟½ce can be made based on absolute certainty. In many cases, exisï؟½ng evidence 290 is incomplete, imperfect, and evolving. This Commission takes stock of how knowledge has evolved over 291 the past five years as one means of reducing this uncertainty. We have based our esï؟½mates on the best 292 science acknowledging uncertainï؟½es where they exist. Confidence levels are provided and improved 293 through wider systemic reviews and broader community efforts to test the validity of findings. For 294 example, a new contribuï؟½on of this Commissionâ€™s work is a mulï؟½-model intercomparison, involving 10 295 integrated assessment modelling teams, tesï؟½ng the contribuï؟½on of adopï؟½ng the PHD and transiï؟½on to 296 sustainable food producï؟½on pracï؟½ces, in feeding humanity within planetary boundaries (featured in 297 secï؟½on 4). This work highlights the degree of certainty, and variance, in outcome results. We have adopted 298 treatment of uncertainty in the same manner addressed by IPCC and IPBES. All of the models in the model 299 intercomparison exercise have calibrated their results ensuring that historical trajectories can be 300 reproduced. Considering the 2019 Commission, and the new research summarized here, we have a high 301 level of scienï؟½fic certainty about the overall direcï؟½on and magnitude of change required, although 302 considerable uncertainty can exist around some of the more detailed quanï؟½ficaï؟½ons. Our modelling work 303 considers major trends and opportuniï؟½es; however, it is important to recognize the myriads of pathways 304 that food systems actors might choose to navigate this transiï؟½on, and which we encourage them to 305 explore. 306 307 This Commission provides scienï؟½fic measures for healthy, sustainable, and just food systems based on 308 our current understanding. The model results are our current best esï؟½mate of plausible change (or lack 309 thereof), and their implicaï؟½ons for human health and planetary stability. However, the actual feasibility and impact will vary based on decisions and pathways adopted by individual socieï؟½es. We do not assess

311 how we got to our current state but evaluate a broad range of soluï؟½ons with demonstrated impacts in 312 the research literature and encourage food system actors to urgently evaluate and adopt appropriate 313 soluï؟½ons to their contexts. Here it is worth reminding that the PHD and the FSBs merely provide healthy 314 and sustainable boundary condiï؟½ons, within which a myriad of food dietary composiï؟½ons and pracï؟½ces 315 fit. It is beyond the scope of this Commission to prescribe which acï؟½ons should be adopted locally, and 316 we recognize the need for much greater interacï؟½ons between science, policy, and pracï؟½ï؟½oners in finding 317 context specific soluï؟½ons. 318 319

### Key Messages

1. Food systems sit at the nexus of health, environment, climate, and jusï؟½ce. A food systems transformaï؟½on is fundamental for solving the climate, biodiversity, crises, and the food related health and jusï؟½ce health crises. The nexus posiï؟½on of food systems emphasizes the interacï؟½ons between goals, rather than each goal separately. This calls for adopï؟½ng food system acï؟½ons as a global integrator across economic, governance and policy domains.
2. The updated Planetary Health Diet (PHD) has an appropriate energy intake, a diversity of whole or minimally processed foods that are mostly plant-sourced, fats that are primarily unsaturated with no parï؟½ally hydrogenated oils, and limited amounts of added sugars and salt. It allows flexibility and is compaï؟½ble with a wide variety of foods, cultures, dietary paï؟½erns, tradiï؟½ons, and individual preferences. The PHD provides both nutriï؟½onal adequacy and diminishes the risks of non-communicable diseases. At present, all naï؟½onal diets deviate substanï؟½ally from the PHD, but a shi to this paï؟½ern could avert approximately 15 million deaths per year (27% of total). Such transiï؟½on would reduce the rates of many specific non- communicable diseases and promote healthy longevity.
3. Food drives five out of six planetary boundary transgressions, including land system change, biosphere integrity, blue water change, biogeochemical flows, and novel enï؟½ï؟½es, while accounï؟½ng for approximately 30% of greenhouse gas emissions driving climate change. How and where food is produced, which foods are produced and consumed, and how much is lost and wasted, all contribute to planetary boundary transgressions. There is no safe soluï؟½on to the climate and biodiversity crises without a global food system transformaï؟½on. Even if the world succeeds with the global energy transiï؟½on away from fossil-fuels, the food system alone will make the world breach the Paris Climate agreement range of limiï؟½ng global mean surface temperature to 1.5آ°C.
4. Human rights related to food systems (the rights to food, a healthy environment, and decent work) are not being met, with nearly half the worldâ€™s populaï؟½on below social foundaï؟½ons. Meanwhile, across countries and people, responsibility for the planetary boundary transgressions from food systems is not equal: the diets of the richest 30% of the global populaï؟½on contribute over 70% of the environmental pressures from food systems. This highlights the large inequaliï؟½es in the distribuï؟½on of both benefits and burdens of current food systems. Naï؟½onal policies that address inequiï؟½es in the distribuï؟½on of both benefits and burdens of current food systems would go some way to meeï؟½ng human rights as they relate to food systems.
5. The Planetary Health Diet needs to be available, affordable, convenient, aspiraï؟½onal, and delicious. To increase demand for healthy sustainable diets and enable necessary dietary

shi s, food environment intervenï؟½ons, increased purchasing power, and protecï؟½on and promoï؟½on of healthy tradiï؟½onal diets are important acï؟½ons.

6. An EAT-Lancet aligned food system transformaï؟½on that includes a shi to healthy diets, improved agricultural producï؟½vity, and reduced food loss and waste would substanï؟½ally reduce environmental pressures on emissions and land-, water-, nitrogen-, and phosphorous- use. However, no single acï؟½on is sufficient to ensure a healthy, just, and sustainable food system. Comparing 2050 values to current state, a shi to healthy diets in isolaï؟½on could lead to a 15% reducï؟½on in agricultural emissions, compared to 20% when all three acï؟½ons are implemented. Individually all three acï؟½ons modestly reduce future nitrogen-and phosphorous-use (27-34% vs 41% increase compared to 2020 under business as usual), however in combinaï؟½on they substanï؟½ally reduce future growth in nitrogen-and phosphorous-use (15% increase compared to 2020)
7. Addiï؟½onal nature-posiï؟½ve benefits are accrued through sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on pracï؟½ces. Unprecidented investments and effort in these pracï؟½ces potenï؟½ally making the food system net carbon negaï؟½ve. A diversity of context-specific pracï؟½ces can sequester addiï؟½onal carbon biomass, create and connect habitat, reduce nutrient applicaï؟½ons and increase intercepï؟½on and capture of excessive crop ferï؟½liser before it pollutes ground and surface water systems. These pracï؟½ces can be enabled by securing equitable access to land and water resources, strengthening public advisory services, addressing structural imbalances between producers and dominant agribusinesses, and through public and private sector investments that support farmers shi ing towards sustainable pracï؟½ces.
8. An EAT-Lancet aligned food system transformaï؟½on could lead to a less resource- and labour- intensive food system capable of supplying a healthy diet for 9.6 billion people with modest impacts on food costs. This would however have profound implicaï؟½ons regarding what, how, and where food is produced and those involved in these processes. For example, as a part of this restructuring some sectors would need to contract (e.g., ruminant meat: -33%) and others would need to expand (e.g. fruits, vegetables, and nuts: +63%) compared to 2020 producï؟½on levels.
9. Jusï؟½ce is needed to unlock and accelerate acï؟½on for transformaï؟½on. A fair distribuï؟½on of opportuniï؟½es and resources, such that the rights to food, a healthy environment, and decent work are met, and a fair distribuï؟½on of the responsibility to produce, distribute, and consume healthy diets within planetary boundaries are the basis of a successful food systems transformaï؟½on. Power asymmetries, discriminatory social and poliï؟½cal structures prevent these rights from being met, which results in harms to health of people, precarious livelihoods of food systems workers, and lack of voice undermining freedom, agency, and dignity. Ensuring liveable wages, and collecï؟½ve bargaining while regulaï؟½ng and limiï؟½ng market concentraï؟½on and improving transparency, accountability, representaï؟½on, and access to informaï؟½on are all

impacï؟½ul acï؟½ons. We emphasize the protecï؟½on of basic human rights in conflict areas a as fundamental foundaï؟½on of jusï؟½ce.

10. Unprecedented levels of acï؟½on are required to shi diets, improve producï؟½on, and enhance jusï؟½ce. A just transformaï؟½on requires building coaliï؟½ons with actors from inside and outside the food system; idenï؟½fying bundles of acï؟½ons; developing naï؟½onal and regional roadmaps for implementaï؟½on; unlocking finance for the transformaï؟½on; and rapidly puï؟½ng joint plans into acï؟½on. Such efforts should closely align with other sustainability and health iniï؟½aï؟½ves (Paris Agreement, Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity Framework, Naï؟½onal Food Based Dietary Guidelines). These frameworks have equally idenï؟½fied food system acï؟½ons as powerful, parï؟½cularly in their capacity to integrate across goals. Mobilising and repurposing finance is criï؟½cal to enabling this transformaï؟½on

323 Glossary Planetary Health Diet Based on latest evidence on diet and health, the Planetary Health Diet represents a dietary paï؟½ern that supports opï؟½mal health outcomes, and which can be applied globally for different populaï؟½ons and different contexts while supporï؟½ng cultural and regional variaï؟½on. It emphasizes a plant-rich diet where whole grains, fruits, vegetables, nuts, and legumes comprise a greater proporï؟½on of foods consumed, with moderate or small amounts of fish, dairy, and meat. The Planetary Health Diet is enï؟½rely based on direct effects of diets on human health, not on environmental criteria. We refer to it as the Planetary Health Diet since there is good evidence that its adopï؟½on would lower the environmental impacts and nutriï؟½onal deficiencies of most current diets.

Food System Boundaries Food system boundaries are science-based targets represenï؟½ng the share of the safe operaï؟½ng space for food systems. They are based on the available evidence represenï؟½ng the required or possible miï؟½gaï؟½on of food systems to return or remain within planetary boundaries, including the present-day contribuï؟½on of food systems to planetary boundary transgressions in relaï؟½on to other sectors, esï؟½mates of minimum environmental impacts from food systems that are hard-to-abate (i.e. through opï؟½mizaï؟½on modelling across sectors), and esï؟½mates of limited Earth system impact while remaining producï؟½ve agricultural systems.

Sustainable and Ecological Intensificaï؟½on (SEI) Sustainable Intensificaï؟½on (SI) entails achieving important reducï؟½ons in the environmental impacts though increased efficiency, reduced losses, and reduced polluï؟½on. Ecological Intensificaï؟½on (EI) is a subset of SI that enhances the environmental performance by promoï؟½ng ecological processes within agricultural fields, farms and landscapes, such as above and below ground carbon sequestraï؟½on, nutrient cycling and storage, pollinaï؟½on, and biological pest regulaï؟½on.

Social Foundaï؟½ons Social foundaï؟½ons require basic human rights, such as food, water, health, and energy, to be met for everyone. We build on previous work defining the minimum resources required to meet peopleâ€™s human rights, to avoid resource deprivaï؟½ons and harms, and focus on the minimum condiï؟½ons that enable peopleâ€™s human rights to be met.

Great Food Transformaï؟½on An unprecedented commitment globally and naï؟½onally to an interlinked range of acï؟½ons to be taken by all sectors to make healthy food accessible to allâ€”produced, processed, distributed, and consumed fairly within planetary boundaries

327 Section 1: What is a Healthy Diet? 328 We review the evidence on diet and health and present reference values for food group intakes in the 1 329 reference Planetary Health Diet (PHD) (Table 1). Since the 2019 Commission, we included additional 330 health outcomes, considered effects of food processing, and examined implications for young children 331 and women of reproductive age. The relation between the PHD with mortality and other health outcomes 332 has now been examined in numerous epidemiological studies that we summarise. This section also 333 describes gaps between current dietary intakes and the PHD in different regions, and how different 334 dietary cultures may align with the PHD. 335 336 Approach to Defining Healthy Diets 35 337 Healthy diets should be adequate, diverse, balanced, and moderate. Defining healthy diets for a global 338 population is complicated because nutritional requirements vary by, for example, age, sex, body size, 339 physical activity level, pregnancy and lactation, health status, and genetic factors. However, the effects of 36,37 340 diet are similar across ancestry or ethnic groups due to common underlying human biology. Here, we 341 focus on diets of generally healthy populations two years and older, although the PHD is also key to 342 resolving health risks in populations facing a high prevalence of undernutrition. 343 344 We have described healthy diets as combinations of foods as this most directly connects health with food 345 production and consumption. Nutrient adequacy is important and included in our analyses, but our 346 primary considerations are specific health outcomes related to insufficient or excessive food intakes, 347 rather than numerical targets for essential nutrients (e.g., average requirements (ARs) or estimated 348 average requirements (EARs)). Nutrient requirements are largely based on small, short-term studies, and 349 are often not supported by sufficient evidence for long-term overall health. In addition to food groups, 350 we include added fats, sugar, and salt as they are substantial components of most diets. Our conclusions 351 assume that most foods are consumed whole, unprocessed, or minimally processed. 352 353 Although the PHD is based on direct effect of diets on health, we also discuss potential indirect effects of 354 diets on health, including antimicrobial resistance, pandemic risk, and those mediated by environmental 355 changes (Panel 3). Control of microbial and other contaminants of food and water is critical but not 356 comprehensively reviewed here. While the cost and affordability of diets, and the environmental impacts 357 of their production and consumption, are critical issues and influence peopleâ€™s access to healthy diets, 358 these factors do not define what is healthy in a diet. We address those separately in other sections. 359 360 Review of Evidence on Diet and Health 361 The goal of this Commission in defining the PHD is to provide the evidence and a quantitative description 362 of a healthy dietary pattern that can be applied globally for different populations and different contexts 363 while supporting cultural and regional variation. In summarising evidence and evaluating causality, we 364 considered prospective epidemiologic studies, randomised trials with intermediate risk factors as 365 endpoints, and the few available randomised trials with health endpoints (Panel 1). Because changes in dietary components by individuals or populations are usually made within a constrained total energy intake, isocaloric substitution analyses comparing specific dietary components, such as red meat versus

368 legumes, nuts, or fish, are particularly informative because these represent realistic dietary choices. A key 369 part of this Commission is the evaluation of the PHD as an overall dietary pattern in relation to mortality 370 and other health outcomes. 371 Panel 1: The Planetary Health Diet and Health: Evidence for Causality Ideally, evidence for effects of specific foods, nutrients, and overall diets on major health outcomes would be based on large, randomised trials with disease and functional endpoints in representative populations across the world. However, this type of evidence is limited because such trials require many thousands of participants, many years or even decades of follow-up, and sustained high adherence to assigned diets. This is neither feasible nor ethical, and randomised trials not meeting these criteria can easily yield misleading, null or negative results. Thus, for studies of diet, and other behavioural and environmental exposures, frameworks to assess causality, such as the Bradford-Hill criteria, P1 have been developed. For dietary factors, these typically include reproducible evidence from prospective epidemiologic studies (e.g., following a cohort over time) in combination with randomised trials that include intermediate risk factors as outcomes. P2 Rigorous control of confounding factors by study design and statistical methods is essential.

An example illustrates the value of combined evidence from observational and experimental studies in informing the links between the PHD and health. A central feature of the PHD is the importance of nuts and legumes, including beans, pulses, and soy as major protein sources, with limited amounts of red meat and dairy foods. The far higher ratio of polyunsaturated to saturated fatty acids in these plant foods compared with red meat and dairy products predicts lower LDL-cholesterol levels; this was confirmed in a meta-analysis of randomised trials of red meat intake. P3 In long-term cohort studies with repeated assessments of diet, mortality was lowest when plant protein was compared to red meat, dairy foods, and poultry; P4 associations with type 2 diabetes and coronary heart disease have been similar. P5 Other key features of the PHD include relatively high amounts of fruits and vegetables, shown to lower blood pressure in the Dietary Approaches to Stop Hypertension (DASH) randomised trial; P6 whole grains, shown to improve blood lipids in randomised trials; P7 and largely unsaturated plant oils, shown to reduce LDL-cholesterol. P8 Components of the PHD may operate by additional pathways, including modulation of the microbiome, but at a minimum, beneficial effects on firmly established risk factors are clear. As described in the main text, in multiple large cohort studies with careful adjustment for smoking and other potentially confounding variables, participants with diets most consistent with the PHD have had lower risks of many major health outcomes, including type 2 diabetes, cardiovascular disease, and total mortality.

Important causal evidence for benefits of the PHD also comes from decades of research on the traditional Mediterranean dietary pattern, a specific example of the PHD. P9 In addition to the types of evidence from observational studies and trials of intermediate endpoints described above, P10 in a large, randomised trial in people with raised cardiovascular disease risk, a Mediterranean diet with

|||P11|P12|
|---|---|---|---|
|P13,P14|P15|||
||||P16|
|P17|P18|||

added nuts or olive oil reduced the risk of cardiovascular disease, type 2 diabetes, cognitive decline, and other adverse health outcomes. In another randomised trial, adherence to a Mediterranean diet reversed the prevalence of a pre-diabetic state by half over one year, and in a two-year randomised trial, this dietary pattern reduced body weight compared to low fat or high fat diets; weight loss was sustained at six years.

Together, the combination of evidence on the composition of food groups, randomised trials with risk factors as outcomes, and large cohort studies provides strong evidence that adherence to the PHD will have major health benefits. This conclusion is further reinforced by randomised trials of the Mediterranean diet, a dietary pattern that is one example of the PHD, documenting benefits for major health outcomes. 372 373 374 Based on this body of evidence, PHD reference values (Table 1) were developed to provide a foundation 375 for optimal health outcomes, and to use as a starting point to evaluate global environmental impacts of 376 food consumption that are addressed in subsequent sections. Importantly, for individuals, these values 377 should not be considered as exact targets, but rather represent approximate ranges and proportions, with 378 potential substitutions among foods to accommodate different total energy intakes and preferences. An 379 updated review of available evidence is included in Appendix 1A; a summary is provided here. 380 381 Dietary energy requirements vary greatly between individuals depending on age, sex, body size, physical 382 activity, and metabolic factors. We estimated an average global energy intake of 2400 kcal/day for adults 383 using a new doubly-labelled water-based methodology, assuming an ideal BMI of 22 kg/m2 and an active 38 384 level of physical activity (Appendix 1C). Assuming current average global physical activity levels and BMI 385 among adults of approximately 25 kg/m2 leads to a similar energy intake. 386 387 Grains, including a diversity of options such as wheat, rice, maize, oats, barley, rye, teff, amaranth, fonio, 388 buckwheat, millets, quinoa, and others are the primary sources of energy worldwide and an important 389 source of fibre, protein, and many micronutrients. Consumption of whole grains (100% of the bran, germ, 390 and endosperm in an intact grain), but not refined (depleted) grains, improves blood lipid values and other 40,41 42 391 biomarkers39 and is inversely associated with risks of weight gain, type 2 diabetes, coronary heart 43,44 43 45 392 disease, colorectal cancer, and total mortality. The inverse association between intake of whole 393 grains and weight appears linear, but a concern is that high intake of whole grains could displace other 394 healthy foods and reduce micronutrient absorption. Thus, we used 210 g/day dry weight as a reference 395 value for grains (midway within a ~20-50% of energy), considering this to be flexible and emphasising 396 whole grains versus refined grains. 397 398 Tubers, including potatoes, sweet potatoes, taro, yams, and cassava amongst others, are a major energy 399 source in some populations, but commonly consumed white potatoes do not provide the amounts of fibre 400 or micronutrients as do whole grains. Compared with whole grains or non-starchy vegetables, 47,48 401 consumption of potatoes is associated with greater weight gain46 and risk of type 2 diabetes, and the 47,48 402 association is stronger for fried potatoes than non-fried potatoes. Data on other tubers are limited. 403 Because these findings support consumption of whole grains over potatoes as a major energy source, we 404 used 50 g/day as the reference value. 405 Fruits and vegetables, including seaweeds and other aquatic plants, are a primary source of many 40,49 nutrients and phytochemicals. Intakes are inversely associated with risks of weight gain, type 2

50,51 51 52,53 54 diabetes, coronary heart disease, some cancers, and total mortality. More recent evidence 55â€“57 supports a reduced risk of cognitive decline and dementia. Because of differences in the composition of specific fruits or vegetables and their distinct associations with health outcomes, a variety is desirable, specifically including green leafy and dark orange foods. Most health benefits accrue with about five servings per day; we used 500 g/day as the reference value with no specified upper limit.

Nuts, legumes, and seeds provide protein, unsaturated fats, fibre, and many micronutrients. Nuts and 58 legumes have beneficial effects on blood lipids, and consumption of nuts, including peanuts, have been 51 51,59 60,61 associated with lower risks of type 2 diabetes, coronary heart disease, and total mortality. Soy foods contain substantial amounts of both omega-3 and omega-6 fatty acids and are uniquely high in 62,63 phytoestrogens, and consumption has been associated with lower risks of cardiovascular disease and 64 breast cancer. As nuts and legumes are healthy sources of protein, the reference value includes 125 g/day dry weight (about 3-5 servings per day of legumes, nuts, and seeds, including at least 1 serving of nuts); a variety is encouraged.

408 409 410 411 412 413 414 415 416 417 418 419 420 421 422 423 424 425 426 427 428 429 430 431 432 433 434 435 436 437 438 439 440 441 442 443 444 445 446 447

Red (mammalian) meat is high in protein, haem iron, and other minerals, but is also high in saturated fat and cholesterol and low in essential polyunsaturated fatty acids. In populations consuming very high amounts of carbohydrates, small amounts of red meat or other animal-source foods can provide critical amounts of essential nutrients that have positive health benefits. Consistent with the fatty acid composition, and compared with plant sources of protein such as nuts, soy, and other legumes, consumption of red meat increases blood levels of LDL-cholesterol 39,65 and is associated with increased risk of coronary heart disease. 51,66 Also, consumption of red meat is linearly associated with elevated risk of type 2 diabetes among different populations, including Asia, Europe, and North America; both unprocessed and processed red meat contribute to risk but the latter association is stronger. 37,67 Red meat intake has also been positively associated with unhealthy weight gain and risks of gestational diabetes, 67

colorectal cancer (especially processed red meat), 68 frailty, 69 and unhealthy ageing. 70,71 Consumption of red meat is associated with higher risk of total mortality in countries that have been consuming high amounts for many decades. 72

Because the association of red meat with type 2 diabetes has been documented in many large studies, 37,51,66,67,73â€“75 and diabetes is a sentinel disease for many adverse cardiometabolic outcomes, this dose-response relationship was used to determine the reference value in Table 1. According to data from three large US cohorts with over 22,000 incident cases of diabetes and many repeated measures of diet over three decades, the increase in diabetes incidence with increase in red meat consumption was approximately linear with a minimal risk close to zero consumption of red meat. 76 We used 15 g/day (about one serving per week) as the reference value with a range including zero to allow a modest intake while avoiding statistically significant increases in risk (see Appendix 1A for additional details).

Poultry meat has nutritional values similar to red meat but an intermediate polyunsaturated fat composition between red meat and plant protein sources. Associations with risks of non-communicable diseases and mortality have also generally been intermediate; 37,77,78 an inverse association with stroke has

449 450 451 452 453 454 455 456 457 458 459 460 461 462 463 464 465 466 467 468 469 470 471 472 473 474 475 476 477 478 479 480 481 482 483 484 485 486 487 488

been an exception. 51 Because these relationships suggest a priority of poultry over red meat, we used 30 g/day of poultry (about two servings per week) as the reference value.

Eggs are a concentrated source of protein and essential nutrients and can play an important role in childhood diets. They are also high in dietary cholesterol and have generally been intermediate with respect to non-communicable diseases risk 79,80 except for positive associations with risks of coronary heart disease among people with diabetes. 81,82 Consistent with other guidelines, we used 15 g/day or about two eggs/week as the reference value.

Milk and derivatives have broad nutritional value but high content of saturated fat and little polyunsaturated fat. High calcium content has been a primary justification for substantial lifetime consumption. However, calcium requirements have been based on short-term studies, and milk intake has not been clearly related to risk of bone fractures. 83 Consistent with its fatty acid composition, dairy fat substantially increases LDL-cholesterol compared to unsaturated plant oils. 84 Epidemiologic evidence is largely based on cow milk; as predicted, dairy fat is positively associated with cardiovascular disease. 85

Risks of cardiometabolic disease51 and overall mortality 86,87 associated with milk intake are intermediate between red meat and healthy plant sources of protein. Consumption of dairy foods is associated with low risks of colorectal cancer88 but elevated risks of prostate cancer. 89 Some evidence suggests that yoghurt consumption, and possibly other fermented products, may be associated with less weight gain90 and reduced risks of type 2 diabetes51 compared with other dairy foods. We used 250 g/day of milk or the equivalent amount of milk derivatives (one serving per day) as the reference value because of its intermediate relation with non-communicable diseases risk and substantial contribution to calcium intake; the range includes zero because many populations globally do not consume milk and still have low fracture risk. 83 In a recent meta-analysis, a small decrease in risk of cardiovascular disease was seen with about one serving/day of dairy foods, especially low fat milk and cheese, but liï؟½le further change in risk with higher intakes. 91

Fish, shellfish, and other aquatic animal-source foods are major sources of protein and essential nutrients for many populations, including in low and middle-income countries. In many diets, small fish and bivalves are often overlooked as potential foods. Collectively, these foods are particularly important as a source of omega-3 fatty acids (minimally present in terrestrial animal-source foods); low intakes of omega-3 fatty acids can slow down neurologic development in childhood92 and possibly with cognitive decline in adults; 93 high intakes of omega-3 fatty acids are associated with reduced risk of cardiovascular disease. 94,95 The relation between intake of fish or omega-3 fatty acids and cardiovascular diseases appears to be non-linear; 94 most of the benefit is achieved with 30 g/day of fish (about two 100-g servings per week), which is used as the reference value.

Added fats Evidence from prospective cohort studies and randomised trials has not suggested a reduction in cardiovascular disease96 or cancer97 by decreasing total fat intake. However, added fats differ greatly in their composition and health effects; partial hydrogenation creates deleterious trans isomers that are associated with increased risks of cardiovascular diseases98 and all-cause mortality99 and this process is now banned in many countries. Non-hydrogenated plant oils that contain mainly unsaturated fatty

101 491 acids reduce LDL-cholesterol, and intake is strongly related to lower risks of cardiometabolic diseases 102 492 and total mortality. These inverse associations appear linear within the ranges studied. Palm oil is 493 relatively high in saturated fat and low in polyunsaturated fatty acids; although studies of health outcomes 103 494 are limited, available evidence favours relatively unsaturated plant oils. Compared with plant oils, dairy 495 fat increases LDL-cholesterol and is associated with increased risk of cardiovascular disease and total 84,85,91 496 mortality. Because non-hydrogenated plant oils have positive health effects, intake can be flexible 497 within limits for total energy, for example, if exchanged isocalorically with whole grains. We used an intake 498 of 17% of energy (primarily as unsaturated plant oils including both omega-6 and omega-3 fatty acids) as 499 a reference value, which is similar to that in a Mediterranean diet that reduces incidence of cardiovascular 104 500 disease. 501 502 Added or free sugars provide no nutritional value and can cause harms in excessive intakes. Intakes, 503 particularly in the form of sugar-sweetened beverages, have adverse cardiometabolic effects and have 105 106 107 504 been positively associated with weight gain, type 2 diabetes, coronary heart disease, and total 106 505 mortality. Consistent with conclusions of the WHO108 and national guidelines, a maximum intake of 5% 506 of energy from added or free sugars is used as a reference value. 507 508 Sodium, commonly consumed as salt or mono-sodium glutamate, is essential, but intakes greater than 509 1500 mg/day increase blood pressure, and intakes greater than about 2000 mg/day are linearly associated 109 510 with an increased risk of cardiovascular disease. Thus, up to 2000 mg/day sodium (5 g/day as salt) was 511 used as the reference value. 512 513 Food processing Minimal processing, such as drying grains, pulses and nuts, chilling or freezing fruits and 514 vegetables, pasteurizing milk and fermenting milk into yoghurt, reduce food microbial contamination, 110 515 extend freshness, enhance sensory properties, facilitate preparation, add variety to diets, and reduce 516 the time and labour costs of food preparation. Micronutrient fortification can improve micronutrient 517 density, notably for nutritionally vulnerable populations. However, processing can also have adverse 518 effects, including reduction in food nutritional content, addition of excessive salt and sugar, and use of 519 unhealthy fats and refined grains. Processes that destroy the structure of whole foods, chemically alter 520 their components, and assemble them with a variety of sensory-related additives into products that are 521 liable to displace unprocessed or minimally processed foods and freshly prepared dishes and meals are 110 522 considered ultra-processing. Dietary patterns high in ultra-processed foods have been associated with 523 thirty-two adverse health outcomes spanning mortality, cancer, and mental, respiratory, cardiovascular, 111 524 gastrointestinal, and metabolic health outcomes. The Commission concluded that most foods should 525 be consumed whole, unprocessed, or minimally processed. 526 527 Overall Dietary Patterns and Health 528 Evaluations of overall dietary patterns in relation to risk of health outcomes represent the combined 529 effects of all dietary components, including their interactions (Appendix 1A). Dietary patterns have been 530 developed to describe traditional diets of specific regions or cultures (e.g., Mediterranean or 112,113 Japanese), using statistical approaches such as principal components or reduced rank regression

114 532 analysis, or, as for the PHD, based on available evidence relating dietary components to health 533 outcomes. 534 535 In meta-analyses, dietary patterns similar to the PHD were associated with lower risk of all-cause 115,116 536 mortality. Within three large cohorts, the Mediterranean dietary score and five other commonly 537 used indices of diet quality were similarly inversely associated with overall mortality and a composite 117 538 outcome of major chronic diseases. Within-person improvements in dietary quality scores over time 118 539 were associated with lower risks of all-cause mortality. These and other dietary patterns have also been 119,120 540 associated with many other outcomes, including lower risks of depression and dementia. Over recent 541 decades, supported by extensive analyses in many populations, a substantial convergence has developed 542 over the broad definition of a healthy dietary pattern. 543 544 Since the 2019 Commission, multiple investigators have created indices representing consistency with the 18,121 545 PHD, which is strongly correlated with other dietary quality indices. In prospective studies across the 546 world, significant inverse associations were reported for the PHD and a number of health outcomes 547 including total cardiovascular diseases, ischaemic heart disease, myocardial infarction, atrial fibrillation, 548 stroke, heart failure, type 2 diabetes, obesity, colorectal cancer, lung cancer, and other cancers. A few 549 studies reported null associations, but these findings need to be placed in the context of methodological 122â€“124 550 limitations (see Appendix 1A). In an analysis of 206,404 men and women followed for up to 33 years, 551 over 54,000 deaths were documented; overall mortality was 28% lower comparing the highest to the 18 552 lowest decile of adherence to the PHD (Appendix 1A). Although findings on the PHD and mortality in 125,126 553 two cohorts of Chinese adults are consistent with what has been seen in the United States and 554 Europe (Appendix 1A), data from low- and middle-income countries are limited by the lack of large cohort 555 studies in most regions. Associations could vary depending on dietary preferences within food groups, 556 and further research from those areas is needed. 557 558 Table 1 presents reference values of the PHD pattern for flexitarian adults. Scenarios for vegetarian or 559 pescatarian dietary patterns and different total energy intakes are presented in Appendix 1A. The PHD 1 560 pattern described in the current Commission is not substantially different from the 2019 Commission. 561 The dietary components in Table 1 have been re-ordered to facilitate interpretation and with slight 562 rounding of several numbers. Total population level adult energy intake is specified at 2400 kcal/day 563 (instead of 2500 kcal/day as in the 2019 Commission) based on updated values (Appendix 1B) and 38 564 recognising that energy requirements vary by individuals. 565 566 567

568 Table 1: Dietary targets for a healthy reference diet for adults, with possible ranges, for a population level energy a 569 intake of ~2,400 kcal/day Most foods are assumed to be unprocessed or minimally processed. The targets, ranges, 570 and exchanges (see footnotes) in this flexitarian version of the PHD are intended to provide flexibility within a specific 571 energy intake while limiting intake of animal-source foods to approximately two servings per day with one being 572 dairy (250 g milk equivalents) and one being non-dairy (e.g. 75 to 100 g from among fish, poultry, red meat or eggs). 573 Various versions of this dietary pattern, including specific vegetarian, vegan, or pescatarian diets, those of different 574 food cultures, and with different total energy intakes are described in Appendix 1B. 590 Nutrient Adequacy of the PHD 591 The primary evaluation of nutrient adequacy of an overall diet is based on health outcomes, which 592 represent the cumulative consequences of all aspects of diet quality, including intakes of total calories, 593 essential nutrients, other dietary components, effects of processing, and their interactions. We evaluated 594 the nutrient adequacy of the PHD for adults compared to current diets by pairing estimated intake with 595 nutrient densities and comparing those to the average requirements of each population group (Appendix 127 596 1D). Current intake was estimated by triangulating waste-adjusted food availability data with data from 597 dietary surveys and estimated energy requirements (Appendix 1F). 598 599 For most nutrients, the PHD is adequate and performs better than current average diets, notably for fatty 600 acid profile, protein, free/added sugar, fibre, folate, magnesium, potassium, and zinc (Appendix 1D). 601 Intakes of calcium, vitamin B12, iron, and iodine from the PHD warrant further attention, especially in 602 populations that have low dietary diversity. Important differences are observed by national income 603 (Appendix 1D). In low-income countries, required intakes of most nutrients in the PHD are substantially 604 higher than current intakes, while in higher income countries, intakes of some micronutrients like vitamin 605 B12 and B6 are lower than current intakes, while remaining adequate. Opï؟½misaï؟½on within the PHD 606 reference values for general food groups (e.g., increasing the proporï؟½on of green leafy vegetables for 607 iron, fermented soy foods for B12, and algae for B12 and iodine) can ensure nutriï؟½onal adequacy for all 128 608 populaï؟½on groups. On average, calcium intake in the PHD is higher than current intakes, but it was 609 estimated to be lower than recommended intake in some countries without optimisation (Appendix 1D). 83 610 However, calcium requirements are likely overestimated because they are based on short-term studies. 611 Notably, Indonesia has the lowest calcium intake (approximately 250 mg/day), and the lowest fracture 83 612 rate. Global average vitamin B12 intakes, already currently slightly below adequate intake, were slightly 613 reduced in the PHD when not optimised. Vitamin B12 intakes are likely to be low if intakes of all animal- 614 source foods are on the lower end of the PHD ranges (e.g., in vegan and vegetarian diets) and no 615 supplementary foods are consumed; this merits attention because deficiencies can result in permanent 129 616 neurologic damage. Dietary modifications with traditionally available food sources (e.g., fermented 617 soybeans and algae in East Asia, fermented sesame in Western Africa, bivalves in coastal areas, and 128 618 nutritional yeast more widely) can ensure vitamin B12 adequacy. In populations with biochemical 619 evidence of inadequate intake, fortification or micronutrient supplementation should be considered. 620 621 Absorbed iron intake in the PHD reference diet is similar to the current global average intake. Absorption 622 efficiency was estimated by accounting for regulatory components such as phytates, vitamin C, and haem 623 and non-haem iron in diets (Appendix 1D). The iron needs of women of reproductive age merit specific 624 attention (see section below). Specific attention should be placed to ensure adequate intake of iron-rich 625 food sources, including legumes, soy products, dark green vegetables, and whole grains. Increases in red 626 meat consumption (as an iron source) above the recommended ranges of the PHD are associated with 627 other adverse health outcomes as outlined above and in Appendix 1A. Estimated iodine intake currently 628 and from the PHD appear marginal, but intake depends strongly on soil composition where foods are 629 produced (proximity to marine environments) and seafood consumption including algae.

631 Absorption of nutrients like iron, zinc, and vitamin A can be compounded by parasitic and other infectious 632 diseases common in low and middle-income countries (LIC, MIC). Because of complexities in estimated 633 nutrient intake, absorption, and losses, monitoring the prevalence of nutrient deficiency is critical. 634 Improvements in diet quality, together with appropriate fortification (e.g., of salt for iodine deficiency) or 635 micronutrient supplementation strategies and non-nutritional interventions (e.g., water, sanitation, and 636 infectious disease control), are possible approaches to address nutrient deficiency. 637 638 The PHD provides approximately 14% of energy from protein, which exceeds requirements (EAR 0.7 g/kg 130 639 body weight, approximately 10% of energy intake), and will provide sufficient essential amino acids. 640 The relation between protein intake and health outcomes will depend on the protein sources. In a meta- 641 analysis, protein intake was inversely associated with lower overall mortality, but this apparent benefit 131 642 was enï؟½rely due to plant rather than animal sources of protein. Somewhat higher protein intakes (e.g., 132 643 15 to 30% of energy) have been suggested as important for older adults. This was supported in an 644 analysis on healthy ageing, where the benefit was almost entirely due to plant rather than animal sources 133 645 of protein. The PHD provides approximately 53% of calories from carbohydrates and 35% from total fat, 646 which is compatible with the flexible amounts of these macronutrients. 647 648 Women of Reproductive Age 649 More than one billion adolescent girls and women suffer from undernutrition, anaemia, and other 134 650 micronutrient deficiencies which can result in devastating health impacts. The overall health of women 651 of reproductive age is supported by the PHD as presented in Table 1 and Appendix 1E. The iron needs of 652 women of reproductive age merit specific attention due to menstrual loss, pregnancy, and lactation. 653 654 A healthy diet during pregnancy is critical to prevent adverse maternal outcomes, support healthy foetal 655 development, and avoid foetal programming that can transmit metabolic dysfunction to future 135 656 generations. Overall food intake should be adjusted for optimal weight gain, and include a variety of 136,137 657 healthy foods, consistent with the PHD (Table 1). Although balanced vegetarian diets can support 138 658 healthy foetal development, use of micronutrient supplements or fortified foods may be indicated if 659 animal-sourced foods are below the reference intakes. Because of extra nutritional demands of 660 pregnancy, the WHO recommends136 routine iron and folic acid supplementation to prevent maternal 661 anaemia, puerperal sepsis, spina bifida, and anencephaly. Other context-specific recommendations 662 include specific micronutrient supplementation (calcium, vitamin A, and zinc) or routine use of multiple 663 micronutrient supplementaï؟½on during pregnancy, which is now used rouï؟½nely in many countries because 139 664 of benefits beyond those provided by iron and folate alone. 665 666 Dietary needs of Infants and Young Children 667 Young children under the age of two years have unique requirements for rapid growth and development. 668 Exclusive breast feeding, including timely access to colostrum, in the first six months of infancy is strongly 669 advised to ensure optimal nutrition, reducing risk of infectious disease, prevention of both undernutrition 140 and obesity, and for other benefits for infants and their mothers. From around six months of age, introduction of safe complementary foods based on diverse, locally available, and nutrient-dense food is

141 672 essential for optimal growth and development. Continued breast feeding is important and deserves 673 promotion, protection, and support. 674 675 Dietary habits and taste preferences are developed in early childhood, and it is important that children 676 become familiar with healthy foods that lays the foundation for a healthy life. Fish, meat, eggs, and dairy 677 foods are rich sources of some key nutrients for children of this age, including iron, vitamin B12, zinc, and 142,143 678 calcium. The amounts of these animal-source foods in Table 1 would be suitable for most young 140 679 children 6-24 months of age. Lower amounts of animal-source foods can result in nutritional 680 deficiencies if not adequately replaced, including Vitamin B12 deficiency, which is associated with poor 142 681 neurodevelopment in young children. For children with animal-source food intakes below the PHD 682 reference values, complementing their diet with the use of nutrient supplements or fortified foods can 140,143,144 683 make nutrient intakes adequate. Minimally processed foods should be prioritised, while ultra- 684 processed foods and beverages should be minimised as most are nutritionally unbalanced and may 685 contain harmful additives. 686 687 Adopting the PHD pattern globally 688 The components of healthy diets, described broadly in Table 1 as reference values with ranges and 689 potential exchanges among food groups, allow great flexibility because they are compatible with a wide 128 690 variety of foods, agricultural systems, cultural traditions, and individual dietary preferences. Possible 691 ranges for individual components are described, but with overall intakes of animal-sourced foods being 692 approximately two servings per day. The suggested daily or weekly amounts can be consumed at once or 693 spread out in small amounts; for example, red meat might be consumed as an 80 to 100 g serving once a 694 week or as small amounts in mixed dishes or soups, as is customary in many food traditions. 695 696 The balance of healthy animal- and plant-source foods presented in the PHD is consistent with many 145,146 113,147 697 dietary patterns, including Indigenous diets, the well-studied Mediterranean diet, and other 698 traditional diets from around the world. The traditional diets have in common with the PHD a base of 699 whole grains or tubers, a wide variety of greens and other vegetables, legumes, and small to modest 145,148,149 700 amounts of fish or meat with herbs and sauces that add flavour, variety, and nutritional value. 701 Moreover, many traditional food practices support improved bioavailability of nutrients and build on rich 702 knowledge of the seasonality of cultivated and wild food species, supporting year-round food and 145,146 703 nutrition security. Although more research on traditional diets in different regions is desirable, many 704 of these patterns are consistent with high dietary quality150 and low rates of chronic disease. Protecting 705 healthy traditional diets and food systems can be a critical way to avoid transitions to unhealthy 706 consumption. Examples of healthy meals based on tradiï؟½onal foods and flavours and consistent with the 707 PHD are described in Appendix 1H. 708 709 In settings with humanitarian crises, deep food insecurity, or in populations where diets remain 710 dominated by staple carbohydrates (e.g. largely rice or cassava), food supplements, including fortified 151,152 711 foods, may be required while improving diet quality. The time-cost and labour, mainly of women, for food preparation needs to be considered, and if cooking fuel is limited, this can prevent the preparation

713 714 715 716 717 718 719 720 721 722 723 724 725 726 727 728 729 730 731 732 733 734 735 736 737 738 739 740 741 742 743 744 745 746 747 748 749 750 751 752

of nutrient-dense foods, such as legumes. Availability of refrigeration and cooking facilities may also constrain food preparation. In some situations, processed, ready-to-eat foods may be necessary, and care is needed to ensure these are nutritionally balanced. Adequate amounts of safe water for drinking and sanitation are always essential. Importantly, achieving PHD-aligned diets represents an important goal for populations shouldering a high burden of undernutrition.

Figure 2 describes the gaps between the PHD reference values and current diets that vary greatly among

regions. Notably, red meat and tuber/starchy root intakes are excessive in most regions except South Asia. Tuber/starchy root intake in Sub-Saharan African substantially exceeds the reference value and is higher compared to other regions. Free or added sugars intake is higher than the reference value in all regions except Sub-Saharan Africa and East Asia-Pacific. In most regions, intakes of many healthy foods are low, including vegetables, fruits, legumes, nuts and seeds, and whole grains (replacing refined grains); increasing consumption of these foods offer important opportunities to improve health.

# Summary and Conclusions of Healthy Diets

We draw on key evidence from long-term observational studies relating individual dietary components and overall dietary patterns to major chronic disease outcomes, randomised trials with intermediate-risk factors as outcomes, together with randomised trials of health endpoints.

New evidence, including dose-response relaï؟½onships, has added substanï؟½al support for the health benefits of the PHD described in the 2019 EAT-Lancet Commission (Appendix 1A). 1 This further supports emphasising whole grains over refined grains and potatoes, 48 plant sources of protein (legumes, pulses, and nuts) over red meat (unprocessed or processed), 51,76 unsaturated plant oils over saturated fats, 85,153,154

including generous amounts of fruits and vegetables, 52 moderate amounts of dairy, fish, eggs, and poultry, and limiï؟½ng free or added sugars and salt. 109,155,156

The elements of a healthy diet and the recommended intake ranges for main food groups outlined in

Table 1 allow great flexibility because they are compatible with a wide variety of foods, agricultural

systems, cultural traditions, and individual dietary preferences. They can be combined in various types of omnivorous or flexitarian diets as well as vegetarian and vegan diets. The findings of health benefits in many populations for this and similar dietary patterns show that healthy dietary patterns can be achieved in contemporary populations and in many countries globally.

In making global estimates of impacts of diets on health, another general conclusion is that available data on the composition of foods, national dietary intakes, and the relation of dietary factors to health are far from optimal. Notably, large cohort studies with long follow-up are lacking from Africa, Latin America, and most parts of Asia. Because aspects of diet strongly influence health, enhancements in data availability and quality are needed to provide better precision in future analyses and more specific regional and local dietary guidance.
