764 Panel 2: The Planetary Health Diet and Estimated Reduction in Avoidable Deaths among Adults The potenï؟½al reducï؟½on in avoidable deaths among adults by adopï؟½ng the planetary health diet (PHD) has been esï؟½mated in two ways for this Commission. One approach used an updated comparaï؟½ve risk assessment (CRA) analysis based on relaï؟½ve risks for specific dietary factors and incidence of specific diseases (e.g., red meat intake and type 2 diabetes) using published meta-analyses of cohort studies, most having a single assessment of diet (Appendix 1G). These associaï؟½ons were then applied to country-specific data on diets and cause-specific mortality. In this approach, adopï؟½on of the PHD was esï؟½mated to prevent about 10 million avoidable deaths per year among adults globally, represenï؟½ng 17% of total mortality. P19 About half of the reducï؟½on in avoidable deaths was due to composiï؟½on- related risks, including increased whole grains, fruits and vegetables, legumes and nuts, and less red and processed meat consumpï؟½on. The remaining half was due to anï؟½cipated reducï؟½ons in underweight, overweight, and obesity.

To esï؟½mate more comprehensively the potenï؟½al populaï؟½on level impact of adopï؟½ng the overall PHD paï؟½ern on mortality, the PHD reference values P20 were used to create the PHD Index (PHDI), a score for the consistency of individual or naï؟½onal diets with the PHD paï؟½ern. P21,P22 The associaï؟½on between individual PHD scores and mortality was esï؟½mated from the follow-up of over 200,000 men and women over more than 30 years; diet was assessed every four years to account for changes in food consumpï؟½on over ï؟½me and to reduce measurement errors (Appendix 1A). P21

Combining the relaï؟½ve risks for PHD scores and mortality with the same data on country-specific diets and mortality used for the CRA analysis(described above), an esï؟½mated 15 million avoidable deaths per year among adults (27 % of total deaths) could be avoided globally by achieving a PHD score of 120 (140 being perfect consistency). P22 This esï؟½mate did not include assumpï؟½ons about reducï؟½ons in overweight or obesity; including such benefits would result in greater reducï؟½ons in mortality. Notably, when only the baseline dietary assessment was used in the follow-up study, about two-thirds of the mortality reducï؟½on was missed. Because achieving a global PHD score of 120 is ambiï؟½ous, we also esï؟½mated the potenï؟½al benefit from achieving a global PHD score of 100 (only 15 points above current diets); the esï؟½mated reducï؟½on of avoidable deaths was seven million annually (13% of total deaths).

In both the CRA and the PHD paï؟½ern analyses, effects of sodium intake were not included due to difficulï؟½es in measurement, and both analyses did not include indirect effects of diet mediated by the environmental impacts of food producï؟½on. Both analyses used data primarily from high-income countries for esï؟½maï؟½ng relaï؟½ve risks because studies from other world regions are currently lacking, represenï؟½ng an important research gap that should be addressed. However, much evidence supports that relaï؟½ve risks for diet and health outcomes are quite generalizable across populaï؟½ons. Addiï؟½onal benefits due to substanï؟½al improvements in diet quality are also likely for children, but these are difficult to esï؟½mate quanï؟½taï؟½vely because of major interacï؟½ons between diet and infecï؟½ous diseases, which are parï؟½cularly important in low- and middle-income countries.

In summary, different methods for esï؟½maï؟½ng the impacts of adherence to the PHD yield different esï؟½mates of the populaï؟½on level impact on mortality. As expected, the use of an overall dietary paï؟½ern that incorporates the addiï؟½ve and interacï؟½ve effects of most major dietary components, combined with repeated assessments of diet over the follow-up period to calculate relaï؟½ve risks, provides a higher esï؟½mate of avoidable deaths. For thoroughness and transparency, we have provided results from two approaches; with conï؟½nued improvements in data sources, further refinements can be expected in the future. 765 766 Panel 3: Indirect Effects of Diet on Human Health In addiï؟½on to the direct effects of diet on human health, food systems and diets also have many indirect effects on health mediated by their environmental impacts (see secï؟½on on Sustainable Food Systems within Planetary Boundaries) or societal factors (see secï؟½on on Jusï؟½ce in Food Systems). Here we provide some examples mediated by environmental impacts which we recommend merit greater aï؟½enï؟½on by the scienï؟½fic community.

Climate Change has many extensively documented effects on human health, including heat stress directly affecï؟½ng human health, extreme weather events such as windstorms, flooding, and prolonged droughts affecï؟½ng food producï؟½on, water security, and aï؟½endant deterioraï؟½on in sanitaï؟½on puï؟½ng people at risk of malnutriï؟½on, and increases in geographic areas and number of people affected by infecï؟½ous diseases such as dengue, malaria, and West Nile fever. P23 Moreover, climate change affects crop yields, P24 nutriï؟½onal content, P25 and food-based pathogens, with major indirect effects on human health through effects on the availability and affordability of nutriï؟½ous food.

Air Polluï؟½on resulï؟½ng from food producï؟½on includes small parï؟½culate maï؟½er and ground level ozone, which increase rates of cardiovascular, pulmonary, and neurogeneraï؟½ve disease, and premature deaths. P26 Globally agriculture accounts for 650,000 deaths or 20% of mortality related to poor air quality. P27 This is largely from nitrogen polluï؟½on from ferï؟½lizers leading to emissions of nitrogen oxides, and nitrogen dioxide polluï؟½on associated with around 4 million new cases of paediatric asthma a year. P28 Biomass burning, notably in the global south, accounts for a smaller proporï؟½on, including from land clearing and cooking fuel (164,000 deaths, 5% of mortality related to poor air quality). An esï؟½mated 3 to 6% of global exposure to small parï؟½culate air polluï؟½on could be avoided by adopï؟½on of more plant-rich diets, resulï؟½ng in approximately 100,000 to 300,000 avoidable deaths annually. P29

Nitrogen Polluï؟½on of drinking water due to excess ferï؟½liser use has been associated with methemoglobinemia, colorectal cancer, thyroid disease, and birth defects. P30 Other adverse effects of nitrogen polluï؟½on in waters may be mediated by eutrophicaï؟½on of water bodies and overgrowth by toxic algal blooms. P31 The ground water threshold is exceeded in 38% of agricultural lands globally P32

parï؟½cularly impacï؟½ng the health of vulnerable rural populaï؟½ons with strong groundwater dependencies.

Biocides (including pesï؟½cides, herbicides, and fungicides) are used extensively in agriculture. P33

Although biocides are regulated in most countries to prevent risks to human health, their misuse (i.e. overapplicaï؟½on and lack of appropriate protecï؟½ve measures, parï؟½cularly for agricultural workers) and co-exposure resulï؟½ng from mixtures in the environment, has mulï؟½ple adverse effects on health. P34

Chronic exposure to pesï؟½cides, including by consumpï؟½on of contaminated foods, is associated with risks of Parkinsonâ€™s disease, diabetes, cancer, cardiovascular disease, and possibly with inferï؟½lity. P35

Anï؟½microbial Resistance and Pandemic Risk have increased in part due to increased animalâ€“human contact (either through direct interacï؟½on with wildlife or indirectly through vectors or intermediate, domesï؟½cated species) which is a prerequisite for zoonoï؟½c spillover. P36 The large-scale global increases in consumpï؟½on of animal-source foods coming from a very small number of species, o en produced in highly concentrated systems, increases risk. P37,P38 Caï؟½le and hogs now make up ~43% of the mammalian biomass on the planet (humans make up ~38%), P39 and domesï؟½c poultry makes up ~70% of the avian biomass. P40 This large biomass poses a risk for the emergence of novel pathogens affecï؟½ng humans and animals, with an acceleraï؟½on in the frequency of such epidemic/pandemic disease events. P41 The scaleup in animal-source food producï؟½on has been enabled by using anï؟½microbials as growth promoters, and anï؟½microbials used for animal producï؟½on represent about two-thirds of the global producï؟½on of anï؟½microbials. P42 The risk of anï؟½microbial resistance emerging from the use of anï؟½microbials in animals is well-documented, P43,P44 and an esï؟½mated 1.4 million people die each year from untreatable microbial infecï؟½ons. P45 We esï؟½mated that adopï؟½on of the PHD would decrease anï؟½microbial use by ~42% due to reducï؟½ons in animal sourced foods. P46

769 Section 2: Sustainable Food Systems within Planetary Boundaries 770 771 Planetary boundaries is an Earth systemâ€™s framework157 that quantifies limits for biophysical processes 772 that regulate the stability and resilience of life-support systems on Earth. Transgression of these 773 boundaries pushes the Earth system into an unsafe environmental space for humanity. The latest update 774 of the planetary boundary framework158 concludes that six of nine planetary boundaries have already 775 been transgressed. The 2019 EAT-Lancet Commission assessed five of these boundaries â€” land, 776 biodiversity, climate, blue water flows, and biogeochemical flows â€” and found that food systems carry 777 the greatest responsibility for boundary transgressions. Here we re-evaluate the evidence on food 778 systems impact on all nine planetary boundaries, including four boundaries not included in the 2019 779 Commission: aerosol loading, ozone depletion, ocean acidification, and novel entities (Table 2). 780 781 We quantify specific food system boundaries that represent the share of the Planetary boundaries 782 allocated to food systems (Figure 3). In contrast to planetary boundaries, food system boundaries are not 783 based on increasing risk levels in the Earth system but represent science-based environmental targets for 784 the global food system, aligned with the planetary boundary framework. As there is no consistent 785 framework to define such targets, we define food system boundaries based on three principles from the 786 exisï؟½ng literature: (i) through proporï؟½onal reducï؟½ons necessary to move back to the safe operaï؟½ng space 787 (based on present-day food system contribuï؟½ons to transgressions); (ii) through top-down sectoral 788 opï؟½mizaï؟½on of miï؟½gaï؟½on costs using integrated assessment models; and (iii) by considering food system 789 boundaries equal to planetary boundaries (based on large uncertainï؟½es or the on dominant contribuï؟½on 790 of food systems to planetary boundary transgressions). In some cases, food system boundaries are based 791 on evidence that considers limiï؟½ng Earth system impacts while retaining the producï؟½vity of agricultural 159 792 systems (see Text A2.1 and Table A2.1). 793 794 Meeï؟½ng rising food demands must be achieved from less land, with food producï؟½on that generates rather 160,161 795 than degrades ecological funcï؟½ons. Sustainable Intensificaï؟½on (SI) entails achieving important 796 reducï؟½ons in the environmental impacts through increased efficiency, reduced losses, and reduced 162 797 polluï؟½on. Ecological Intensificaï؟½on (EI) is a subset of SI that enhances the environmental performance 798 of food producï؟½on by promoï؟½ng ecological processes within agricultural fields, farms and landscapes, 799 such as above- and belowground carbon sequestraï؟½on, nutrient cycling and storage, pollinaï؟½on, and 163,164 800 biological pest regulaï؟½on. We synthesise evidence on the impacts of key groups of pracï؟½ces for 801 sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on (SEI) across field-scale indicators, to assess the potenï؟½al of SEI 802 in miï؟½gaï؟½ng planetary boundary transgressions (Secï؟½on Reducing the Environmental Foodprint of Food 803 Producï؟½on). We use a novel ecological intensificaï؟½on module from the FABLE model165 to assess the 804 global potenï؟½al of these pracï؟½ces across planetary boundaries (Panel 6).

820 Climate change 821 Our food systems release almost a third of the emissions driving climate change, and are also especially 170 822 vulnerable to global warming, with profound effects on peopleâ€™s food supply and health. Agriculture â€“1 170â€“ 823 and food release 16â€“17.7 GtCO2e yr, or about 30% of total global greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. 172 824 Around a third of these emissions come from agriculture, including both livestock and crop production, 825 a third from conversion of natural ecosystems to cropping and grazing lands, and a third from non- 826 agricultural aspects of food supply â€” including transport, cold storage, processing, retail, catering, food 827 management in the home, the manufacture of fertilisers and other agricultural inputs, and waste 170,172 828 management (Table A2.2). Most agricultural emissions come from producï؟½on of animal-source food 829 (Figure 10a). Enteric fermentaï؟½on from ruminant animals (i.e. beef and lamb, and dairy) and manure 830 management linked to red meat and poultry account for large shares (Figure 1). Ruminant farming is the 164 831 primary driver of land use change. Up to a third of food system-wide emissions derive from off-farm food-related processes, including ferï؟½lizer producï؟½on and distribuï؟½on, post-farm food processing and -1 transformaï؟½on and transportaï؟½on. For example, more than 10% (1.9 GtCO2e yr) of the global food

173 834 system emissions are aï؟½ributed to food that is lost or wasted along the supply chain. Reaffirming the 835 boundary set in the 2019 Commission, we propose that GHG emissions from food systems are kept under â€“1 836 5 GtCO2e yr by 2050 to keep global warming below 1.5 or 2آ°C (Figure 1). Several recent analyses support â€“1 837 a 5 GtCO2e yr boundary, highlighï؟½ng that a more ambiï؟½ous boundary is possible when miï؟½gaï؟½on opï؟½ons 838 include dietary change (Table A2.3). The residual emissions in this food system boundary comprise only 839 methane (CH4) and nitrous oxide (N2O) emissions from biological processes in agriculture. These emissions 840 are difficult to abate even with global adoption of the PHD, though the boundary could in theory be further 841 reduced with wider adoption of increasingly plant-rich diets or major advances in emission-reducing 842 technologies. 843 844 To meet the climate boundary involves tremendous ambition. Full energy decarbonisation and zero land 845 use change would together eliminate two-thirds of food system emissions, by largely eradicating CO2 846 emissions. Energy use would need to be decarbonised throughout the food system, including in food 847 transport, refrigeration and preparation, on-farm machinery, and manufacture of agricultural inputs. Net- 848 zero land use change, aligned with the Land System Change and Biosphere Integrity boundaries, needs a 849 combination of secure land governance, increases in productivity, and reductions in demand. The 850 remaining third â€“ the residual CH4 and N2O emissions from agriculture â€“ can be reduced through dietary 851 change, management of food waste, and farming pracï؟½ces (e.g. improved livestock feed and ferï؟½liser use 852 efficiency). A further opportunity to keep food systems within the boundary is carbon sequestraï؟½on in 853 agriculture, which, when coupled with emissions reducï؟½ons, could make food systems a net carbon sink 854 (e.g. Panel 5 & 6, Secï؟½on Improving Environmental Performance of Food Producï؟½on) by removing by â€“1 170,174,175 855 removing 1.4â€“5.5 GtCO2e (esï؟½mated economic potenï؟½al at a carbon price of US$100 per tonne). 856 Sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on pracï؟½ces that sequester carbon include soil carbon -1-1 857 management and agroforestry with an esï؟½mated 0.4â€“0.9 GtCO2e yr and 0.4â€“1.1 GtCO2e yr respecï؟½vely 858 with a soil carbon prices of US$100 per tonne. 859 860 A systems approach is essential to navigate trade-offs and rebound effects. Since options to reduce 861 emissions diminish as our remaining carbon budget falls below 200 GtCO2, direct capture of emissions is 862 now a universal component of scientific pathways to achieve 1.5 or 2آ°C (Figure 1). The preferred 863 technology is Bioenergy, Carbon Capture and Storage (BECCS), a land-intensive solution that would 176 864 compete with afforestaï؟½on and offset reduced demand for land to feed livestock. 865 866 Land system change 867 Human activities have substantially altered the Earth surface through conversion of intact lands to 868 croplands, grazing lands, and other industrial and urban land uses. Approximately 50% of ice-free lands 31 869 can still be considered largely 'intact' (natural ecosystems with low human appropriation or use). Halting 870 further conversion of these lands, particularly protecting large forest biomes, is necessary to halt 871 biodiversity loss, reduce human climate forcing, and secure hydrological functioning of ecosystems. 31,158,177 872 We set the land boundary at 85% intactness of ecoregions included in boreal and tropical forest 873 biomes and at 50â€“60% for all other ecoregions as defined in the Planetary Boundaries and Earth

874 875 876 877 878 879 880 881 882 883 884 885 886 887 888 889 890 891 892 893 894 895 896 897 898 899 900 901 902 903 904 905 906 907 908 909 910 911 912 913

Commission. 34 These levels are required to miï؟½gate the impacts of climate change, and to halt the loss of biodiversity.

Currently, 48 Mkm2 (37% of the global land area) are used for agricultural production, of which croplands and grazing lands cover 33% and 67% respectively. 178 Almost 70% of ecoregions fall below the 50% intactness boundary, 31 predominantly driven by historical conversion of intact nature to agriculture (Figure A2.1). Agricultural land alone breaches the local intactness boundary (>50% agricultural land cover) in a third of the ecoregions (Figure A2.2). The conï؟½nuing loss of intact ecosystems, notably forests, is predominantly driven by agriculture, 179 and is particularly high in tropical forests that are cleared for pasture, oil crops and soy production. 179,180

Remaining within the land system boundary requires zero conversion of intact lands, by keeping global agricultural area well below 48 Mkm2. This is a modification of how the land boundary was articulated in the 2019 EAT-Lancet Commission as 13 M km2 which only included croplands. In addition to halting conversion, reducing agricultural land and restoring intactness is critical across ecoregions, and particularly important in forest biomes that are currently strongly transgressed, and require 5.5 Mkm2 and 3 Mkm2 of tropical and temperate forest restoration, respectively, at the expense of agricultural land (Figure A2.3).

The key strategy to reduce agricultural land area is a shift to healthy diets, particularly away from animal- based products and associated feed production (Section 4). 181,182 Decreasing food loss and waste is a lever with which the demand-side pressure can be further reduced. In aggregate, maintaining or increasing yields is criï؟½cal to keep agriculture within a reduced land area, recognising regional differences depending on current yield levels. Sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on (SEI) pracï؟½ces chiefly demonstrate either improved (desirable effect sizes) or maintained (negligible effect sizes) producï؟½vity (Secï؟½on Reducing Environmental Foodprints of Food Producï؟½on). EI would require slightly more land than other practices aiming to improve production but still fall below the land boundary globally (Panel 4).

# Biosphere integrity

Life on our planet depends on the capacity of the biosphere to safeguard critical Earth system functions, which in turn requires healthy, functioning ecosystems. Two complementary control variables for biosphere integrity include biosphere functional integrity and ecosystem functional integrity. Biosphere functional integrity refers to the sum of Net Primary Producï؟½vity (NPP) from all ecosystems on Earth and thus is used as an Earth system-wide control variable. 183 NPP captures the photosyntheï؟½c energy and material flows into the biosphere, with the deviaï؟½on between potenï؟½al NPP of intact ecosystems and the actual NPP of converted systems. The biosphere integrity boundary sets a limit to human appropriaï؟½on of this energy (HANPP) at <10% of Holocene NPP (5.5 GtC yr -1 ). 158 Funcï؟½onal integrity of ecosystems is defined as ecosystemsâ€™ capacity to sustain natureâ€™s contribuï؟½ons to people (e.g. food producï؟½on). 184

Intact ecosystems are funcï؟½onally intact, i.e. with high funcï؟½onal integrity. This boundary, which is set at 20â€“25% natural or semi-natural habitat per km2, is thus applied to agricultural and urban land-use systems. From an Earth system perspecï؟½ve, funcï؟½onal integrity contributes to ensuring gene and species

915 flow within and between ecosystems, reducing the risk of species exï؟½ncï؟½ons when complemented with 916 the land boundary described above. The 2019 Commission used a 10% threshold with evidence that below 917 this value ecosystem services to and from food producï؟½on are lost. Recent reviews have increased this 918 threshold to 20â€“25% necessary to maintain minimal funcï؟½oning, rather than complete loss of 184,185 919 funcï؟½on. 920 921 Agricultural systems, notably extensive monocultures, are the single largest drivers of both biosphere and 922 ecosystem functional integrity loss, through lowering actual NPP from land conversion, extracting NPP by 923 harvesting biomass, and below threshold retention of habitat within agricultural lands. Agriculture is -1 186 924 responsible for appropriating 72â€“85% of the total HANPP (9.9â€“11.7 GtC yr), while a significant portion 925 of agricultural lands (33â€“66%) are below functional integrity thresholds, and 88% of agricultural land are 184,187 926 used for food production (Figure 4a). 927 928 Remaining within the food system boundary for biosphere integrity requires a reduction of HANPP back -1 159 929 within the safe planetary boundary level (<5.5 GtC yr), by increasing productivity on existing cropland 930 (i.e. closing agricultural yield gaps from e.g. nutrient deficits, see Section Nitrogen), and restoring 931 ecosystems on former agricultural lands (e.g. reforestation), to increase actual NPP back to levels of 932 potential NPP (Section Land System Change). Ecological Intensification practices, while not directly 933 assessed against HANPP here, include practices that ensure that larger portions of NPP are retained in or 934 returned to fields, for example as crop residue, manure, or perennial vegetation. Maximum field scale 935 HANPP of sustainable production systems merits attention as a key scalable sustainability metric. -1 186 936 Furthermore, reducing HANPP from harvested crops (currently آ±2 GtC yr) is only possibly through 937 dietary shifts that reduce HANPP from crop production for animal feed, and by reducing food loss and 938 waste and feeding losses. Globally, around 775 kcal per capita per day is lost or wasted (represenï؟½ng 939 approximately 20% of the agricultural land use), of which the majority are crops. These losses occur along 173,188 940 the full supply chain (post-harvest, retail, food service, households), see Panel 4 (Section 941 Transformation). 942 943 Increasing the functional integrity on productive lands requires widespread inclusion of embedded habitat 184,185 944 in agricultural lands to reach the planetary boundary safe zone. This target can be achieved by 945 protecting and restoring riparian buffers and hedgerows, improved fallows, returning crop waste to fields, 946 and other practices fitting agroecosystem context. Agricultural diversification practices support the 947 ecosystem functional integrity directly and can be managed to further enhance carbon sequestration 948 (Climate Boundary), increase biological pest control and pollination (Novel Entities Boundary), and 949 reduce losses of nutrients to the environment (Biogeochemical Flows Boundary). 950 951 Freshwater change 952 Freshwater availability is critical to support drinking water needs, ecosystem functioning, and climate 189 953 mitigation by enabling carbon sequestration in the biosphere. The freshwater cycle is increasingly 954 altered by human actions, affecting both blue water (rivers, reservoirs and groundwater) and green water 190 955 (held in soil and in plants). Recent planetary boundary assessments show that we have transgressed 158,191 boundaries for both blue and green water. These boundaries propose limits to the occurrence of

excepï؟½onally dry and wet local events (including blue and green water availability), and deviate from the blue water boundary proposed in the 2019 Commission, which is based on volumetric limits to -1 192 consumptive water use. It constrained the global agricultural consumptive use to <2,500 km3 yr to preserve regional ecosystem flow requirements (hereafter â€کEFRâ€™, meaning the minimum water volume, timing and quality needed to maintain good ecological status of aquatic systems). Here, we adopt the novel boundary definiï؟½on for green water (i.e. global land area beyond pre-industrial local green water variability baseline), while keeping â€“ although updaï؟½ng â€“ the volumetric limit for blue water.

957 958 959 960 961 962 963 964 965 966 967 968 969 970 971 972 973 974 975 976 977 978 979 980 981 982 983 984 985 986 987 988

Food systems are major blue water users, mainly via irrigation. 159,193 Water withdrawal estimates from rivers and groundwater range between 2,700â€“3,100 km 3 yr -1 , 171,193 of which between 1,200â€“1,800 km 3 yr - 1 are consumptive use. 193,194 Irrigation is responsible for around 70â€“90% of the global consumptive water use, although estimates vary depending on the accounting methods and models used. 159,194 The majority of agricultural land and producï؟½on159 is rainfed and hence depends solely on green water193, but soil moisture availability is changing across the world, 191 including on agricultural lands (16.8% of agricultural land is currently transgressed). 196,197 We preliminarily define the food system boundary for green water based on the latest planetary boundary definition (i.e. limiï؟½ng the land area beyond pre-industrial variability baseline to 11%) 159 ). For blue water, we propose that agricultural water consumpï؟½on remains below 2,000 km 3 yr -1, which is lower than in the 2019 Commission (2,500 km3 yr -1 ), that allocated 90% of the safe operaï؟½ng space to agriculture. We propose a more stringent approach considering uncertainï؟½es in agricultural consumpï؟½ve water use 193 , and acknowledge the lower (stricter) end of the planetary boundary uncertainty range (1,100 km 3 yr -1 ). 192 While this suggests that food systems are within the safe operating space globally (Table 2), many rivers are experiencing severe water stress from irrigation water withdrawal alone. 198 In fact, >5% of the global food production depends on regional blue water transgressions by tapping into EFRs (Figure 4b). 198 In those areas, limiting irrigation water demand without affecting crop yield could be achieved through shifting production to less water demanding foods, 199

reducing food loss and waste (currently wasting 20-25% of freshwater withdrawals), 171,173 and increasing water productivity through the adoption of agricultural practices that improve soil water holding capacity (Section Reducing Environmental Footprint of Food Production). SEI practices are significantly effective in increasing soil organic carbon (SOC) content, which in turn improves the water-holding capacity of soils. 200 Crop and non-crop diversification, especially agroforestry (e.g., Muchane et al., 2020 201 ), exhibit strong desirable effects on the water regulation of soils by e.g. creating diverse root-systems and enabling infiltration.

# Biogeochemical flows: nitrogen and phosphorus losses

Nitrogen (N) and phosphorus (P) play a critical role in biological life, including crop and pasture production. In some regions, crop yields are below their potential due to deficient N and P availability, while in others, agricultural N and P surplus from excessive inputs cause environmental pollution, leading to biodiversity loss and human health impacts. 166,204 In addition, N2O emissions from fertiliser and manure contribute to climate change (Section Climate Change) and stratospheric ozone depletion (Section Stratospheric Ozone Depletion). Here, we adopt the â€کN surplusâ€™ from agricultural land as control variable, 167 defined as N input minus the removal of N via crops and grasses. In contrast to â€کN inputâ€™ adopted in the 2019 Commission, N surplus covers remaining reactive nitrogen that can lead to environmental harm via deposition on natural ecosystems, groundwater leaching and surface water load.

Unlike N input, esï؟½mates of N surplus are not affected by nitrogen use efficiencies (NUE, i.e. N removal from plants compared to total N input), but ignore N removal from plants that moves up the food supply chain and can end up in the environment via waste-water streams (i.e. via human excreta). The planetary boundary for N surplus on agricultural land is 57 Tg N yr -1 , allowing for an increase in N use in regions that are currently deficient (Figure 4c). For P, we adopt â€کP losses to surface waterâ€™, which strongly depend on the available P stocks in soils and erosion rates. Unlike the â€کP applicaï؟½onâ€™ control variable used in the 2019 Commission, we use P losses since applied (excess) P can be adsorbed and build up soil P stocks without directly causing harmful environmental effects (unlike N). Based on critical P concentrations to preserve

205,206 good ecological status of global surface waters, we propose a global P loss boundary of 6.1 Tg of P -1 207 yr . 1028 1029 1030 1031 1032 1033 1034 1035 1036 1037 1038 1039 1040 1041 1042 1043 1044 1045 1046 1047 1048 1049 1050 1051 1052 1053 1054 1055 1056 1057 1058 1059 1060 1061 1062 1063 1064 1065 1066 1067

Current agricultural N inputs on cropland and managed grasslands (233 Tg N yr -1 ) are partly removed by crops (114 Tg N yr -1 ), while 119 Tg yr -1 remains as surplus. 167 This surplus â€” together with other N sources from wastewater, aquaculture, NOx deposition from transport, and natural sources (i.e. allochthonous matter)â€” contributes to N deposition on terrestrial ecosystems (23 Tg N yr -1 , around 50% from food systems), N loading in surface water (71 Tg N yr -1 , around 70% from food systems), and leaching to ground water (56 Tg of N yr -1 , around 80% from food systems) (Table A2.4). 159,207 Furthermore, almost 10 Tg of P yr -1 is delivered from soils to surface water, with 75% of the P delivery (7.2 Tg P yr -1 ) derived from food systems (Table A2.4). 166

Bringing agricultural N surplus back within global safe levels (< 57 Tg N yr -1 ) requires a reducï؟½on of agricultural N input from 233 to 134 Tg N yr -1 , 167 (Panel 4) based on current NUE. Assuming proporï؟½onal reducï؟½ons across other sectors can only slightly relax this value (to 140 Tg N yr -1 ) 167 since most N sources are aï؟½ributed to the food systems (including agriculture, aquaculture and N from waste water) (Table A2.4); while further reducing the contribuï؟½on from natural N sources (from allochthonous organic maï؟½er) is not possible. We therefore propose the food system boundary for N surplus to be equal to the planetary boundary.

Increasing NUE from 0.48 (current global average) to an achievable 0.67 208 may bring us close to the safe operating space without compromising yield. 167 Nutrient surpluses are highly unevenly distributed, with redistribuï؟½on being as important as a reducï؟½on in ferï؟½lizer rates to limit planetary boundary effects (Figure 4c).

Agricultural diversification and fertiliser management show significant potential to reduce nutrient losses to the environment, improve NUE, with some increased and chiefly neutral yield effects (Section Reducing the Environmental Foodprints of Food Production). While reduced disturbance showed mixed results for reducing nutrient losses in general, no-tillage significantly reduces phosphorus losses. Soil P stocks below target levels should be increased globally, and P applicaï؟½on should maintain this soil P status by replacing P removed during culï؟½vaï؟½on and harvest and reducing physical loss via erosion control.

Returning within boundary conditions remains a challenge without greater attention to food system losses originating from food consumption. Recovery of N and P sources from sewage water must similarly be implemented to prevent post-consumption losses (i.e. around 26 Tg N yr -1 from human excreta of which 12 Tg N yr -1 is lost to surface waters). 166 The impact of circular systems remains poorly assessed (but see Panel 4) and merits attention. Recycling of N and P on fields, not only by using manure but also compost and sludge, decreases losses to the biosphere, although these practices are increasingly challenged by novel entity contaminants (e.g. Forever Chemicals or PFAS) in organic waste streams which can make agricultural fields toxic. 209

Panel 4: Circular food systems Transiï؟½oning to more circular systems is thought to potenï؟½ally reduce (mineral) N and P use. To assess these potenï؟½al contribuï؟½ons, we used CiFoS, P47 a biophysical opï؟½mizaï؟½on model to simulate four 2050 scenarios that were benchmarked to a 2020 simulaï؟½on (see Van Zanten et al., 2025 P48 and Appendix 2 for more details):

1. BAU-2050-Opt: scenario opï؟½mizes a future 2050 scenario by minimizing mulï؟½ple environmental impacts (use of land, N and P and GHG emissions) towards the food system share of planetary boundaries, by reallocaï؟½ng producï؟½on globally. The economic and social implicaï؟½ons of this reallocaï؟½on were not assessed.
2. BAU-2050-Opt+Cir: in addiï؟½on to the BAU-2050-Opt, allows a transiï؟½on to a more circular food system where le overs (e.g., by-products, human excreta, feeding losses and food loss and waste) are recycled.
3. EL-Opt: simulates a scenario which takes into account dietary shi s compaï؟½ble with the Planetary Health Diet, allows food loss and waste (FLW) reducï؟½ons of 50% and producï؟½on increases (in line with the mulï؟½-model ensemble described in Secï؟½on 4).
4. EL-Opt+Cir: scenario adds circularity components to EL-Opt.
In the BAU-Opt scenario the producï؟½on of le overs was lower (4.1 Gt) compared to BAU-Opt+Cir (6.8 Gt) and the difference between EL-Opt (1.7Gt) and EL-Opt+Cir (3.1Gt). Uï؟½lized le overs increased from 24% in BAU-Opt to 84% for BAU-Opt+Cir and from 22% in EL-Opt to 90% for EL-Opt+Cir (Panel Figure

A). Results in the circular scenarios (BAU-Opt+Cir and EL-Opt+Cir) show a preference for recycling over reducing the producï؟½on of le overs. In 2020, the total use of N is 259 TgN and total use of P is 40 TgP, including both mineral ferï؟½lizers and organic sources (Panel Figure B). Compared to 2020, in an opï؟½mized but not circular food system (BAU-Opt) the use of N decreased by 21% to 205 TgN, and the use of P decreased by 49% to 21 TgP. Adding circularity measures (BAU-Opt+Cir) reduced N by 29% to 184 TgN and P by 55% to 18 TgP and EL-Opt reduced N by 45% to 142 TgN and P by 67% to 13TgP. Combining circularity with an EAT-Lancet-aligned food system (EL-Opt+Cir) reduced N by 50% to 128TgN and P by 73% to 11TgP. N and P use in both EL scenarios are in line with staying within their respecï؟½ve planetary boundary (boundary value of 134 TgN total N input to stay within the N surplus boundary of 57 TgN per year (Table 2) and between 8 - 16 Tg of mineral P ferï؟½lizer per year based on Springmann et al., 2018
P49 to limit the amount of P reaching surface waters). Although the results of EL-Opt and EL-Opt+Cir only slightly differ, there are clear differences in the ferï؟½lizer sources used, e.g. more organic ferï؟½lizer sources are used in EL-Opt+Cir (Panel Figure B). Nevertheless, despite substanï؟½al reducï؟½ons in mineral ferï؟½lizer use due to increases in circular sources of N and P (El- Opt+Cir), mineral ferï؟½lizers remain a criï؟½cal input into agricultural producï؟½on.

Panel Figure AB. Effect of circularity on le overs use and biogeochemical cycles on a global food system level. (A) Total le overs and total le overs used per scenario. (B) Nitrogen use and phosphorus use per scenario by source. 1069 1070 1071 Novel entities 1072 The planetary boundary for novel entities proposes limits to the release of human-made substances in 1073 the environment â€” ranging from synthetic chemicals, such as plastics and endocrine disruptors, to 1074 radioactive materials and genetically modified organisms. The risk posed to Earth system stability and 1075 resilience from novel entities is uncertain, but potentially large and persistent following cumulative and 1076 mixing effects (i.e. chemical interaction of multiple substances in the environment), thereby threatening 1077 all life on Earth. There are >350,000 synthetical chemicals produced and released in the environment, and 210 many are insufficiently tested for their potential environmental risks. Uncertainties regarding the associated risks of novel entities continue to frustrate the definition of a novel entities boundary, which

is therefore preliminarily and precautionarily placed at zero release of synthetic chemical compounds without adequate safety testing that can demonstrate that no harmful environmental effects occur upon 158,210 release.

While novel entities were not assessed in the 2019 Commission, food systems contribute to the release 168,203 of various novel entities, such as pesticides, genetically modified crops, antimicrobials, (micro- 211 212 )plastics, and â€کforever chemicalsâ€™, such as PFAS, throughout the food chain. Here, we adopt â€کpesticide applicationâ€™ and â€کantimicrobial useâ€™ as indicators for novel entities, because their widespread use in 159,213,214 agriculture and potential risks to the environment and human health.

1080 1081 1082 1083 1084 1085 1086 1087 1088 1089 1090 1091 1092 1093 1094 1095 1096 1097 1098 1099 1100 1101 1102 1103 1104 1105 1106 1107 1108 1109 1110 1111 1112 1113 1114 1115 1116 1117 1118 1119

Pesticides are widely used in crop protection and weed control and pose major health risks to non-target species. Global pesticide application amounts to 3.3â€“3.7 Tg of pesticide active substances (PAS) yr -1 213 , of which 85â€“90% is used in agriculture. 215 Safety levels for individual pesticide concentrations are crossed throughout terrestrial and aquatic systems (Figure 4d). Although most pesticides degrade over time, remaining residues build up in organisms and pose risks through mixing-effects in the environment. 216

Furthermore, legacy contamination can continue for extended periods of time, decades in some cases, in soil, surface water and groundwater. 168 These factors challenge the definition of safe levels of pesticide use. To minimise exceedance of high risks (i.e., a greater than 90% probability for a random species to be affected by pesticides), we propose a global reduction of 70% compared to current levels equaï؟½ng to a maximum applicaï؟½on of 1 Tg of PAS yr -1 (Figure A2.4). A further reduction of more than 95% (max. application of 0.2 Tg PAS yr -1 ) is required to remain below â€œlowâ€‌ environmental risks (i.e., a less than 5% probability for a random species to be affected by pesticides). 159,203

Ecological intensificaï؟½on pracï؟½ces can manage pest outbreaks without compromising crop yield, while enhancing biosphere ecosystem funcï؟½onal integrity (Figure 5). 217 However, some agricultural pracï؟½ces such as reduced soil disturbance can result in higher weed loads (Figure 5), highlighï؟½ng the importance of employing integrated complementary pracï؟½ces (Panel 5).

Anï؟½microbial use for food-animal producï؟½on is esï؟½mated at 73â€“130 kilotons yr -1 169,218,219 and has increased anï؟½microbial resistance prevalence. 66 Seï؟½ng a boundary for anï؟½microbial use is challenging due to the uncertainï؟½es in the environmental risks from increasing anï؟½microbial resistance. 214 Based on WHO recommendaï؟½ons and available evidence on lowering average applicaï؟½on rates while keeping effecï؟½ve producï؟½on systems, 219 we propose the complete halï؟½ng of prophylacï؟½c anï؟½microbial use, and halving of exisï؟½ng average applicaï؟½on rates from 2020 values of 50 to 25 mg kg -1 animal. 159 Global shi s to flexitarian diets can reduce global anï؟½microbial use by around 42% compared to baseline values for 2050, whereas combining with moderate biosecurity measures could reduce global use by 49%. 221

# Aerosol loading

Air pollution is a major human health issue (Panel 3), negatively affects crop production by changing the photosynthetically available light, 222 and can have critical Earth system impacts on precipitation and circulation patterns of the atmosphere and ocean. The planetary boundary for aerosol loading is

1121 1122 1123 1124 1125 1126 1127 1128 1129 1130 1131 1132 1133 1134 1135 1136 1137 1138 1139 1140 1141 1142 1143 1144 1145 1146 1147 1148 1149 1150 1151 1152 1153 1154 1155 1156 1157 1158 1159 1160

expressed as the mean annual interhemispheric difference in the aerosol optical depth (AOD), 158 capturing the reduction in sunlight from air pollutants, such as nitrogen components (NH3, NOx and N2O), SO2, CO, organic compounds, and particulate matter (PM). Increased AOD affects regional rainfall patterns, and changes in the interhemispheric difference are associated with abrupt transitions in monsoon systems. Although the planetary boundary for atmospheric aerosol loading is not transgressed (Table 2), food systems are a prominent source of air pollutants (Figure A2.5), such as ammonia from fertiliser use and livestock production (37â€“47 Tg N yr -1 , 86% of total NH3 emissions); 167,223 airborne dust from human-driven land system change (e.g. desertification and land management practices); and primary PM2.5 emissions,

i.e. aerosolsâ€™ diameter smaller than 2.5 micrometre (>28% of total primary PM2.5; emissions), mostly from burning for clearing vegetation.
223 Dominant sources of emissions vary regionally and between hemispheres. 224 In parts of the northern hemisphere, N-based emissions from livestock and fertiliser are the dominant source of aerosol-loading, accounting for >80% of NH3 emissions forming secondary PM2.5 particles, while in the southern hemisphere, biomass burning is the predominant source of trace-gas emissions and PM2.5 concentrations. 224 Emissions from biomass burning (globally estimated to contribute 42 Tg yr -1 PM2.5), 225 that can be attributed agriculture are uncertain, although some estimates suggest that 28% of the global direct PM2.5 emissions are food related (Table A2.1). Regionally, shares can be higher, particularly in countries in the southern hemisphere (i.e. biomass burning contributes 90% to PM2.5 in Angola). 224

Remaining within aerosol boundaries requires emission reductions in both the northern and southern hemisphere. The boundary for nitrogen surplus (Secï؟½on Biogeochemical Flows) suggests a reduction of NH3 emissions (>45% reducï؟½on of current emissions, from 37 to 20 Tg N yr -1 ) is required, mostly in the northern hemisphere where N surplus (and associated NH3 emissions from manure and fertilizer167) is high (Figure 4c). Dietary shifts can reduce agricultural NH3 emissions with more than 50% in North America and Europe. 226 In the southern hemisphere, biomass burning from land conversion and on agricultural lands should be eliminated in line with the Land System change and Climate change boundaries. However, it is uncertain how much reduced NH3 emissions and biomass burning can bring back total PM2.5 concentrations across hemispheres. 159

# Ocean Acidification

Ocean acidification is linked to loss of marine species dependent on calcium carbonate, and changes to marine carbon storage. Acidification also aggravates impact of other biogeochemical changes (e.g. ocean warming and associated thermal heat stress) and therefore is considered a threat multiplier to marine life. 228 Ocean acidification, measured through the change in the aragonite saturation state (خ©arag), 158 is driven by atmospheric CO2 concentration (i.e. 25% of historical anthropogenic CO2 emissions are absorbed by the ocean 229 ) and is therefore strongly related to the climate boundary. Although the ocean acidificaï؟½on planetary boundary is not transgressed (Table 2), 158 it is moving rapidly into the zone of increasing risk following relatively constant CO2 emissions (total of 40.6 GtCO2yr -1 ï؟¼ of which food systems are responsible for nearly 25%. 159,172 Remaining with the food system boundary for climate change and biosphere integrity can effecï؟½vely bring back CO2 emissions close to zero and allows us to remain within ocean acidificaï؟½on boundary.

1162 1163 Stratospheric ozone depletion 1164 The planetary boundary for stratospheric ozone depletion limits the breakdown of ozone that protects 1165 life on Earth from harmful solar radiation. Coordinated global intervention phasing out production of 1166 ozone-depleting chlorofluorocarbons and halons (i.e. through the Montreal Protocol adoption in 1987) 158 1167 has restored the ozone layer. However, the most important ozone-depleting substance remaining is 1168 N2O, which is not regulated under the Montreal Protocol. Agriculture is the largest emitter of 230 1169 anthropogenic N2O (54â€“69% of total N2O emissions), but ozone depletion rates remain insignificant. 1170 Even without reductions in N2O emissions, stratospheric ozone levels are expected to recover by the end 167 1171 of this century. We therefore restate the nitrogen surplus boundary that respects local boundaries for 1172 biodiversity and water quality (Section Nitrogen and Phosphorus losses), and thereby reduces N2O 1173 emissions from reduced fertilizer and manure applications with 55% compared to current levels (from 3.9 -1 159,167 1174 to 1.8 Tg N yr). 1175 1176 Reducing the environmental footprint of food production 1177 Sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on (SEI) practices offer considerable potential to mitigate planetary 1178 boundary transgressions without negaï؟½vely affecï؟½ng yields (Figure 5). Importantly, many practices offer 1179 simultaneous benefits across several boundaries. Soil organic carbon (SOC) accumulaï؟½on can be 1180 enhanced with agricultural diversification practices that promote plant root-formation, microbial activity, 1181 and the return of organic matter to soils, thereby reducing HANPP, while uncertainï؟½es remain regarding 231,232 1182 the permanence of soil carbon pools which challenge estimates of this potential. Agricultural 1183 diversification increases agroecosystem biodiversity, which can be specifically managed to assist in weed 1184 and pest abatement, or to reduce the prevalence of crop diseases and nutrient losses while maintaining 1185 or improving yields. Improved ferï؟½liser management enhances crop NUE, reduces nutrient losses, and 1186 contributes to SOC, although with higher variability for GHG emissions and yields. 1187 1188 The effects of the pracï؟½ces (Figure 5) are not directly addiï؟½ve, since they were typically obtained from 1189 studies comparing a single pracï؟½ce against a controlled alternaï؟½ve. Pracï؟½ce effects vary depending on 1190 context-specific, biophysical factors such as climaï؟½c and soil condiï؟½ons, as well as the exact 1191 implementaï؟½on of the pracï؟½ce. Working farms typically implement mulï؟½ple pracï؟½ces simultaneously, 1192 and the evidence to date suggests synergies leading to enhanced environmental, as well as social, 233 1193 benefits. While the meta-analyses provide valuable informaï؟½on on the technical potenï؟½al at the field- 1194 scale, esï؟½mates of the global potenï؟½al of selected SEI pracï؟½ce groups have also been explored in 1195 modelling studies such as, but not limited to those menï؟½oned in Panels 5-7. These modelling studies, 1196 using different methodologies, factor in addiï؟½onal constrains and moderators, such as our current 1197 understanding of applicable areas. When applied to all suitable areas globally and assuming maximum 1198 complementarity among measures, sets of SEI pracï؟½ces were found to provide a potenï؟½al increase in 208 1199 nitrogen use efficiency, from its average of 48%, to 78%, and a global carbon sequestraï؟½on potenï؟½al âˆ’1 1200 between 0.44 and 0.68 Gt C yr. When limitaï؟½ons of applicaï؟½on as well as current applicaï؟½on levels were â€“1 175 factored in, the carbon sequestraï؟½on potenï؟½al was esï؟½mated to be 0.28 to 0.43 Gt C yr, while the GHG miï؟½gaï؟½on potenï؟½al of 4.4 Pg CO2e on average, was esï؟½mated to occur by 2050. Despite these

1203 limitaï؟½ons, our synthesis shows substanï؟½al evidence that the evaluated, key groups of SEI pracï؟½ces can 1204 contribute to maintaining or improving yields while miï؟½gaï؟½ng planetary boundary transgressions, based 1205 on the indicators considered (Figure 5; Table A2.5, Appendix 2B). The potenï؟½al global benefits of 1206 acceleraï؟½ng adopï؟½on of ecologically intensive pracï؟½ces on cropland vary across boundaries, but in 1207 combinaï؟½on with other food system pracï؟½ces enable significant impacts notably on climate and 1208 biosphere integrity boundaries (Panel 6). 1209

While field studies have shown that the exact outcomes of CA are context-specific, evidence shows that CA improves crop productivity, especially in dry climates and rainfed production systems, thus offering a climate-change adaptation strategy. Cells are coloured according to whether results fall inside our proposed food system planetary boundaries: within boundary (green), close to boundary (orange), beyond boundary (red).

FABLE model results show that under BAU, the food system boundaries for land, biodiversity, emissions, water and nutrient use are exceeded by 2050. Under an EL pathway, the food system stays within land, biodiversity, and emission boundaries. Increases in producï؟½vity, dietary shi s, and reduced food loss and waste, reduce the land area needed for agricultural producï؟½on which in turn drives the expansion of natural land available for biodiversity. Annual net GHG emissions would fall from 7.35 Gt CO2e in 2050 under BAU to 2.75 Gt CO2e under EL (~60% reducï؟½on compared to 2020). This reducï؟½on in GHG emissions is greater than those suggested by the economic model ensemble (median 20%), or DIA-GIO (32%) (Figure 10b), which may be explained by differences in how emissions are computed across models. GHG emission reducï؟½ons in the FABLE EL pathway are driven by a decrease in emissions from agricultural producï؟½on for both crops and livestock, and increased carbon sequestraï؟½on from avoided deforestaï؟½on and restoraï؟½on of abandoned agricultural land to forest and other natural land (with higher rates of carbon sequestraï؟½on in living biomass and soil). Nitrogen use is reduced by 30% compared to BAU, while there are relaï؟½vely small reducï؟½ons in water consumpï؟½on and phosphorus use, yet nutrient and water use conï؟½nue to exceed food system boundaries.

Combining EL with ecological intensificaï؟½on (EL+EI pathway) further reduces agricultural land area, water and nitrogen use, and substanï؟½ally reduces emissions to -0.51 Gt CO2e. Many of the EI pracï؟½ces that the FABLE Calculator are not used in many of the ensemble models. Emission reducï؟½ons are driven by increased sequestraï؟½on on croplands (in soil) and, to a lesser extent, addiï؟½onal conversion of agricultural land to other uses due to enhanced producï؟½vity for some commodiï؟½es. The FABLE model results suggest that significant effort in integraï؟½ng EI pracï؟½ces could contribute to achieving a net zero- emission food system. Esï؟½mates of the effect of the pracï؟½ces on soil organic carbon sequestraï؟½on are taken from global meta-analyses, and do not account for regional or local soil carbon saturaï؟½on limits, P56 which mean the results should be interpreted with cauï؟½on.

The most effecï؟½ve applicaï؟½on of EI pracï؟½ces needs to be tailored to local contexts and should be co- selected with local farmers and landscape stakeholders, integraï؟½ng local and scienï؟½fic knowledge. The complexity of implementaï؟½on at scale across mulï؟½ple domains, and local carbon sequestraï؟½on saturaï؟½on points may limit both the speed of implementaï؟½on, and duraï؟½on of carbon sequestraï؟½on potenï؟½al of ecological intensificaï؟½on. 1249

1251 Section 3: Justice in Food Systems 1252 1253 What is a Just Food System 1254 Justice involves the â€کfair treatmentâ€™ of people, both individuals and groups. Although various arï؟½culaï؟½ons 1255 of jusï؟½ce exist across disciplines and sectors (e.g., Philosophy, Economics, Sociology, and Law), here we 1256 draw on three interrelated dimensions of jusï؟½ce that feature prominently in the literature on social and 1257 environmental jusï؟½ce: namely, Distribuï؟½ve, Representaï؟½onal (or Procedural), and Recogniï؟½onal (Figure 259,260 1258 6). Distributive justice involves the fair distribution of important resources, opportunities, or 1259 capabilities (healthy food, wages, clean, healthy, and sustainable environment) as well as the fair 33 1260 allocation of benefits and burdens. Representational justice concerns decision-making and power, 259,261 1261 including formal policymaking processes as well as broader societal decision-making processes. It 1262 requires fair processes and a fair distribuï؟½on of power, the protecï؟½on of key freedoms, and poliï؟½cal voice 1263 and representaï؟½on, including meaningful participation by those most affected by injusï؟½ce. Recognitional 1264 justice manifests in the structures and norms of society and recognises a diversity of intersecting identities 1265 and experiences, shaped by cultural, legal, historical and spatial contexts, and involves people from all 259,261,262 1266 social groups being able to participate as equals. 1267 1268 Our analysis concerns how these three dimensions of jusï؟½ce play out both within and across socieï؟½es. For 1269 a society to be just, all people must experience distribuï؟½ve, representaï؟½onal and recogniï؟½onal jusï؟½ce, 1270 and for global and internaï؟½onal jusï؟½ce this must also exist among naï؟½ons and people throughout the 261,263 1271 world. Our analysis also encompasses both jusï؟½ce among people living at the same ï؟½me (intra- 33 1272 generaï؟½onal jusï؟½ce) and jusï؟½ce among people in different generaï؟½ons (inter-generaï؟½onal jusï؟½ce). For 1273 example, intergeneraï؟½onal jusï؟½ce requires that there be distribuï؟½ve jusï؟½ce across generaï؟½ons, including 1274 a fair distribuï؟½on of benefits and burdens between current and future generaï؟½ons of people. Keeping 1275 food systems within planetary boundaries can help to ensure that in the future, people have a safe 1276 environment, and ensuring healthy diets are accessible to all, parï؟½cularly for women of childbearing age 1277 and young children, can help ensure the health of future generaï؟½ons. Jusï؟½ce of all these forms is central 1278 to our analysis and recommendaï؟½ons. Another form of jusï؟½ce, interspecies jusï؟½ce, concerns whether 33 1279 humans treat other species fairly. However, beyond recognising the need to transiï؟½on to within the 1280 planetary boundaries, including keeping the Earth within 1.5 degrees C of global warming, and the need 1281 to minimise biodiversity and environmental losses, we do not focus on interspecies jusï؟½ce in our jusï؟½ce 1282 analysis. 1283 264 1284 Establishing what counts as â€کfair treatmentâ€™ for an analysis of jusï؟½ce, requires consensus, and in the 1285 absence of a global process relevant to food systems, we chose to align substanï؟½ally our account of â€کfair 265,266 1286 treatmentâ€™ with internaï؟½onal human rights treaï؟½es and other instruments (Figure 6). These human 1287 rights instruments represent a consensus view of what consï؟½tutes minimum acceptable standards for 265 1288 specific resources (e.g., food) and opportuniï؟½es (e.g., work). We appeal to the content of recognized 1289 human rights to idenï؟½fy jusï؟½ce-based enï؟½tlements, and to specify, and quanï؟½fy minimum acceptable levels (Figure 7, Appendix 3, Table 3). Our account of jusï؟½ce within food systems can thus be seen as the minimum condiï؟½ons for what would consï؟½tute jusï؟½ce. Recognising that all human rights are universal,

1292 indivisible, interdependent, and interrelated, we focus on three rights closely related to food systems: the 1293 right to food, the right to a healthy environment, and the right to decent work. We also draw on 1294 internaï؟½onal human rightsâ€™ commitments to non-discriminaï؟½on, parï؟½cipaï؟½on, and civil and poliï؟½cal 1295 rights. Central to our account is agency, understood as peopleâ€™s capacity to make choices that shape their 1296 own circumstances and to exercise voice through parï؟½cipaï؟½on in broader societal decision-making 1297 processes. Agency is recognized as an important contributor to food security, secure livelihoods and to 1298 addressing inequiï؟½es within food systems (Clapp et al., 2022) (Appendix 3). 1299 1300 Justice thus requires that everyone everywhere, regardless of social identity, has their rights to food, a 1301 healthy environment, and decent work realized, and has poliï؟½cal voice and fair representaï؟½on. Achieving 1302 jusï؟½ce therefore requires recognizing the social systems and other condiï؟½ons that can promote jusï؟½ce, 1303 or, conversely, can funcï؟½on as structural barriers to it. These include poliï؟½cal insï؟½tuï؟½ons and economic 1304 systems, and the interplay between them, resulï؟½ng in different kinds of voice, power, and agency for 1305 government, businesses, formal and informal food producers and workers, civil society organizaï؟½ons, and 1306 the public; food, health, educaï؟½onal, and other social systems, which provide important resources and 1307 opportuniï؟½es; and other social structures and norms. People are unevenly impacted by these social 1308 systems, with some groups systemaï؟½cally having fewer resources and opportuniï؟½es, and less voice, 1309 power, and agency, thus experiencing structural inequality that leaves them vulnerable and marginalised 1310 (Panel 7) Achieving jusï؟½ce may require fundamental transformaï؟½on of these poliï؟½cal systems. However, 1311 here our focus is that jusï؟½ce requires paying parï؟½cular aï؟½enï؟½on to these people and addressing their 262 1312 specific needs. 1313

1314 1315 1316 Figure 6. Conceptual framework of the jusï؟½ce secï؟½on. The three jusï؟½ce dimensions and three human rights are 1317 interrelated and interdependent. Achieving the three jusï؟½ce dimensions of distribuï؟½ve, recogniï؟½onal, and 1318 representaï؟½onal jusï؟½ce requires meeï؟½ng the human rights. 1319 Panel 7: Recogniï؟½onal Injusï؟½ces: The need for aï؟½enï؟½on to gender and intersecï؟½onality Different groups of people â€“ women, men, and children â€“ of different wealth and social groups, age and stage in the life cycle, of different ethniciï؟½es, and from different geographic locaï؟½ons have different livelihood opportuniï؟½es and o en have differenï؟½al nutriï؟½onal needs. They also have differing levels of choice in terms of how far they can access and afford healthy diets and decent work. Not paying aï؟½enï؟½on to these group differences can lead to significant harm, not only for the individuals concerned, but for humanity more broadly by losing contextual knowledge and pracï؟½ces (e.g., Indigenous farming pracï؟½ces).

Ethnic minoriï؟½es and Indigenous peoples are o en at the greatest risk of losing their livelihoods and access to healthy and culturally important food, processes that start with a loss of access to land or natural resources (e.g., forests) through unjust conservaï؟½on or extracï؟½on policies (e.g., exclusion from management), P57 or the lack of protecï؟½on policies. Many of these marginalised groups, including women, and parï؟½cularly women from minority groups, also face significant structural barriers and discriminaï؟½on, rooted in colonialism, racism, and patriarchy leading to lower access to technology,

training and underrepresentaï؟½on in poliï؟½cal and managerial posiï؟½ons, P58,P59 making it difficult for them to build decent and resilient livelihoods.

People living in countries with conflict are especially marginalised in numerous ways. Hunger and starvaï؟½on have o en been used as poliï؟½cal weapons of war, and conï؟½nue to be used as such today, through intenï؟½onal destrucï؟½on of food systems, especially agricultural lands (e.g., Sudan, Ukraine and Palesï؟½ne), withholding/restricï؟½ng food aid (e.g., Yemen and Palesï؟½ne), and unilateral coercive measures (e.g., Sudan, Venezuela and Syria). The impact of these conflicts spread across the whole food system, affecï؟½ng the availability and price of food and other resources (e.g., ferï؟½lizer), regionally and globally, though parï؟½cularly amongst the rural and those in poverty. P60 Thus, conflicts and war lead to further marginalisaï؟½on and directly or indirectly cause hunger, food insecurity, and famines undermining the right to food for all, P61 with worse effects on people in poverty, minority groups, women, and children.

These characterisï؟½cs are not exhausï؟½ve as marginalisaï؟½on can manifest differently across and within regions/countries. Furthermore, the opportuniï؟½es people have access to o en differ based on the intersecï؟½on of key characterisï؟½cs, such as wealth or poverty with gender, geographic locaï؟½on, age, and ethnicity. For example, in middle-income countries (MIC) women tend to be more food-insecure than men (Appendix 3, Figure A3.1). Yet, women appear to have greater dietary quality than men, especially in higher income countries (Appendix 3, Figure A3.1). Meanwhile poverty is increasingly urbanising at a global scale, with the urban poor lacking access to healthy food, paï؟½erns that emerge in part due to a changing climate and the migraï؟½on of the rural poor to urban areas. P62

An intersecï؟½onal lens (i.e. paying aï؟½enï؟½on to mulï؟½ple injusï؟½ces and aspects of idenï؟½ty that intersect to exacerbate the experience of marginalisaï؟½on) is therefore key to idenï؟½fying the specific vulnerabiliï؟½es affecï؟½ng marginalised people. P63 This requires sufficient levels of data disaggregaï؟½on across contexts, as well as recogniï؟½on of alternaï؟½ve knowledge systems, especially Indigenous knowledge. P64 Yet, data remains skewed towards parï؟½cular social groups and geographic regions. For example, evidence from Canada and the US suggests that migrant workers face poor and dangerous working condiï؟½ons and food insecurity, but literature on migrant workers in other regions, especially LMICs, is limited. P65 Similarly, some geographies, such as the Middle East, are persistently underrepresented in English published literature.

1321 Social Foundations 1322 We operaï؟½onalise our jusï؟½ce analysis through the lens of social foundaï؟½ons which require basic human 267,268 1323 rights, such as food, water, health, and energy, to be met for everyone. Previous work has focused 1324 on defining the minimum resources required to meet people's human rights, to avoid resource 1325 deprivaï؟½ons and harms. 33,268 However, the availability of resources alone is insufficient to avoid these 1326 harms. 269 Therefore, we instead focus on the condiï؟½ons that enable peopleâ€™s human rights to be met. We specify condiï؟½ons (i.e., social foundaï؟½ons) that would help enable each of the three rights (food, decent work, healthy environment) to be realized (Table 3). Through a combinaï؟½on of expert consultaï؟½on and a

1329 review of the literature, we first idenï؟½fy the individual and cross-cuï؟½ng food system components relevant 1330 to each human right. Next, and drawing on the outcomes from a recently completed mulï؟½-stage, mulï؟½- 29 1331 stakeholder evaluaï؟½on of food systems indicators, we idenï؟½fy a suite of candidate indicators for each 1332 component and screen for coverage, scale, relevance, and overlap. Where indicators for key food system 1333 components were not available (e.g., individual and collecï؟½ve agency) we reviewed the literature to 270 1334 propose addiï؟½onal indicators for development and monitoring. The final selecï؟½on included a limited 1335 set of indicators that conveyed discrete dimensions of the condiï؟½ons that enable peopleâ€™s rights to be 1336 met, rather than outcomes of these rights being (un)met, which act as our proxy variables. (Appendix 3). 1337 For each proxy variable, we specify the level at which the social foundaï؟½on would be considered met, 1338 esï؟½mate the number of people falling below each social foundaï؟½on globally, and examine the extent of 1339 harms associated with each of the three human rights not being realized (Table 3, Figure A3). 1340

1342 Figure 7a-f: The global status of social foundations in food systems. Each panel visualizes one of six (out of 8), proxy 1343 variables and its corresponding social foundation (Table 3). Maps show the global distribution of values, with places that fall below the social foundation in brown, and those where the social foundation is intact in green (white indicates no data available). Histograms on the colour legends indicate the total number of people that fall below

1346 each social foundation.
