1364 Evaluating Injustices 1365 1366 The Right to Food 1367 The right to food involves the right to a secure and stable supply of sufficient quanï؟½ï؟½es of affordable, 1368 healthy, culturally appropriate and sustainably produced food that considers peoplesâ€™ pracï؟½ces and 271,272 1369 customs, to ensure all forms of malnutriï؟½on can be avoided, and a dignified life supported. The right 1370 to food has been interpreted not only as a right to have access to sufficient food to enable a healthy diet 1371 but also as placing responsibiliï؟½es on states to promote good nutriï؟½on and health, for example through 1372 regulaï؟½ng food adverï؟½sing, labelling requirements and other measures that shape food environments to 271,273 1373 encourage healthier diets. We thus idenï؟½fy two social foundaï؟½ons linked to the right to food, namely 1374 healthy affordable diets and food environments that do not undermine peopleâ€™s capaciï؟½es to make 1375 healthy choices, which we refer to as â€œhealthy food environments.â€‌ 1376 1377 Healthy diets are considered, by the FAO, to be affordable when they cost less than 52% of average 274 1378 household income. We therefore align our minimum condiï؟½on/social foundaï؟½on for affordable healthy 1379 diets with the FAOâ€™s definiï؟½on of an affordable healthy diet (Table 3). In 2022, 2.8 billion people globally 1380 were below the social foundaï؟½on of an affordable healthy diet. This causes diet-related harms that extend 1381 beyond their immediate impacts to damage economies and increase risk of conflict and disease. Indeed, 1382 in the same year 2.3 billion people were food insecure and 1.8 billion had no access to clean water- 1383 strongly linked condiï؟½ons to poor food safety and nutrient absorpï؟½on (Table 3, Figure 7). 1384 1385 The extent to which regions fall below these foundations, and the severity of the associated harms, differ 1386 across geography and social group (i.e., ethnicity, gender, and wealth). While the rapid globalisaï؟½on of 1387 the food system through trade and subsidies has made more food available globally and brought food 1388 prices down, it has also suppressed wages and the prices producers receive, leaving healthy diets least 1389 affordable in Low-Income Countries (LIC) and Lower Middle-Income Countries (LMIC), where food 1390 insecurity, low-birth weights, and anaemia are also most prevalent (Figure 7, Figure A3.1). It is also these 1391 contexts where most food system workers live, leaving them o en least able to afford a healthy diet. 1392 1393 Although jusï؟½ce requires that healthy food becomes more affordable and available, this will not 4,275 1394 necessarily result in healthier diets. As economies develop and family incomes grow, healthy diets 1395 become more affordable but ultra-processed foods, animal-sourced red meats, and sugar sweetened 275â€“277 1396 beverages also become more available and exposure to unhealthy food adverï؟½sing increases. As a 1397 result, diet quality globally is lowest in Upper Middle-Income Countries (UMIC) and High-Income Countries 1398 (HIC), parï؟½cularly among men, while rates of overweight and obesity are highest in these same regions, 1399 parï؟½cularly among women (Figure A3.1). Furthermore, while healthy diets are more affordable in HIC, 1400 they remain unaffordable or inaccessible to many people, resulï؟½ng in lower diet quality and higher obesity 278,279 1401 rates for those on low incomes. In contrast, in UMIC such as places in Laï؟½n America, higher income 1402 groups, despite being food secure, have a poorer diet quality than low-income groups, likely due to the 280 1403 hyper-availability of unhealthy foods. Rapidly changing food environments and unequal access to housing, water, health and market services mean LMIC and UMIC, parï؟½cularly in urban and peri-urban

4,281 1405 areas, are now epicentres of mulï؟½ple forms of malnutriï؟½on and associated outcomes (Figure A3.1). 1406 These regions are also where most of the global populaï؟½on currently lives, and where most future 1407 populaï؟½on growth will occur, underscoring the criï؟½cal global importance of healthy diets being available 25 1408 and affordable, in a food environment that is conducive to healthy choices. 1409 1410 Food environments are the physical, economic, political, socio-cultural and digital contexts in which 1411 consumers engage with the food system to make decisions about acquiring, preparing and consuming 282 1412 food. Food environments and food choices are increasingly shaped by adverï؟½sing and commercial 1413 markeï؟½ng, thus expenditure on markeï؟½ng of unhealthy food would be an apt proxy variable for healthy 1414 food environments. However, global data on this exposure is lacking. As an alternaï؟½ve proxy indicator for 1415 an unhealthy food environment, we use intake of sugar from sugar sweetened beverages (SSB), which is 283 1416 associated with cardiometabolic disease and social inequities. We align our minimum condition for a 1417 healthy food environment with the WHOâ€™s recommendaï؟½on of less than 10% of total energy intake from 108 1418 free or added sugars (ideally reduced to less than 5%, as used in the PHD). In 2018, 5.6 billion people 1419 globally, were below the minimum condiï؟½on/social foundaï؟½on of a healthy food environment, with their 1420 intake of free or added sugar exceeding this level. These paï؟½erns are associated with over 75,000 early 1421 deaths, 2.5 billion adults, and 360 million children and adolescents who are overweight or living with 1422 obesity (Table 7, A3). 1423 1424 Food environments determine food choices in a range of ways depending on context, and considering the 1425 affordability, availability, accessibility or convenience, and desirability of foods in the food environment, 284 1426 especially for healthy food versus unhealthy food. Thus, while higher incomes can promote greater diet 284 1427 diversity, the type of food environment can weaken or strengthen this relaï؟½onship. In the face of rising 1428 corporate concentraï؟½on and markeï؟½ng of unhealthy food, territorial and local markets, with a focus on 1429 small-scale and informal actors, are seen to have the potenï؟½al not just to revitalise and stabilise local 281 1430 producï؟½on but also enhance healthy consumpï؟½on choices for poor people. This becomes criï؟½cally 1431 important where rapid urbanisaï؟½on is changing food environments in UMIC and LMIC, creaï؟½ng food voids 282 1432 (environments â€کlacking in foodâ€™), food deserts (areas where sufficient healthy food is inaccessible) and 1433 food swamps (areas where unhealthy, usually ultra-processed, foods285 are abundantly available, 282 1434 accessible, and affordable), earlier seen only in HIC (HLPE Report 19). In each of these cases, people are 281,282,286,287 1435 at higher risk of food insecurity, micronutrient deficiencies, overweight/obesity, and NCDs, but 1436 for different reasons. Low diet quality could be due to lack of availability, accessibility, and/or affordability 282 1437 of healthy foods, or because unhealthy foods are abundantly available, affordable, and convenient. 1438 1439 Changes in food environments have also accompanied changes in food cultures. The shift to unhealthy 1440 diets is characterised by a loss of traditional foods and food deskilling â€“ a loss of cooking skills and 275,288 1441 knowledge. Changes in food cultures can be explained by several factors. First, time poverty has 1442 increased especially for families due to greater womenâ€™s participation (who largely remain in charge of 1443 food provisioning despite improvements in gender equality) in the labour force and generally low food 289,290 1444 involvement of fathers. Second, the loss of local biodiversity, notably cultivated and foraged species, and resource access have impacted healthy food provision in some regions, especially amongst Indigenous communities. Children are especially susceptible to unhealthy food advertising, leading to unhealthy

1447 food habits, which can persist to adulthood. Shifts to unhealthy diets can involve moving food culture 1448 away from traditional foods, including family meals. These changes have implications for diets and health, 293 1449 but equally for the acquisition of food knowledge and skills, especially for children. 1450 1451 The Right to a Healthy Environment 1452 The right to a healthy environment rests on the recogniï؟½on that realizing the right to food and many other 1453 rights requires having a clean, healthy and sustainable environment. This right is understood as 1454 encompassing several substanï؟½ve rights, including to a safe climate and non-toxic environment, which 294 1455 we adopt as two social foundaï؟½ons of this right. Climate conditions habitable for humans have been 297,298 1456 broadly characterised as land with a mean annual temperature of â‰¤ 29 Cآ°, and we use this as our 1457 minimum condition for a safe climate. By 2070, 419 million people globally will fall below the social 33 1458 foundaï؟½on of a safe climate, and already in 2022 high temperatures accounted for the loss of 14 million 1459 DALYs6 (Figure 7, Table 3, Figure A3.2). Climate impacts are and will continue to be experienced 1460 inequitably, intersecting with poverty and geography to disproportionately impact certain peoples and 1461 regions. Despite being the least responsible, climate change will exacerbate experiences of food insecurity 299,300 1462 for many LICs across Africa and for people living in poverty. Meanwhile, women, particularly in rural 1463 areas, LMICs and Indigenous communities, and agricultural producers and workers are more likely to have 296,301â€“303 1464 their livelihoods undermined by climate change, and experience physical injury and ill-health. 1465 More generally, within food systems, women, children, Indigenous and rural communiï؟½es, and those in 1466 poverty, because of their marginalizaï؟½on, are most impacted by unsafe and unhealthy 295,296 1467 environments, underscoring the importance of human rights for all. 1468 1469 A wide range of food system pollutants exist that create toxic environments and have negaï؟½ve human 33 1470 health effects (Panel 3). We focus on only nitrogen leaching to groundwater, 75% of which comes from 33 1471 agriculture, due to data availability. Nitrogen pollution is connected to adverse reproductive effects, and 33,167 1472 cancer in humans. WHO guidelines specify a safe level of nitrogen for drinking water as 50 mg 304 1473 NO3âˆ’/litre, thus defining our minimum condition for healthy environments. In 2022, 5 billion people 1474 globally fell below this social foundaï؟½on and were exposed to unsafe levels of nitrogen leaching, which 1475 was associated with the loss of 42 billion DALYs (Figure 7, Table 3, Figure A3.2) and 4 million new cases 6,33,167,304 1476 of paediatric cancer a year. As with the right to food, these harms are not distributed equally with 305,306 1477 rural dwellers most likely to be affected by agricultural pollution and with the poorest regions of the 1478 world particularly across Africa, South Asia, and China experiencing the greatest number of DALYs due to 1479 unsafe water, with as few as 10% of rural populations having access to clean water (Figure A3.2). 1480 1481 The Right to Decent Work 15,307 13,307,308 1482 The right to decent work entails the right of everyone to live in dignity, to work in just and 1483 favourable condiï؟½ons, to be free to choose their work with a salary that allows them to live and support 1484 a family, and to receive equal pay. We idenï؟½fy two social foundaï؟½ons linked to the right to decent work, 1485 namely a living wage and meaningful representaï؟½on. A living wage conveys the minimum level of 307,308 1486 renumeraï؟½on necessary to support an acceptable standard of living for the worker and their family. Although not always put into effect, a legal minimum wage has been set in 90% of the 187 ILO countries.

13 1488 It equates to an average of 67% of the median wage in developing and 55% in developed naï؟½ons, which 1489 we use as the social foundaï؟½on of a living wage. In 2022, an esï؟½mated 34% of the food system workforce, 1490 including agricultural (agriculture, forestry, fishing) and non-agricultural, formal and informal workers, 1491 was below the social foundaï؟½on of a living wage, women earned 50% of what men did, and 159 million 309,310 1492 children were involved in child labour, predominantly in agriculture (Table 3, Figure 7, Figure A3.3). 1493 However, trade-offs exist and policies must balance the compeï؟½ng need of ensuring healthy food is 1494 affordable while providing adequate and liveable renumeraï؟½ons (Secï؟½on 5). 1495 311 1496 Womenâ€™s work in agriculture remains undervalued and often seen as an extension of domestic chores. 1497 Women are often paid by the piece instead of hourly rates like men, and their wages are sometimes given 312 1498 to male relatives, limiting their economic independence. If gendered wage gaps in agricultural labour 1499 remain unaddressed, condiï؟½ons will likely worsen because of the ongoing â€œfeminisation of agricultureâ€‌ in 313 1500 all regions other than HICs. This is due to men leaving for non-farm work in sectors such as construction 314 1501 to obtain more stable and lucrative livelihoods, inflaï؟½ng the presence of women in a low-paid sector. 1502 Furthermore, sexual harassment due to power imbalances between female farmworkers and employers 1503 remains pervasive (for example, studies have reported incidences of 24-93% in the US), with few 312 1504 opportunities for reporting due to stigma and risk of retaliation. However, food system injustices are 1505 not limited to women as male migrant farm workers, for example in the US, also face abuse and 312 1506 exploitation. Jusï؟½ce and the right to decent work require living wages for all food systems workers, 1507 regardless of gender and other forms of marginalisaï؟½on. 1508 1509 Collecï؟½ve bargaining is one way to enable the meaningful representaï؟½on of both workers and employers 316 1510 and ensure opportuniï؟½es to regulate the condiï؟½ons of work are present. The right to collecï؟½ve 317 1511 bargaining is a fundamental ILO convenï؟½on (No 98) and an enabling right that paves the way for 1512 inclusive and effecï؟½ve governance of work, ensuring decent wages and working condiï؟½ons, and social 316 1513 protecï؟½on. Collecï؟½ve bargaining coverage varies considerably around the world, but when present 1514 facilitates equality, contributes to safe and healthy work places, and builds resilience. We set our social 1515 foundaï؟½on for meaningful parï؟½cipaï؟½on at a collecï؟½ve bargaining coverage rate of 72% of employees, 318 1516 which although below the EUâ€™s threshold of 80%, is equivalent to that found by the ILO in mulï؟½- 1517 employer seï؟½ngs, that tend to be more successful. Between 2008-2020, 2.6 billion people globally were 1518 below the social foundaï؟½on of meaningful representaï؟½on, with no access to collecï؟½ve bargaining (Figure 319 1519 7, Table 3). 1520 1521 Collective bargaining is highest in HICs, reaching close to 100% in some countries (e.g., Italy, France), but 316 1522 low in LMICs, where it ranges from 0%-40% and can be close to 1% (e.g., in Malaysia and Paraguay). The 320 1523 majority (69%) of the approximately 1.02 billion people employed in food systems (Figure A3.3), who 310 1524 support an esï؟½mated 3.8 billion people, are found in LMICs (i.e., Asia or Africa). Most of these jobs are 1525 informal, seasonal, unregulated, and unmonitored, with workers living in poverty, reflecï؟½ng the low levels 13 1526 of representaï؟½on. Average agricultural wages are near or below naï؟½onal minimum wages (Figure A3.3), 1527 leaving food system workers likely one of the groups least able to afford a healthy diet. Yet, in these contexts, agricultural livelihoods account for over 50% of employment (Figure A3.3) underscoring the considerable and cascading importance of meaningful representaï؟½on for these workers.

1530 1531 Despite generally high levels of collective bargaining in HIC, recent shifts in food systems towards more 1532 concentrated systems and specialized units of production (e.g., intensive production of a single crop or 1533 species with high technological inputs such as salmon or chicken) has undermined worker participation 315 1534 resulting in poor labour standards and animal welfare. This is partly due to informal contracts being the 321,322 1535 norm among small and medium enterprises in LMICs and some HICs. There is evidence that informal 1536 enterprises are highly organized and streamlined, yet the labour conditions can be exploitative, including 323,324 1537 exploitation of child labour. Forced labour is prevalent on land and at sea, not only in LMICs but also 325 1538 in some HIC, including the US food supply chain. Therefore, a just food system would include food 1539 systems workers (especially smallholders) earning a living wage, with access to collective bargaining and 1540 the ability to advocate for their rights and working conditions. 1541 1542 Individual and Collecï؟½ve Agency 1543 Agency, peopleâ€™s capacity to make choices that shape their own circumstances and to parï؟½cipate in 1544 broader societal decision-making processes, has been recognized as an important contributor to food 1545 security and nutriï؟½on, secure livelihoods, decent work, healthy environments, and to addressing wider 1546 inequiï؟½es within food systems. Meanwhile, ample evidence exists on how peopleâ€™s freedoms and agency 296,326,327 1547 are curtailed within current food systems. We thus recognise individual and collecï؟½ve agency as 1548 not just important in themselves, but specific forms of agency are also, arguably, guaranteed by human 1549 rights. Human rights emphasise the importance of rightsholders parï؟½cipaï؟½ng in decisions that affect 1550 them, and human rights treaï؟½es and other instruments include many rights guaranteeing civil and poliï؟½cal 1551 freedoms, and specific forms of choice or control for individuals and groups. Human rights is commiï؟½ed 1552 to eliminaï؟½ng discriminaï؟½on. Taken together, these components of human rights embody a broad 1553 commitment to individual and collecï؟½ve agency. Recognising that it is not possible to fully quanï؟½fy 1554 agency, and that it remains challenging to idenï؟½fy proxy variables that capture agency in this capacious 1555 sense, we propose three social foundaï؟½ons and related proxy variables that capture three facets of 270,328â€“ 1556 agency: non-discriminaï؟½on, freedom from corporate control, and freedom of voice and expression. 330 1557 1558 1559 Non-Discriminaï؟½on 1560 Peopleâ€™s agency â€“ their capacity to make choices and parï؟½cipate in decision-making -- can be constrained 1561 by structural inequaliï؟½es shaped by social posiï؟½on and idenï؟½ty, parï؟½cularly class and gender. Ensuring 1562 that all people have agency thus requires addressing discriminaï؟½on in all its forms, including inequaliï؟½es 1563 in access to key resources and services, unjust power differenï؟½als between different groups and 1564 intenï؟½onal discriminaï؟½on. As a proxy variable for non-discriminaï؟½on, we use the Social Insï؟½tuï؟½ons and 1565 Gender Index which measures discriminaï؟½on against women, though this indicator is parï؟½al given the 1566 presence of discriminaï؟½on against many disï؟½nct groups. 1567 1568 Discriminaï؟½on against women is prevalent globally, although highest in South Asia, the Middle East & 1569 North Africa, and across many parts of sub-Saharan Africa (Figure A3.4). In no country in the world are women afforded the same opportunities as men that allow them equal representation in government or in the workforce more broadly, undermining their access to decent work and healthy food. Agency is

1572 1573 1574 1575 1576 1577 1578 1579 1580 1581 1582 1583 1584 1585 1586 1587 1588 1589 1590 1591 1592 1593 1594 1595 1596 1597 1598 1599 1600 1601 1602 1603 1604 1605 1606 1607 1608 1609 1610 1611

therefore about inequality and choice; what maï؟½ers is the â€œinequaliï؟½es in peopleâ€™s capacity to make choices rather than in differences in the choices themselvesâ€‌. 270,296,326â€“328

Freedom from corporate control Highly concentrated market structures, where just a few firms dominate key steps along food supply chains, 332 can signal power imbalances where dominant firms, due to weakened compeï؟½ï؟½on, can exercise market power that enables them to generate excess profits 14,333,334 The exercise of market power by large firms can in turn undermine the agency within food systems, especially for the most marginalized people. Most economists consider that the likelihood of firms exercising market power increases where the top four firms control over 40% of the market. 334 Although other metrics are also o en used, due to data limitaï؟½on we focus on CR4, for which data is more available, and specify 40% as our minimum social foundaï؟½on for freedom from corporate control, CR4 above 40% would indicate peopleâ€™s agency is at risk of being curtailed and thus requires further invesï؟½gaï؟½on (Table 3).

Strong evidence has shown a very high degree of corporate concentraï؟½on all along food supply chains, with just a few large firms dominaï؟½ng internaï؟½onal markets for agricultural inputs (seeds and ferï؟½lisers), agricultural commodity trade (soybean, poultry), certain processed and packaged foods (biscuits, confecï؟½onary, so drinks) and naï؟½onal markets for food retail. 14 Evidence suggests that access to market power is extensive in food systems, allowing dominant firms to set prices and generate excess profits. 14,17

Concentrated firmsâ€™ capacity to raise prices for seeds, ferï؟½lizer, grain, or packaged foods, has an impact on peopleâ€™s access to food, influencing which foods they can and cannot consume. Dominant firms that source from smaller scale food producers have a degree of â€کbuyer powerâ€™, meaning they can dictate the prices they are willing to pay to producers and other suppliers, such as workers, thus directly impacï؟½ng suppliersâ€™ incomes and ability to access food. 17

Large firms and concentrated commercial acï؟½vity also shape broader condiï؟½ons within food systems through business decisions. Corporate strategies to increase the level of processing of packaged convenience foods, for example, have widespread impacts on both food producï؟½on and consumpï؟½on environments, and can encourage people to eat less healthy foods because of limited product choices and manipulaï؟½ve adverï؟½sing and packaging. 17,335 Business decisions of dominant firms have a disproporï؟½onate effect on agricultural producï؟½on technologies, labour condiï؟½ons for food systems workers, including in some cases forced labour, 336 and environmental pracï؟½ces, 337 which directly impact producersâ€™ and consumersâ€™ opï؟½ons. Large and dominant firms in concentrated markets can influence policy and governance processes in ways that influence democraï؟½c parï؟½cipaï؟½on in food systems governance, which can directly undermine agency. 14 This influence is through direct lobbying as well as indirect means including sponsorship of research and revolving door appointments between industry and government, influencing measures such as dietary guidelines and regulaï؟½ons within food systems, 338,339

o en crowding out ciï؟½zen voices in the policy process.

Measuring the impact of corporate power on human agency in food systems is not straighï؟½orward, in large part because well-established metrics for agency do not exist, and firmsâ€™ market share data and other indicators like firmsâ€™ marginal profits and markups (the amount firms charge over and above their

1614 costs) are not always publicly available. Corporate concentraï؟½on (as measured by CR4) and profit data 1615 can usefully serve as proxy indicators to idenï؟½fy where corporate power may exist within food systems. 1616 However, food systems market data, at both global and naï؟½onal scales is o en unavailable or behind 1617 prohibiï؟½ve paywalls presenï؟½ng challenges for assessment of these dynamics. We therefore underscore 1618 the need for companies to make profit and markup data available, enabling the tracking of an indicator of 1619 market power. 1620 1621 Although this approach would benefit from more transparent data reporï؟½ng from firms, these indicators 1622 highlight markets where dominant firms are more likely to influence food systems in ways that can 1623 constrain peopleâ€™s agency. Stronger policies are needed at the naï؟½onal and internaï؟½onal levels to prevent 1624 corporate concentraï؟½on from subverï؟½ng agency through market and other forms of power available to 1625 large and dominant firms. More robust compeï؟½ï؟½on policies, for example, can help in prevenï؟½ng excessive 17,340 1626 market power and ensure prices remain fair for both buyers and sellers. 1627 1628 Freedom of expression and parï؟½cipaï؟½on in decision-making 1629 We focus on freedom of expression as an important facet of individual and collecï؟½ve agency, because it 1630 allows ciï؟½zens and governments to be beï؟½er informed, enables civil society to hold corporaï؟½ons to 1631 account, and helps to sustain a range of other human rights16 However, in many parts of the world, most 1632 notably in parts of the Middle East, North Africa, Eastern Europe, and Asia, freedom of expression is 1633 significantly curtailed, which in turn undermines agency (Figure A3.4). Although indicators can never fully 1634 capture complex concepts, as policies and laws are mediated by social context, the V-Dem freedom of 1635 expression index captures the extent to which a government respects freedom of press and media, 1636 freedom of people to discuss poliï؟½cal maï؟½ers at home or in public, as well as the freedom of academic 1637 and cultural expression. Countries with laws upholding the freedom of expression, may curtail freedom in 1638 pracï؟½ce and vice versa, we nevertheless draw on this as a preliminary indicator in the absence of more 1639 nuanced data. 1640 1641 When people have agency to parï؟½cipate in food systems governance and decision-making in meaningful 1642 ways, they can shape their food systems to make them more just. Mulï؟½ple possibiliï؟½es for civil society 1643 and ciï؟½zen engagement exist in democracies, including on maï؟½ers related to food systems, such as 4 1644 elecï؟½ons, public debates, and consultaï؟½ons on keys laws and policies. However, where these 1645 opportuniï؟½es to engage are lacking due to discriminaï؟½on, exclusion or in many cases, lack of freedom of 1646 expression (table 3), people are more likely to be marginalised and exposed to harm, and may take acï؟½on 15 1647 through protests or other forms of movements. 1648 1649 With the growing concentration of power-financial, technological, economic, and political â€“ across many 1650 food systems, citizen agency is increasingly claimed or manifested through diverse forms of food activism. 1651 These forms of food acï؟½vism span rights-based movements for justice, reactive street protests, alternative 1652 food systems governance structures, initiatives safeguarding the quality and cultural significance of food, 341 1653 and other modes of individual and collective advocacy. Movements for food jusï؟½ce and protests o en involve marginalised groups â€“ farmers, women, Indigenous and rural peoples â€“ and are moï؟½vated by concerns about food and nutriï؟½on security, prices and affordability, and livelihoods, and seek the

1656 implementaï؟½on of the basic right to food. At ï؟½mes however, as in Europe more recently, farmersâ€™ 1657 protests, though challenging the growing concentraï؟½on of power within the food system, may go against 342 1658 more ambiï؟½ous state policies to address climate change or food systems transformaï؟½ons. Nevertheless 1659 ciï؟½zensâ€™ freedom of expression and other aspects of agency remain central to addressing structural 341,343 1660 inequaliï؟½es and discriminaï؟½on within the food system. 1661 1662 The nature of state response to ciï؟½zen agency varies, ranging from neglect or repression to concessions 1663 and even policy change, and widespread ciï؟½zen consultaï؟½on in processes of decision-making cannot be 341 1664 taken for granted. States appear responsive to movements for food justice, o en to avoid poliï؟½cal 342 1665 conflict or public criï؟½que, though at ï؟½mes also driven by the global push for sustainability. Different 1666 forms of ciï؟½zen agency and their associated state responses give voice to diverse groups of stakeholders 341 1667 affected by food system changes and are key to representaï؟½onal jusï؟½ce. 1668 1669 In sum, agency is a social foundaï؟½on for the rights to food, decent work and a healthy environment, and 1670 is independently important from a jusï؟½ce perspecï؟½ve. Freedom of expression and other civil and poliï؟½cal 1671 freedoms support agency within food systems, while corporate concentraï؟½on and discriminaï؟½on 1672 undermine it. When people lack agency, they may assert it with protests and other forms of acï؟½vism. For 1673 all these reasons, it is important to try and characterize, quanï؟½fy and track agency. 1674 1675 A Safe and Just Space 1676 A just transformaï؟½on should look to keep everyone within a safe and just space, below the boundary that 1677 provides planetary stability, and above the foundaï؟½on that supports jusï؟½ce (shown in green in Figure 8). 1678 However, the social harms associated with falling below the social foundaï؟½ons defined here are not evenly 1679 distributed, leaving far too many people with unmet human rights (to the le of the dashed verï؟½cal line 1680 in Figure 8). Meanwhile, people are not equally responsible for the extent to which the food system 1681 boundaries are already transgressed; far too many peopleâ€™s dietary habits, if adopted globally, would 1682 cause the global food system to exceed the planetary boundaries (e.g. dietary paï؟½erns of countries that 1683 lie above the dashed horizontal line in Figure 8). Moving the global population to a safe and just space 1684 requires everyone to take reasonable responsibility for keeping the food systems within planetary 1685 boundaries, by minimising the pressures they exert on the planet and supporï؟½ng efforts to ensure the 1686 benefits from food systems are distributed fairly and everyone's human rights are met (Figure 8). 1687

1688 Decisions regarding what constitutes â€کreasonable responsibilityâ€™ for keeping the food systems within 1689 planetary boundaries involve considerations of fair allocation. These decisions should be reached through 1690 deliberative processes that take current capabilities and past responsibilities into account, including 1691 where lasting legacies (e.g. colonial histories) continue to produce unjust outcomes for many marginalised 1692 communities. These processes can draw on existing principles and mechanisms that support equitable 1693 burden-sharing in international processes and negotiations (e.g. climate change and trade), such as 344 1694 common but differentiated responsibilities and differentiated capabilities.

1696 Here, we apply the simplest principle as a starï؟½ng point, which assumes an equal share of the planetary 1697 boundaries but makes no accommodation for differentiated capabilities or needs (e.g. more efficient 344 1698 technologies). For each country, and for five planetary boundaries where combined data is available 1699 (GHG emissions, land use, water use, nitrogen, phosphorus), we calculate if and how the food systems 1700 share of the planetary boundary values would be exceeded if the dietary consumption patterns of that 1701 one nation were to be adopted globally. We contrasted this with esï؟½mates of a countryâ€™s distance from 1702 three social foundaï؟½ons, which had the most comprehensive data (diet affordability, agricultural wages, 1703 and nitrogen leaching) (Appendix 3). We used Kobe plots to visualize associations between the social 1704 foundations and planetary boundaries. 1705 1706 On average 3.7 billion people, predominantly in low-income and lower-middle countries in Sub Saharan 1707 Africa and South Asia do not benefit from the social foundations (countries whose foundation <100). In 1708 these contexts, key barriers limit ciï؟½zens abiliï؟½es to meet their human rights. At the same ï؟½me, 6.9 billion 1709 people, from all income groupings, lie above the planetary boundaries (countries with average boundary 1710 >100%) (Figure 8). These are countries whose dietary paï؟½erns, if adopted globally, would transgress all 1711 planetary boundaries. Therefore, policies that look to secure social foundaï؟½ons, must simultaneously 1712 ensure that changes to dietary paï؟½erns will not put disproporï؟½onate pressure on planetary boundaries. 1713 Meanwhile only 115 million people are currently in the safe and just space. Efforts to support jusï؟½ce have 1714 not succeeded in protecï؟½ng the planet, and conversely condiï؟½ons that support a safe planet, do not 1715 necessarily guarantee jusï؟½ce. Policies are therefore needed to simultaneously protect the planet and 1716 address jusï؟½ce, because currently, almost every country in the world is outside (or on the edge) of the 1717 Safe and Just corridor, with places least responsible for destabilising the planet falling the furthest below 1718 the social foundaï؟½ons. Similarly, those furthest above the planetary boundaries tend also to be the 1719 wealthiest, with the diets of the top third of the global populaï؟½on, from High-Income and Upper Middle- 1720 income naï؟½ons, amounï؟½ng to over 70% of the food system share of the planetary pressure. 1721 1722

1725 1726 Figure 8: Global population distribution among planetary boundaries and social foundations. Kobe plot 1727 contrasts each countryâ€™s food system contribution to five planetary boundaries (y-axis, average % for GHG 1728 emissions, land, nitrogen, phosphorus, and water, where 100% is the boundary) by their status of three social 1729 foundations (x-axis, average status for diet affordability, agricultural wages, and nitrogen exceedance to 1730 groundwater, where 0% is the foundation). Large circles are regional averages, sized by total population. We 1731 estimated the total number of people in countries that fell below social foundations (3.7 billion people) or 1732 transgressed planetary boundaries (6.9 billion people). Countries above social foundations and below planetary 1733 boundaries would be in the Safe and Just corridor. Kobe plots comparing each social foundation and planetary 1734 boundary are shown in Figure A3.4. Each countryâ€™s social foundaï؟½ons status was normalized to have each social 1735 foundaï؟½on = 100%, where countries would fall below (<100%) or above (>100%) the foundaï؟½on. Values above 1736 the foundaï؟½on were capped at a maximum of 300%.

1738 Secï؟½on 4: Assessing potenï؟½al environmental and socioeconomic

1739 consequences of a food systems transformaï؟½on 1740 1741 Exploring what food systems that are more aligned with health, environmental, and justice objectives 1742 could look like, requires assessing the potential consequences of how food systems would be 1743 restructured by dietary change, increased productivity, and reduced food loss and waste (FLW). We 1744 use two complementary modelling approaches for that purpose. 1745 1746 First, we assembled a multi-model ensemble of ten global economic models used in high-level 345â€“350 1747 assessments of climate change, land use and food security and involved in the Agricultural Model 350â€“352 1748 Intercomparison and Improvement Project (AgMIP). The ensemble of economic models includes 1749 a mix of parï؟½al equilibrium models (agricultural sector only), with more detailed biophysical and 1750 sectoral representaï؟½on, and general equilibrium models (whole of economy), which can simulate 1751 interacï؟½ons across non-agricultural sectors (e.g., energy). All simulate important system dynamics that 1752 would drive changes across a transforming food system, including, for example, the ability of producers 1753 to subsï؟½tute inputs based on their availability and cost. 1754 1755 Second, we used DIA-GIO (the Global Input-Output module of the Dietary Impact Assessment model), 1756 an updated version of the static input-output food model developed for the 2019 EAT-Lancet 194 1757 Commission (See modelling description in Appendix 4 for summary of updates). This was done to 1758 improve comparability with the modelling methodology used by the 2019 Commission, and 1759 recognizing there is uncertainty to the capacity of economic systems to adapt to rapid large-scale 1760 changes. Delays to a food systems transformaï؟½on, for example, would constrain the scope of food 1761 systems to adapt, limiï؟½ng the possibility to efficiently reallocate resources across alternate uses such 1762 as energy or fibre producï؟½on. Therefore, we used DIA-GIO as an accounï؟½ng tool to scope the 1763 biophysical opï؟½on space based on the current and projected supply, demand, and trade relaï؟½onships, 1764 but without the effects of economic feedback. This allowed us to consider potenï؟½al implicaï؟½ons of a 1765 food system with constrained capacity for, or dedicated policies (e.g., on land-use changes) that would 1766 regulate, resource reallocaï؟½on. 1767 1768 There is substantial uncertainty in how food systems would transform, and using a range of models 1769 and modelling approaches allows us to engage with this uncertainty, which we will do by reporting 1770 ensemble median, and where appropriate the full ensemble range (i.e., min to max) as well as 1771 comparing to DIA-GIO. See supplementary materials (Appendix 4) for more detailed documentation 1772 on models, and modelling approaches applied in this analysis. 1773 1774 Defining alternative future food systems 1775 In addition to updating and extending the modelling suite, this analysis has updated the scenario 1776 framework used to assess global and regional changes across food systems. We constructed three core 1777 scenarios described below (Table A4.3). 1778 1779 The business-as-usual scenario (BAU) projects how food systems could look by 2050 assuming a continuation of current trends and policies. This scenario assumes population and economic growth based on the â€œMiddle-of-the-Road" scenario (SSP2) of the Shared Socioeconomic Pathways, a scenario

st 353â€“ 1782 framework designed for the IPCC to model the consequences of climate change in the 21 century. 356 1783 This scenario projects global population and GDP to grow by 23% to 9.6 billion people and 127% to 357â€“359 1784 282 trillion US$ between 2020 and 2050 respectively (Figure A4.2, Table A4.9). To simulate the 1785 consequences of climate change in the BAU, we applied a representaï؟½ve concentraï؟½on pathway (RCP 1786 7.0) which assumes limited miï؟½gaï؟½on efforts and temperature increases of around 2آ°C by 2050. The 1787 biophysical consequences of this future climate were represented using published estimated changes 360 361 362 1788 in crop yields, livestock yields, and agricultural labour productivity. Following model 1,194 1789 parameterization in the 2019 Commission, DIA-GIO BAU projections to 2050 were informed by 1790 projections from IMPACT363, one of the participating global economic models. 1791 1792 The EL scenario (EAT-Lancet style food systems transformation) starting in 2020 deviates from BAU 1793 along three key dimensions. In EL, we assume that by 2050: (1) Healthy diets (Table 1) are adopted by 1794 all; (2) a 7-10 percent increase on top of global BAU agricultural productivity based on projections 350,364 350,364 1795 quantified as a part of previous model intercomparisons for SSP1, a more sustainable 1796 shared-socioeconomic pathway (Table A4.10); and (3) a halving of BAU food loss and waste (FLW), 1797 consistent with SDG 12.3. Finally, an additional scenario, ELM (EAT-Lancet Mitigation) combines EL 347,350 1798 with ambitious mitigation. Ambitious mitigation was based on previous modelling efforts to 1799 assess determinants of land-use across SSP projections350 and land-use policies and decarbonization 347,350 1800 pathways to achieve 1.5آ°C warming by the end of the century. Ambiï؟½ous miï؟½gaï؟½on includes a 1801 range of policies (emissions pricing and land-use regulaï؟½ons), which define market condiï؟½ons that 1802 determine the mix of technologies and pracï؟½ces (e.g., afforestaï؟½on, renewables, and bioenergy) 1803 adopted to maintain carbon budgets. Each economic model has somewhat different capacity to model 1804 decarbonizaï؟½on pathways, see Table A4.17 for a summary of each model's implementaï؟½on of 1805 miï؟½gaï؟½on. This scenario was run recognising that a transiï؟½on towards more sustainable food systems 1806 cannot be achieved without a wider societal transition towards greater sustainability. This scenario 1807 also allowed us to assess potential synergies and trade-offs between a food systems transformaï؟½on 1808 and ambiï؟½ous economy-wide miï؟½gaï؟½on efforts, parï؟½cularly through potenï؟½al compeï؟½ï؟½on for land- 1809 use. While miï؟½gaï؟½on efforts would be expected to reduce climate impacts by end-of-century, we keep 1810 BAU climate impacts (RCP 7.0) in ELM, recognising the uncertainty of these efforts translaï؟½ng to 1811 reducï؟½ons in climate impacts by 2050. 1812 1813 We emphasise that these scenarios aim to explore end points of a food systems transformation, and 1814 do not explore possible pathways that could lead to these outcomes by 2050. Assessing the implication 1815 of these endpoints is an important first step in understanding the potential benefits and costs of the 1816 changes simulated in these scenarios. Future work is needed to explore the pathways of change under 1817 varying assumptions of global development. 1818 1819 Comparing alternative food systems projections across modelling approaches 1820 Across the model ensemble, the BAU scenario, compared to 2020, shows a 37% increase in global 1821 agricultural production by 2050 (Figure 9a). Livestock production increases by around 33% and crops 1822 by 37%. All regions see increased overall agricultural production from 2020, with the largest increases 1823 in Sub-Saharan Africa (around 100%) and the smallest increases in China (11% for ensemble, 18% for DIA-GIO) and Europe (18% for ensemble, 16% for DIA-GIO) (Figure 9b).

1826 Model results suggest that by 2050, the combined effects in an EL scenario could result in total 1827 agricultural production (AGR) near 2020 levels, but with important changes to the composition of 1828 production (Figure 9c). Compared to 2020, livestock production declines by 22% (27% for DIA-GIO). 1829 Driven primarily by reductions in demand for crop production for animal feed which would decline 1830 substantially from BAU 2050 levels. While the models agree on the direction of change (i.e., declining 1831 crop production compared to BAU) there is less agreement on the magnitude of this reduction, with 1832 some models suggesting 2050 crop production would remain above 2020 levels, whereas others 1833 suggesting it could fall slightly below 2020 levels. 1834 1835 A sectoral analysis demonstrates that the largest declines are in ruminant (RUM) production (cattle, 1836 goats, sheep) with a 55% (75% for DIA-GIO) reduction compared to BAU 2050 levels, or a 33% (63% for 1837 DIA-GIO) decline on 2020 levels across the ensemble (Figure 9c, Figure A4.3). This is accompanied by 1838 a decline in global ruminant animal numbers of 26% (result reported by IMPACT and GLOBIOM). Non- 1839 ruminant production saw a smaller decline of 24% (-46% in DIA-GIO), with larger declines for pork (- 1840 48% based on three ensemble models reporting, -83% in DIA-GIO) than poultry (-14% for both DIA-GIO 1841 and the mean of two reporting ensemble models) production (Figure A4.4). The economic models 1842 generally show smaller declines in production compared to DIA-GIO, as other sectors (e.g., for pet 1843 food, bone meal) increase their demand for animal sourced foods (See Figure A4.10 for changes in 1844 food, feed, and other demand). EL demand for animal feed declines (-3%) from around 2.5 billion 1845 tonnes in 2020 to 2.4 billion tonnes by 2050. This compares to a 39% increase in feed production to 1846 around 3.5 billion tonnes in BAU. 1847 1848 There is less ensemble model agreement on changes in cereals, but the median results show modest 1849 production declines in rice (-13%), wheat (-4%), and coarse grains (all other grains) (-3%). Sugar crop 1850 production also shows absolute declines (-7% for ensemble, -30% for DIA-GIO) on 2020 levels in EL. 1851 1852 The modelling approaches differ in the projections of vegetables, fruits, nuts and seeds, legumes, roots 1853 and tubers, primarily due to different commodity aggregations methods (VFN in Figure 9). When we 1854 disaggregate the food group, we see much greater agreement between the ensemble and DIA-GIO, 1855 with vegetable production increasing by 42% (48% for DIA-GIO), fruit by 61% (36% for DIA-GIO), nuts 1856 by 172% (155% for DIA-GIO), legumes by 187% (56% for DIA-GIO), with roots and tubers declining by 1857 9% (-42% for DIA-GIO). Differences in legumes can be attributed to ensemble models reporting 1858 soybeans (accounting for the bulk of legume production) as oilseeds (OSD) rather than legumes, 1859 highlighting differences in how different disciplines (i.e., nutrition vs production) analyse food 1860 production and consumption. When we combine OSD and legumes across the ensemble the median 1861 production by 2050 for EL is 1.07 billion tonnes compared to 1.08 billion for DIA-GIO. 1862 1863 Considering the impacts of individual drivers, if we only include improved productivity the median 1864 projecï؟½on for producï؟½on across the ensemble is similar to BAU (38% vs 37% for BAU), but the range 1865 of model projecï؟½ons is somewhat higher (31%-53% min-max vs 25%-41% in BAU) (Figure A4.5). The 1866 use of global economic models highlights that, in isolation (and without dedicated policies), efficiency 1867 gains may not translate to lower overall production and may even increase it. Reducing FLW or dietary 1868 change in isolation leads to a smaller increase in production of 29% (17% for DIA-GIO) and 15% (18% for DIA-GIO) respectively compared to 2020 (Figure A4.5). Reduced FLW achieves this by shrinking the gap between what is consumed and what is purchased, signalling a need for less production to meet

1871 demand, whereas a shift to healthy diets would avoid overconsumption and reduce the demand for 1872 more resource intensive animal sourced foods and animal feed. 1873 1874 ELM shows larger declines in production compared to EL across livestock sectors, with RUM, NRM, and 1875 DRY declining by 37, 35, and 15 percent compared to 33, 24, and 7 percent respectively. These larger 1876 declines are the consequence of increased production costs from the pricing of emissions. On the other 1877 hand, ELM has slightly larger increases across crop production, due to increased demand for crops 1878 from a growing bioeconomy (e.g., biofuels, biomass, bioplastics) to facilitate a transition away from 1879 fossil fuels (Figure 9e, Figure A4.10). 1880

1897 OSA = Other South and Central America; SEA = Southeast Asia; SSA = Sub-Saharan Africa; USA = United States of 1898 America. 1899 1900 Exploring the biophysical option space of a food systems transformation 1901 We estimated the consequences of an EAT-Lancet food systems transformation on the Earth system. 1902 Where possible, we align the control variables with the food system boundaries defined in Section 3 1903 (land, GHG, blue water). However, where models cannot currently report against these variables 1904 (Nitrogen surplus and Phosphorus loss) we have used control variables from the 2019 Commission 1905 (Nitrogen use and Phosphorus use) as proxy indicators. 1906 1907 Assessing potential impact on Climate, Land, Water, Nitrogen, and Phosphorous 1908 Boundaries 1909 The modelling results show that in BAU, the environmental impact of agricultural production is 1910 projected to increase compared to 2020, where agricultural production is already above the food share 1911 of multiple planetary boundaries, with animal products responsible for the majority of emissions, and 1912 grains for the majority of nitrogen, phosphorous, and water use (Figure 10a). Agricultural emissions 1913 are expected to rise by 33% (20 to 50%; 51% for DIA-GIO) in the model ensemble, primarily driven by 1914 a 22% (18 to 25%) increase in ruminant animal numbers. The model ensemble projects agricultural 1915 crop yields to increase by 24% (17 to 46%), slowing but not preventing agricultural land expansion, 1916 which increases by 4% (1 to 7%; 16% for DIA-GIO) or ~200 Mha, with cropland expanding by 10% (4 to 1917 25%). Resource use for producing crops increases in BAU according to ensemble results, with blue 1918 water use increasing by 13% (-10 to 43%; 1% for DIA-GIO), suggesting uncertainty on future increases 1919 on water-use efficiency (i.e., adoption of irrigation technologies, planting of more water efficient 1920 crops) and rates of expansion of irrigated area to 2050. Nitrogen and phosphorous application 1921 increasing by 41% (22 to 55%; 31% for DIA-GIO) and 41% (36 to 46%; 26% for DIA-GIO) respectively. 1922 BAU results suggest food systems could exceed the resource-use boundaries by more than 100% for 1923 nitrogen-use and by 55-75% for land, water, and phosphorous use (Figure 10b). 1924 1925 All three components of EL (dietary change, increased productivity, reduced FLW) contribute to more 1926 efficient and less resource-intensive food systems by 2050. To achieve the agricultural emissions target -1 1927 of 5 GtCO2e yr from CH4 and N2O from biological processes, non-CO2 emissions at the farm-gate would -1 172 1928 need to decrease by more than 30% from current estimates of 7.1 GtCO2e yr. Under EL, agricultural 1929 non-CO2 emissions decline, primarily due to fewer ruminants, with the ensemble median and DIA-GIO 1930 showing a 20% and 32% decline compared to 2020 respectively. However, there is low agreement 1931 across models on whether these reductions are sufficient to meet the food system boundary (â€“48 to 1932 13% from 2020 across models). All regions show emissions reductions in EL compared to BAU by 2050, 1933 with the largest decreases occurring in Sub-Saharan Africa, Brazil, and the rest of Latin America (Figure 1934 A4.6). When EL is coupled with emissions pricing and land-use policies in ELM, the model ensemble 1935 shows agricultural emissions declining further to 34% (-84 to -18%) of 2020 levels, and below the food 1936 system boundary. 1937 1938 Ensemble results suggest total global agricultural land use (cropland + grazing land) could fall by 7% (- 1939 26% to 1%) below 2020 levels (decrease of ~340 Mha) in EL, a substantial departure from BAU projections described above. Most of this decline comes from reductions in grazing land (-11%, -59% to 1%). Latin America accounts for 30% of the decline in grazing land from 2020 (with Brazil alone

1942 accounting for 16%), while Sub-Saharan Africa accounts for 22%. Such a reduction in future agricultural 1943 land-use could provide room for conservation or restoration of natural ecosystems, particularly in 1944 these regions. The ensemble results suggest an increase in cropland (10%, -3% to 42%) from 2020, 1945 whereas DIA-GIO suggests that cropland area could decrease to 22% below 2020 levels without other 1946 uses of land (Figure A4.9). Coupling ambiï؟½ous mitigation with a shift to healthy diets under ELM shows 1947 even greater reductions in future agricultural area, with agricultural land declining globally by 14% (- 1948 28 to -4%) compared to 2020. This larger reducï؟½on is due to both drivers reducing the demand for and 1949 producï؟½on of animal products, and in parï؟½cular ruminant producï؟½on, the main users of grazing land. 1950 1951 Current estimates indicate that global blue water use remains below the food system boundary. 1952 However, regional water scarcity remains a critical concern, and addiï؟½onal efforts may be needed to 1953 increase water use efficiency and manage water-use, particularly in areas already experiencing water 1954 stress (e.g., Indo-Gangeï؟½c plains, Mediterranean, etc.). EL lowers water use compared to BAU 2050 1955 levels, but there is low agreement across the ensemble in terms of whether this reduction is sufficient 1956 to fall below 2020 levels (4%, -25 to 39%; -8% for DIA-GIO). Ensemble results suggest similar blue water 1957 use in ELM compared to EL (3%, -40 to 45%), but with results showing a wider range of outcomes. 1958 While EL reduces both N- and P- use compared to BAU, usage levels are sï؟½ll projected to increase 1959 compared to 2020 (15%, -17 to 32%; and 15%, 5 to 25%, respecï؟½vely), and remain above the planetary 1960 boundaries. DIA-GIO results suggest that under EL, nitrogen and phosphorus inputs could decline 1961 substanï؟½ally compared to current levels (-35 and -53%, respecï؟½vely) and near or below planetary 1962 boundaries, when demand from non-food sectors does not increase. 1963 1964 There are important complementariï؟½es across the three drivers of EL, which in combinaï؟½on achieved 366 1965 beï؟½er environmental outcomes than any individual driver in isolaï؟½on. Dietary change alone could 1966 substanï؟½ally contribute to improvements along land and emissions boundaries, but there is greater 1967 uncertainty on the outcome across water, N- and P-use by 2050, as many commodiï؟½es growing in 1968 demand also require these inputs. DIA-GIO showed greater environmental gains from increased 1969 producï؟½vity and reduced FLW, than did the economic models, where rebound effects in demand limit 1970 the environmental gains from these drivers. For example, increased producï؟½vity in ruminant 1971 producï؟½on lowers the cost of ruminant products, which in turn leads to lower consumer prices and 1972 sï؟½mulates increased consumer demand. This increase in demand leads to a larger ruminant herd 1973 leading to similar emissions under the increased producï؟½vity scenario compared to BAU (Fig 10b). A 1974 shi to healthy diets would avoid this outcome as it would reduce demand for ruminant products. A 1975 shi to healthy diets, on the other hand, would increase demand for more perishable fruits and 1976 vegetables and could increase the volume of FLW, highlighï؟½ng the increased importance of 1977 intervenï؟½ons to reduce FLW in an EL future. 1978 1979 The range of environmental results (cropland extent, and blue water, N, and P use) highlights 1980 substanï؟½al uncertainty in how agri-food systems could respond to a food system transformaï؟½on. A 1981 transiï؟½on to a healthy diet would include reducï؟½ons in demand for some agricultural commodiï؟½es 1982 (e.g., animal-sourced foods, and sugar), accompanied by reducï؟½ons in intermediate demand from 1983 these sectors for inputs (e.g., animal feed). However, this transiï؟½on would also increase the demand 1984 for other agricultural commodiï؟½es (e.g., fruits, vegetables, legumes, and nuts).

1986 Whether this transiï؟½on would lead to a net reducï؟½on in resource use by agriculture depends on a 1987 variety of factors. First is whether resources (cropland, water, ferï؟½lizers) dedicated to producing crops 1988 no longer demanded can easily be converted to producing agricultural commodiï؟½es that are increasing 1989 in demand. For example, not all cropland dedicated to feed crops would be suitable (agro-climaï؟½cally 1990 or economically) to produce fruits, vegetables, legumes, and nuts. Second, are the crops increasing in 1991 demand less resource intensive than those that are declining in demand. While animal-sourced foods 1992 are generally resource intensive, many of the crops increasing in demand can also be relaï؟½vely 1993 resource intensive, parï؟½cularly with respect to water and ferï؟½lizer use. Third, many crops that are 1994 important animal feeds (e.g., cereals and oilseeds) are also inputs to a variety of industrial uses (e.g., 1995 cosmeï؟½cs, bioplasï؟½cs, biofuels, etc.). Less demand for animal feeds would make these agricultural 1996 commodiï؟½es cheaper, which could spur addiï؟½onal demand from outside the food system, limiï؟½ng 1997 environmental gains. Models with larger reducï؟½ons in resource use (i.e. DIA-GIO), assume limited 1998 rebounds in demand and less compeï؟½ï؟½on for reallocaï؟½ng resources to producing a healthy diet, 1999 whereas many of the economic models suggest there could be a wider range of potenï؟½al uses for 2000 resources that could lead to smaller environmental gains without addiï؟½onal economic incenï؟½ves (e.g., 2001 true cost accounï؟½ng) or policies (land-use regulaï؟½on). Potenï؟½al increased compeï؟½ï؟½on for resource 2002 use is more evident in ELM, where there is increased demand for agricultural products from the 2003 bioeconomy (i.e., biofuels, biomass, bioplastics) as a part of transitioning away from fossil fuels, and 2004 land-use policies that restricts land expansion leading to increased agricultural intensification of 2005 existing cropland. 2006 2007 This suggests that the modelled changes could contribute substantially to reducing environmental 2008 pressure across many dimensions compared to remaining on a BAU trajectory. However, there is 2009 uncertainty with respect to reducing environmental impact compared to today or fully remaining 2010 within a safe operating space. Much of this uncertainty depends on how broad the scope is for 2011 alternaï؟½ve uses for natural resources and agricultural commodiï؟½es, emphasising the need to couple 2012 supply- and demand-side changes to avoid undesired consequences and rebound effects. The model 2013 results present a criï؟½cal foundaï؟½on for a food system transformaï؟½on, even as additional policies and 367,368 2014 innovations that promote improved food system efficiency and resource use (e.g., precision ag, 2015 fertilizer management, improved producï؟½on allocaï؟½on), further reductions in FLW, applications of 369â€“371 2016 sustainable intensification approaches (Panels 5 and 6 and Appendix 2), or circularity (Panel 4) 2017 may be required to ensure a complete transiï؟½on to a safe operaï؟½ng space. 2018 2019 2020

These results are 2050 consistent with recent analyses using current sectoral and regional labour requirements, which found 375 2051 a shift to a healthy diet would lead to a less labour-intensive global food system. All of which 2052 suggests that a shift to a healthy diet would not only be less resource-intensive, as highlighted in the 2053 previous secï؟½on; but also modestly less labour-intensive to produce. The global reducï؟½on in labour 2054 intensity is driven by various factors, including a reducï؟½on in the producï؟½on of animal feeds, and a 2055 reducï؟½on in output from more labour-intensive sectors like industrial pork, poultry, and dairy 2056 producï؟½on offseï؟½ng increased labour demand in expanding crop sectors like vegetable, fruits and 2057 nuts. 2058 2059 Livestock sectors would face parï؟½cular economic challenges, with the livestock share of total 2060 agricultural value of producï؟½on declining from ~40% in 2020 to around 15% by 2050 in EL and ELM 2061 (Figure 11b). This is due to a 41% decline in global producï؟½on, coupled with a 31% decline in producer 376 2062 prices for livestock products, which contributes to a 36% reducï؟½on in livestock labour demand by 2063 2050 compared to BAU. The increased demand for some plant-based foods, especially vegetables, 2064 fruits, and nuts, would drive increased demand for labour in these expanding sectors, which globally 2065 across all of agriculture offset most of the declines in livestock labour demand. These ensemble results 2066 are broadly consistent with DIA-GIO results, which suggest the shi s in sectoral demand for labour 2067 would result in a modest reducï؟½on in overall agricultural labour demand (<5%) globally. 2068 2069 The agricultural sector contributes a larger share of GDP and employment in lower-income regions (up 377 2070 to 40%) compared to higher-income regions (<5%). Despite a decline in the contribuï؟½on of 2071 agriculture to the economy compared to current levels, by 2050 agriculture would remain a relatively 2072 large source of GDP and employment in many regions (e.g., India, and Sub-Saharan Africa). The labour 2073 demands by sector also vary substantially across region and food system. As such, the challenges of 378 2074 restructuring agriculture and food sectors would vary regionally. The restructuring of food systems 2075 could be relatively more challenging in regions with large agricultural labour forces (e.g., India, Sub- 2076 Saharan Africa, and China), or large livestock sectors (e.g., Latin America). Regions with larger 2077 vegetable, fruit and nut sectors (e.g., China), may benefit from changes in demand without the need 2078 for substantial reallocation of resources across agricultural sectors. P68 (Figure 5).

The meat and dairy sectors are the most impacted by shi s to healthy diets, which must criï؟½cally take into account livelihoods, culture, and power dynamics. While we recognise the contenï؟½ous and complex nature of animal-sourced foods producï؟½on and consumpï؟½on, a smaller livestock sector creates opportuniï؟½es to give greater consideraï؟½on to animal health and wellbeing, environmental outcomes, and labour quality. We flag six important opportuniï؟½es and consideraï؟½ons to navigate this transiï؟½on which we recommend considering in combinaï؟½on, rather than in isolaï؟½on:

Gains through Efficiency and Technology: There are significant efficiency gains in producï؟½on, notably in the global south, that can markedly reduce anï؟½cipated environmental and health impacts of producï؟½on. Emissions intensity can be improved so that per unit producï؟½on, less GHG would be emiï؟½ed. P69 Cauï؟½on is urged around densely occupied confined systems notably around other environmental effects such as concentrated nutrient polluï؟½on, and disease risk, over-use of anï؟½microbials, animal health and welfare and labour condiï؟½ons. The assumed conservaï؟½on gains of improved efficiency are only aï؟½ained with complementary policies and acï؟½ons that limit, for example, conï؟½nued expansion into intact lands.

Zero Conversion of Intact Forests, and Grasslands: The addiï؟½onal conversion of forests for pasture or feed systems is antagonisï؟½c to achieve both human and environmental goals. O en forgoï؟½en are intact grasslands, which are even more threatened than forest globally. Only 30% of intact grasslands are remaining, making them the most converted, but also least protected ecosystems globally. P70

Gains through Nature Posiï؟½ve Rangeland Management: While intensive (over)grazing contributes to land degradaï؟½on, P71,P72 integraï؟½ng grazing livestock to maintain grasslands is also a possibility for conserving and restoring grassland ecosystems P73 including carbon sequestraï؟½on. Bai et al. esï؟½mate increases in SOC of 2.3 to 7.3 GtCO2e year âˆ’1 for grassland restoraï؟½on in general, with Improved grazing management providing 0.15 to 0.7 GTCO2e year âˆ’1 . P74 Improving grazing management ranges from complete removal of grazing livestock to different ways of reducing grazing intensity, highlighï؟½ng the need to balance the management with the agroecosystem condiï؟½ons. P73 Importantly, the producï؟½on of supplementary feeds needs to be internalised into the overall effects of the livestock producï؟½on systems, due to e.g. deforestaï؟½on. P75 In forest biomes, silvo-pastoral systems present some opportunity to miï؟½gate effects of livestock while contribuï؟½ng to producï؟½vity gains. P76

Gains Through Integraï؟½on: Modern agriculture has o en separated animal and plant producï؟½ons systems. Methods reconnecï؟½ng these systems, whether integrated in landscapes or connected through circular producï؟½on such as insect-based feed offer promise. P77-79 Feeding livestock with byproducts in circular systems has potenï؟½al, but the number of animals that could be reared is limited by the availability of such feed, resulï؟½ng in substanï؟½ally lower producï؟½on levels than what is currently produced. P79

Gains in Animal Welfare: Perspecï؟½ves emphasising the welfare and wellbeing of animals in agriculture have risen due to a growing concern related to intensive, confined, industrialised farming systems, or â€کfactory farmingâ€™. For instance high stocking densiï؟½es, P81,P82 long transportaï؟½on ï؟½mes, P83 restricï؟½ng natural behaviour P84 pose animal welfare concerns, and also extensive systems come with

P85 their own challenges. Animal welfare concerns have also been shown to influence consumer P86 choice, along with the produce quality, even outranking environmental concerns. Importantly, discussion on animal welfare and wellbeing should reflect current, mulï؟½disciplinary knowledge, bringing together advancing research on animal cogniï؟½on and behaviour, veterinary science and P87 ethics.

Non-ruminant Animal Sourced Foods: In some regions beef consumpï؟½on is flat or declining (e.g. USA, Europe), largely replaced by poultry and pork producï؟½on. Blue foods, or aquaï؟½c foods are common to many cultures and tradiï؟½ons. In general, animal sourced foods will have higher environmental footprints than plant sourced foods because of lost energy conversion between trophic levels. This loss is lowest for aquaï؟½c and avian animal sourced foods and can be further miï؟½gated by more circular systems. However, most livestock producï؟½on systems share similar disease, and polluï؟½on risks at scale, notably in dense highly confined systems and all have concerns related to animal health and welfare. 2102 2103 2104 Assessing production costs and food expenditure implications 2105 Using various modelling approaches, we observe a smaller and less resource intensive food system 2106 with EL compared to BAU. It is one that would require fewer natural resources (i.e., land and water), 2107 chemical (N and P), and labour inputs. Across the ensemble, these changes could amount to a 2108 moderate reduction in average agricultural producer prices of 8% for EL by 2050 compared to BAU 2109 (Figure 12a). Assuming current input requirements and prices, DIA-GIO projected a similar reduction 2110 in costs (10%). This suggests that transformed food systems by 2050 could produce healthy diets for 2111 all while reducing input costs, natural resource use, as well as environmental pressures compared to 2112 BAU. However, whether this potential is realized will ultimately depend on the transiï؟½on pathway (i.e., 2113 the mix of policies, technologies, and cultural changes) followed to foster an EL compaï؟½ble food system 2114 transformaï؟½on. 2115 2116 Across food systems, lower production costs could increase average food affordability if passed along 2117 to consumers. That said, it is important to note that lower producer prices can contribute to lower 2118 agricultural wages and income with negative consequences on food affordability, particularly in rural 2119 areas. Ensemble results suggest that average agricultural prices would not change substantially from 2120 2020 levels for both BAU and EL. This contrasts with ELM, where increases in producer prices 2121 contribute to a 10% increase in consumer prices compared to 2020. Food expenditure (reported by 2122 AIM, ENVISAGE, and MAGNET), which is a factor of consumer prices and food demand, is projected to 2123 grow in all three scenarios, but at a slower rate than per capita income, with the share of global income 2124 spent on food falling from 7% to 5%, 4%, and 4% by 2050 for BAU, EL, and ELM respectively (Figure 2125 12b). Globally, changes in food expenditure between BAU and EL are driven by two offsetting shifts in 2126 the consumption bundle. First, a decline in the demand for, and the prices of, relatively more expensive 2127 animal sourced foods. For example, demand for, and consumer prices of, ruminant meat fall by 62% 2128 and 16% respectively compared to 2020, even as ruminant prices remain substantially higher than 2129 prices of all other commodities (Figure 12a). Second, demand for and prices of VFN increase 2130 substantially, which at the global level mostly offset decreased spending on animal products.

2132 While globally, by 2050, these two shifts mostly offset each other, some regions currently consuming 2133 relatively low levels of animal sourced foods (e.g., India), or would require relatively large increases in 2134 VFN demand (e.g., Eastern Europe, Caucasus, and Central Asia) to achieve a healthy diet could see 2135 increased food expenditure in 2050 compared to BAU. By contrast, regions that are currently 2136 overconsuming animal sourced foods (e.g. USA) could see declining food expenditures. This result is 2137 consistent with recent analysis using DIA-GIO that found that for individual consumers today, healthy 2138 diets could already be more affordable than current unhealthy diets in high- and upper middle-income 2139 countries, even as they remain more expensive in low- and lower middle-income countries8. 2140 2141 Even as the share of income spent on food is declining for all regions and scenarios compared to 2020, 2142 the affordability of diverse healthy diets remains a concern. Lower income regions that import a 2143 substantial share of their VFN (e.g., Other Asia), could have additional concerns for increased 2144 vulnerability to international price shocks on domestic food affordability and availability. And while 2145 progress is made, Sub-Saharan Africa continues to spend a larger share of income (21%) on food in EL 2146 than any other region. This suggests lower income regions (bottom of list of regions in Figure 12b) 2147 could remain relatively more vulnerable to food poverty, and that targeted interventions to account 2148 for higher VFN prices and social protections to ensure consumers can afford a healthy diet may be 2149 needed. 2150 2151 These results highlight that a food systems transformation, if achieved, could contribute towards social 2152 and environmental goals. However, the mechanisms for getting there (e.g., taxes, repurposing of 2153 subsidies, incorporation of true cost of food approaches) will be critical to informing and incentivizing 2154 behaviour change towards more sustainable production practices and healthier consumption patterns. 2155 The cost of these actions will ultimately determine whether a food system transformation increases or 2156 decreases the cost of individual foods and the overall diet. 2157

Median GDP per capita for 3 reporting models (AIM, ENVISAGE, and MAGNET) is located at 2165 the region's first row followed by food expenditure results in the second row, which are expressed as percentage 2166 change from BAU 2020. Sectors: AGR = all agricultural products, RUM = ruminant meat, NRM = non-ruminant 2167 meat (poultry, pork, eggs), DRY = dairy, VFN = vegetables, fruits, and nuts (and legumes, roots and tubers), OSD 2168 = oilseeds, SGC = sugar crops, RIC = rice, WHT = wheat, CGR = coarse grains. Regions: WLD = World, ANZ = 2169 Australia and New Zealand; BRA = Brazil; CAN = Canada; CHN = China; EUR = Europe; ECC = Eastern Europe, 2170 Caucasus, Central Asia; IND = India; MEN = Middle East and North Africa; OAS = Other Asia; OSA = Other South 2171 and Central America; SEA = Southeast Asia; SSA = Sub-Saharan Africa; USA = United States of America. 2172 2173 Mitigation efforts (Figure A4.7) could increase the cost of agricultural production by limiting land 2174 supply and pricing emissions. Across the ensemble, mitigation efforts in isolation would increase 2175 consumer prices by 17% (0 to 40%) compared to BAU 2050 prices. This is broadly consistent with past 349 2176 studies suggesting that mitigation efforts could increase food prices. This increased risk can be 2177 explained by multiple factors that raise the price of producing and consuming food. First, land use and 2178 mitigation policies increase the cost of key agricultural inputs (e.g., land, fertilizers) raising the cost of 2179 production. Second, emissions pricing raises the cost of agricultural products, particularly of emission 2180 intensive commodities like ruminant meat. Vulnerability to changes in food prices varies, with lower 2181 income regions already at higher risk of food insecurity, like Sub-Saharan Africa, the most vulnerable 2182 to price increases. Panel 9 explores how the EL components contribute to changes in producer and consumer prices, and their potential policy implications.

2185 Low- and middle-income regions contribute the largest reductions in emissions and agricultural land- 2186 use in EL and ELM (e.g., China, Brazil, Sub-Saharan Africa), and predominantly are regions where 2187 dietary change risks increasing food prices. These are also the regions that historically have 2188 experienced large changes in land-use or had the least access to novel technologies and affordable 2189 reliable energy. This highlights the importance of increased productivity to reduce agricultural yield 2190 gaps, as well as reducing FLW, both of which could play a critical role in alleviating price pressures 2191 caused by a shift to healthy diets and/or ambitious mitigation. There are potential complementarities 2192 between a shift to a healthy diet and ambitious mitigation, both alleviates pressure on land, and 2193 globally, reduces GHG-intensive foods in the consumption bundle. Further demand-side interventions 2194 could also play a role in managing food affordability concerns. Modelling suggests that targeted food 2195 subsidies bundled with miï؟½gaï؟½on policies 379,380 or full cost efforts381 could help manage these price 2196 increases, as discussed in the following section. 2197 Panel 9: Understanding the drivers of price changes to inform policy priorities To assess which scenario drivers (dietary change, increased producï؟½vity, reduced food loss and waste, and ambiï؟½ous miï؟½gaï؟½on) were most responsible for changes in agricultural prices we conducted a decomposiï؟½on analysis using four models from the ensemble (AIM, CAPRI, ENVISAGE, and MAGNET) that reported regional consumer and producer (e.g., farm gate) prices (See Appendix 4 Fig A4.7 for full ensemble results of producer prices).

Ambiï؟½ous miï؟½gaï؟½on could increase the cost of producï؟½on in all regions by limiï؟½ng agricultural land expansion and pricing emissions. Increased producer prices translate to higher consumer prices, posing a risk to food security. Dietary change on the other hand has more varied impact on prices. In Brazil and the USA, regions with high animal-sourced food consumpï؟½on, adopï؟½on of healthy diets could reduce average consumer prices by 7% and 4% respecï؟½vely (Panel figure g and h, yellow bar). However, in India, where there is low red meat consumpï؟½on, increased demand for vegetables, fruits, and nuts could contribute to a 10% increase in average consumer prices (Panel figure e, yellow bar)

Increased producï؟½vity and reduced FLW contribute to more efficient food systems, reducing the cost of producï؟½on and leading to lower consumer prices in all regions. The potenï؟½al to miï؟½gate price increases is parï؟½cularly criï؟½cal in lower income regions with greater food security challenges. For example, in India and Sub-Saharan Africa reduced FLW and increased producï؟½vity parï؟½ally offset the price increases due to ambiï؟½ous miï؟½gaï؟½on and dietary change (Panel figure blue and red bars). FLW reducï؟½ons contribute more to reducï؟½ons in consumer prices than producer prices, with the biggest gains in regions with relaï؟½vely high consumer waste (e.g., USA Panel figure h). Broadly, these results suggest the importance of supply-side intervenï؟½ons that increase the efficiency of food systems, parï؟½cularly in low-income seï؟½ngs where price rises pose immediate food security risks.

Previous modelling P88â€“91 has suggested supply-side measures (investments in resource use efficiency, agricultural R&D, improved extension services), would be insufficient to alleviate food insecurity. As such, policies and intervenï؟½ons that improve infrastructure and funcï؟½oning of markets, as well as demand-side intervenï؟½ons, such as price subsidies and poverty alleviaï؟½ons measures, would be needed to manage higher diet costs in lower income regions.

Panel figure: The key drivers of price changes in ELM. Regional impact of ELM (purple) on producer (a-d) and consumer (e-h) prices as percent change (%) from the business-as-usual (BAU) scenario, decomposed into four key drivers shown as percentage points (%p): miï؟½gaï؟½on (green), diet shi (yellow), improved producï؟½vity (blue), and reduced food loss and waste (FLW) (red).
