2202 sustainability, and justice 2203 1 2204 A â€œgreat food transformationâ€‌ is required if the world is to align with the EAT-Lancet's vision by 2050. 2205 This transformation must operate across multiple leverage points, including profound shifts in the 382,383 2206 underlying objecï؟½ves of food systems outcomes. The focus must move beyond simply maximizing 2207 profit and volume in agriculture to achieving food security in ways that prioriï؟½ze diet quality and 2208 advance health, sustainability, and social justice. Equally essenï؟½al is intervenï؟½ons that change 382,383 2209 underlying rules and incenï؟½ves of the food system, such as taxes, subsidies, and regulations, as 2210 these form the condiï؟½ons under which deeper shifts in values, norms, and governance can emerge. 2211 2212 This section focuses on these underlying rules and incentives through the lens of eight domain-specific 2213 solutions aimed at advancing health, environment, and/or justice goals simultaneously. For every 2214 solution, we identify two to three actions that the Commission has evaluated, based on synthesis of 2215 exisï؟½ng literature, as critical for transforming food systems. These acï؟½ons are grounded in available 2216 scientific evidence demonstraï؟½ng their potenï؟½al to deliver meaningful impacts across health, 2217 environment, and jusï؟½ce outcomes, while also creaï؟½ng the enabling environment for more profound 2218 systems change. Figure 13 summarizes these solutions and their associated actions. 2219 2220 Several analyses have shown that individual policies and interventions, on their own, are insufficient 2221 to drive multi-goal transformative change. Instead, bundled or integrated approaches are neededâ€” 2222 for example, to keep global warming below 1.5 degrees384 or to reduce the hidden costs of current 9 2223 food systems (estimated to be 15 trillion US$/year). This is because the drivers and outcomes of food 385 2224 systems are highly interconnected, interacting in both direct and indirect ways. Investing in 2225 coordinated sets or packages of solutions is therefore essenï؟½al to steer food systems transformation 2226 in positive directions. 2227 2228 We highlight some of these dynamics in actions 3 and 15-23. The domain-specific actions presented in 2229 this secï؟½on are not isolated; rather, they are deeply interconnected and can produce synergistic effects 2230 across goals, while also navigaï؟½ng potenï؟½al trade-offs. For example, introducing regulations to limit 2231 land use expansion may promote health and environmental sustainability but could risk undermining 2232 justice if it restricts access for marginalized or indigenous peopleâ€™s access to that land. However, if such 2233 regulations are implemented alongside efforts that recognise and protect the resource use of these 2234 groups, both sustainability and justice can be enhanced. By implementing actions as part of carefully 2235 designed 'bundles,â€™ it becomes possible to advance multiple goals simultaneously (see Section 6: 2236 Transformation). 2237 2238

## Goal 1: Achieve the Planetary Health Diet for All

### Soluï؟½on 1: Shi to healthy diets

To shi diets towards The Planetary Health Diet, it should ideally be more available, affordable, convenient, aspiraï؟½onal, and delicious than unhealthy alternaï؟½ves. Two sets of feasible intervenï؟½ons emerged from a scoping review of systemaï؟½c reviews. 386 Both sets of intervenï؟½ons focus on food environmentsâ€”the physical, economic, poliï؟½cal, and sociocultural seï؟½ngs in which people engage with the food system to acquire food387 â€”most o en considered more cost-effecï؟½ve and equitable than those targeï؟½ng individuals or households. 387â€“389 The first set includes price-based intervenï؟½ons, which are likely to reduce the purchase and consumpï؟½on of unhealthy foods. 390â€“392 The second set involves non-price-based intervenï؟½ons that restrict adverï؟½sing and availability of unhealthy foods and mandate warning labels on them.

Taxes on foods and beverages with high content of added sugar, salt, or saturated fats (or a combinaï؟½on thereof) are parï؟½cularly impacï؟½ul when combined with subsidies on nutriï؟½ous foods that decrease their relaï؟½ve price. 392,393 Modelling studies suggest opï؟½mal price levels for miï؟½gaï؟½ng health and environmental impacts of unhealthy foods and meat that would require calibraï؟½on by context. 379,380 By contrast, restricï؟½ng adverï؟½sing and markeï؟½ng of unhealthy foods could shi populaï؟½ons towards healthier foods. 394,395 Labelling foods to denote higher energy content and those high in fat, sugar, and salt decreases purchasing and consumpï؟½on 396,397 using either mandatory front- of-pack warning labels as in several Laï؟½n American countries or traffic light systems as is common in Europe. 398 Reducing the proporï؟½on of unhealthy foods that are available in the market in parallel with increasing the proporï؟½on of healthier foods 399,400 can reduce purchasing and consumpï؟½on, as can reducing porï؟½on, package, and serving sizes of energy dense and unhealthier foods. 401,402

Affordability of food is determined by supply and demand side dynamics as well as by purchasing power. 8,287 When making the PHD affordable for all, increasing purchasing power for lower income consumers is more important than â€کreducing the priceâ€™ of foods. 403 Social protecï؟½on measures (see also Soluï؟½on 8) including, providing nutriï؟½ous school meals (Panel 11), public procurement and distribuï؟½on systems, employment guarantee schemes, and government investments in health- promoï؟½ng insurance systems, all have the potenï؟½al to contribute to increased affordability for low- income households. Living wages, universal basic income, income growth, and targeted income transfers, especially for economically disadvantaged people, 404 and more equitable distribuï؟½on of the benefits of naï؟½onal economic growth through fiscal policies, would also support increased purchasing power. Previous modelling studies suggest that the relaï؟½ve cost of a healthy diet can be substanï؟½ally reduced, even in LMICs, through combinaï؟½ons of pro-poor income growth, reduced food loss and waste, and a cost accounï؟½ng that includes the cost of diet related climate and health. 8

## Goal 2: Produce the Planetary Health Diet within Planetary Boundaries

### Solution 3: Implement sustainable and ecological intensification

In regions characterized by significant yield gaps, policies aimed at increasing productivity through sustainable and ecological intensification (SEI) practices can increase resource use efficiency by lowering the environmental impact per unit of production. 417 Conversely, in areas where agricultural productivity is already high, policy efforts should prioritize reducing environmental degradation and pollution associated with intensive farming systems and practices.

Securing long-term access to land and water resources is essential to enabling farmers to adopt and implement SEI practices, many of which entail substanï؟½al up-front costs and/or require longer time horizons to yield benefitsâ€”particularly when used to rehabilitate or recover exhausted or degraded lands. 418 Access to land is especially criï؟½cal for women, who frequently face additional and entrenched legal and sociocultural barriers to ownership and control. 419 Despite their pivotal role in supporï؟½ng marginal groups and remote communiï؟½es, public advisory (extension) services have suffered from chronic underfunding. 420 Revitalizing and redesigning these services, with coordinated support from public, private and civil society actors, is essenï؟½al to assist farmers in transitioning and adopting new methods towards sustainable pracï؟½ces. 420 Strategic investments in farmer networksâ€”facilitating knowledge exchange, equipment sharing, and improved market accessâ€”can also lower adoption barriers. Brazil's success in promoï؟½ng diversified and organic farming through the integraï؟½on of farmer networks with supporï؟½ve public policies offers a compelling model.

2332 2333 2334 2335 2336 2337 2338 2339 2340 2341 2342 2343 2344 2345 2346 2347 2348 2349 2350 2351 2352 2353 2354 2355 2356 2357 2358 2359 2360 2361 2362 2363 2364 2365 2366 2367 2368 2369 2370 2371 2372 2373 2374

Addressing the structural imbalances between producers and dominant agribusiness corporations is critical. Strengthening anti-trust legislation and implementing policies to reduce excessive market concentration can reduce systemic barriers to SEI adoption and practices. 421 Robust competition policies can foster more equitable pricing for producers, support diversified market structures, and stimulate innovation, 14 all while contributing to the resilience of food systems. 422,423 In parallel, tighter regulation of food commodity speculation could stabilise producer incomes and create more favourable conditions for long-term investments. 424

Both public and private sector investments are needed to support the transition to SEI practices, parï؟½cularly in offseï؟½ng iniï؟½al costs to advance the research and development of innovations and appropriate technologies. 425 Investment in labour-saving technologies can reduce the burden of otherwise labour-intensive practices, while crop breeding for high-yielding or climate resilient varieï؟½es suited to low chemical input condiï؟½ons can improve the productivity and profitability for SEI. Financial mechanisms such as payments for ecosystem services, 426 cost-share payments, 421 and similar instruments could provide crucial support to farmers shifting towards sustainable pracï؟½ces. Historically, most agricultural subsidies have been directed toward a narrow set of staple cropsâ€” wheat, rice, maize, soy (for feed), and dairy. 417 To realign agricultural producï؟½on to dietary recommendaï؟½ons that emphasize increased consumpï؟½on of legumes, nuts, fruits, and vegetables, subsidies must reform. 427 Targeted investment in under-supported foods, alongside research to improve their yield, resilience, and regional adaptability, is essenï؟½al to building more sustainable, health promoï؟½ng agriculture systems.

### Solution 4: Halt agricultural conversion of intact ecosystems

The multi-model ensemble results indicate a projected 7% decline in agricultural land by 2050 relaï؟½ve to 2020 driven by dietary shifts, increased agricultural productivity, and reductions in food loss and waste. However, to limit the expansion of agriculture and aquatic systems into intact systems, a combination of regulatory policies, community management, and multi-sectoral commitments and coordinaï؟½on are necessary. This may include the strict protection of natural ecosystems, and the suspension or limiï؟½ng of logging concessions and commercial fishing licenses in protected areas. It is essenï؟½al that these regulations are implemented in ways that minimise leakage, i.e. ensuring that the protection of land and water in one region does not inadvertently result in increased exploitaï؟½on elsewhere. 428 Aï؟½enï؟½on should be given to populaï؟½ons inhabiï؟½ng or using resources in protected areas. This includes ensuring meaningful stakeholder engagement and where appropriate, empowering leadership by Indigenous communiï؟½es. 429 Recognizing and securing land and sea tenure rights for these communiï؟½es is essenï؟½al. Retaining biodiversity intactness can also be compatible with certain forms of sustainable land/water use, such as wild harvesting, selecï؟½ve logging with native species, or extensive rangeland management that maintains naï؟½ve biodiversity. In such cases, sustainable uses are those that are well below the 10% Human Appropriation of Net Primary Productivity (Table 2), and where biodiversity intactness index values stay well above 95, thereby minimizing impact on species composition. 157

Both regional policies and voluntary iniï؟½aï؟½ves in the EU and the UK have introduced zero deforestation commitments across supply chains. While a few region- and commodity-specific commitments have contributed to small reductions in deforestation, the evidence of effectiveness of these efforts is

435 2381 2382 Solution 5: Reduce food loss and waste 2383 The ensemble scenario results indicate that halving food loss and waste (FLW), in alignment with SDG 2384 target 12.3, yields moderate benefits across all five modelled Planetary Boundaries. Some level of loss 2385 or waste is inevitable for food safety and reliability of supply. Food losses and waste can be reduced, 2386 so that there is less demand on food producï؟½on, or else recycled into the circular economy (see Panel 436 2387 4). The â€کFood Waste Pyramidâ€™ outlines a hierarchy of recycling management options based on 2388 financial and environmental returns: depending on context, the highest priority is usually to feed waste 2389 to livestock (though livestock numbers are reduced in one of the EAT-Lancet scenarios), followed by 2390 industrial usesâ€”including anaerobic digesï؟½on for biogas and other productsâ€”then composï؟½ng, 2391 incineraï؟½on, and finally, landfill disposal. 2392 2393 In lower-income contexts, food loss predominantly occurs at the producï؟½on and post-harvest stages, 437 2394 whereas in higher-income contexts, consumer-level food waste is the main contributor. Reducing 2395 food loss depends on improving storage, transportaï؟½on logistics, and access to labour and equipment 2396 to ensure ï؟½mely harvests and transformaï؟½on of crops into food products sought a er by the 2397 consumer. In some cases, shortening supply chains may reduce food loss by reducing the need for 2398 extended storage and distribuï؟½on. Circular economy opportunitiesâ€”such as the producï؟½on of low- 2399 cost high-quality animal feed, renewable energy usage, and improved food safetyâ€”can provide 438 2400 demonstrable benefits (see Panel 4). 2401 2402 To address food waste in households, food service, and retail, education iniï؟½aï؟½ves, awareness 2403 campaigns, and skill-building remain the primary institutional tools. Technical intervenï؟½ons focus on 2404 leveraging data and analytics to improve the efficiency of food supply chains. To raise effectiveness of 439 2405 these soft tools, they should be complemented by regulatory measures, taxes and subsidies. More 2406 systemic resolution of food waste must confront underlying drivers, insï؟½tuï؟½onal lock-ins, and rebound 440 2407 effects. For example, aestheï؟½c standards and preferences (e.g., size, colour, blemishes) imposed by 2408 markets contribute to supply chain losses and consumer waste. Similarly, current food safety laws and 2409 regulaï؟½ons, combined with exisï؟½ng investments in infrastructure, o en favour incineration or landfill 2410 over alternaï؟½ve uses such as feeding waste to animals. 2411 2412 Goal 3: Secure Social Foundations 2413 Solution 6: Secure decent work 2414 There are several measures that can be implemented to ensure that work within food systems is safe, 2415 dignified, and equitable without discrimination based on gender, race, or other forms of difference. 2416 Securing decent work requires, among other acï؟½ons, raising wages for food system workers to living 441 2417 wage levels4 and promoï؟½ng gender equitability across all roles and sectors. ### Solution 7: Ensure meaningful voice and representation

Securing meaningful voice and representation is criï؟½cal to ensuring that decision-making processes in food systems are more democratic and inclusive. Collective bargaining mechanisms, supported and implemented through unions and civil society organizaï؟½ons, can advocate effectively for the rights and interests of food systems workers and small-scale producers and actors. For policies concerning both production and consumption, the proactive engagement of diverse coalitions and perspecï؟½ves â€” including unions, civil society actors, and marginalised populationsâ€”can foster more democratic and participatory decision-making governance. 330 Although these processes may be slowed by disagreements and trade-offs, expert facilitation and the establishment of clear procedural guidelines can help ensure momentum and constructive progress. 330 Importantly, such inclusive platforms can contribute to improved food systems governance by enabling interaction among actors across sectors and scales, 446 while empowering local institutions and promoting decentralised decision-making authority. 447

Power imbalances similarly affect consumers, who often have limited influence over the terms of food access, affordability, and quality. 423 Ensuring consumer representation through regulatory mechanisms, participatory food policy councils, and consumer advocacy groups can help rebalance these asymmetries and strengthen individual and collective agency within food system governance. 448

Addiï؟½onal measures to democraï؟½ze food systems include the allocaï؟½on of reserved seats in policy forums for marginalised groups, 449 the insï؟½tuï؟½onalisaï؟½on of citizensâ€™ assemblies and consultations. 450 Efforts to counter corporate influence and concentraï؟½on should involve stricter regulaï؟½ons on political donations, mechanisms to mitigate conflicts of interest, and greater transparency in lobbying and the funding of scientific research. 14 Policies to address corporate concentraï؟½on should include strengthened naï؟½onal and internaï؟½onal compeï؟½ï؟½on frameworks, 14 alongside regulatory mechanisms to idenï؟½fy, monitor, and miï؟½gate undue corporate influence on policy and governance processes.

Deliberate efforts to build inclusive coalitions that include marginalised groups while also increasing transparency and accountability and expanding access to information can create new spaces for dialogue.

### Solution 8: Recognise and protect marginalised groups

Embedding justice into decision-making processes ensures that the responsibility for advocacy does not fall disproporï؟½onately on marginalised groups. The implementation of a comprehensive set of social protection policies can improve access to, and affordability of, healthy and sustainable diets. Social protection measuresâ€”including school meals (Panel 11), unconditional cash transfers, maternity entitlements, old age pension, and disability benefitsâ€”are broad-based intervenï؟½ons that directly or indirectly improve food security and nutriï؟½on. 4,451

2466 2467 2468 2469 2470 2471 2472 2473 2474 2475 2476 2477 2478 2479 2480 2481 2482 2483 2484 2485 2486 2487 2488 2489 2490 2491 2492 2493 2494 2495 2496 2497 2498 2499 2500 2501 2502

Addiï؟½onal strategies include targeï؟½ng financial support for women farmers, crop and livestock insurance to buffer against shocks, subsidies for farm inputs and technologies, and food price stabilisation mechanisms such as internationally coordinated food storage policies. 452 Ensuring that solutions are co-created with marginalised communiï؟½es can help create context-specific solutions that are socially acceptable. 453 Co-creation should be facilitated through improved representation, as previously discussed, and by engaging directly with affected groupsâ€”especially early in the transformation process. 454 This also requires sustained support, including funding for inclusive initiatives and training programs that build capacities within marginalised populaï؟½ons. Finally, NGOs play a vital role in safeguarding basic human rights for people in conflict-affected areas, and this role should be supported by multilateral organisations and naï؟½onal governments. Interventions may include establishing safe zones as a short-term measure, rebuilding food supply chains with internaï؟½onal assistance in the medium term, and creaï؟½ng dedicated funding mechanisms to prevent and alleviate famines in conflict areas over the longterm. 327

## Adopt responsible, equitable technology and innovation across solutions

While none of the solutions explicitly focus on emerging technologies, technological innovation is relevant across food system transformaï؟½on. Emerging technologiesâ€”such as artificial intelligence, cellular agriculture, nanotechnology, and roboticsâ€”have the potenï؟½al to significantly reshape and disrupt agriculture and food systems. 367,455 These innovaï؟½ons could enhance agricultural productivity without expanding land use, thereby contribuï؟½ng to reduced deforestation and habitat loss. They may also facilitate precision farming, reduce waste, and optimize the use of key inputs like water and fertilizers. 367

The effecï؟½veness of these technologies will depend on balanced and strategic investments that align their development, deployment, and use with principles of equity, responsible innovation, and coherent policy frameworks, 456,457 as well as the broader transition to renewable energy sources. Redirecting public and private funding toward near-ready technologies that support the solutions described in this report will be critical. Comprehensive evaluation of emerging technologiesâ€” considering their impacts across different geographical and cultural contextsâ€”is essenï؟½al to ensure they contribute to just food systems transformations. 458 This includes recognizing that technologies can have both positive and negative effects, many of which remain uncertainâ€”parï؟½cularly in the case of rapidly advancing tools like arï؟½ficial intelligence. To avoid and or miï؟½gate unintended environmental and social trade-offs, investments should be carefully targeted to ensure broad accessibility and fair distribuï؟½on of the technologies. 455,457 Among the most discussed technologies relevant to dietary shifts are novel alternatives to replacing convenï؟½onal meat. Panel 10 explores their potential implications for health, environment, and justice.

Panel 10: The potenï؟½al role of meat alternaï؟½ves in meeï؟½ng EAT-Lancet targets The Commission recommends a plant-rich diet with large shares of protein coming from minimally processed plant foods, such as legumes (Secï؟½on Healthy Diets). We also find that globally, and in most regions, consumpï؟½on of red meat and other meats is above healthy and sustainable levels. As consumer studies have revealed several barriers to replacing animal protein sources with tradiï؟½onal plant protein sources, including convenience, taste, and cultural values, P92 a range of meat alternaï؟½ves are emerging across several regions, parï؟½cularly in HICs. They include plant-based alternaï؟½ves (PBAs),

fermentaï؟½on-derived products (using microorganisms to produce protein-rich foods), and culï؟½vated meat from animal cells designed to replicate the sensory properï؟½es of animal-source foods. P92 Previous systemaï؟½c reviews P94,P95 and modelling studies P96,P97 suggest that replacing convenï؟½onal meat with these alternaï؟½ves could have both posiï؟½ve and negaï؟½ve implicaï؟½ons on health, environment, and jusï؟½ce. The potenï؟½al impacts are context-dependent and vary based on the specific ingredients, processing levels, and what they replace within the diet.

Environmental Implicaï؟½ons It is well established that whole high-protein plant foods and tradiï؟½onal products prepared from these foods (tofu, tempeh) have lower environmental impacts than animal foods. Meta-analyses and modelling studies indicate that PBAs can also have substanï؟½ally lower environmental impacts compared to meat, parï؟½cularly regarding greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and land use, but greater impacts than minimally processed plant foods. P94,P95,P97 Culï؟½vated meat and fermentaï؟½on-derived products are anï؟½cipated to have higher GHG emissions than PBAs and minimally processed plant foods, and lower to similar GHG emissions, land and water use compared to convenï؟½onal meat, with its impact reducï؟½on potenï؟½al being largely dependent on the transiï؟½on to renewable energy sources. P93,P98

Health Implicaï؟½ons Replacing red meat with legumes and other plant protein sources can provide several health benefits. The nutriï؟½onal performance of emerging meat alternaï؟½ves varies widely based on each productآ´s specific formulaï؟½on, levels of processing, nutriï؟½onal composiï؟½on and density, and what they are replacing in the diet. PBAs that contain substanï؟½al amounts of legumes, vegetables, and nuts are found to have favourable nutriï؟½onal profiles, P95 contribuï؟½ng to higher intake of fibre and folate, and lower saturated fat intake. P94,P97 However, some PBAs are highly processed, high in added sugar, salt, and saturated fat. Further research is required on the bioavailability of vitamins and minerals as well as the protein quality of PBAs and fermentaï؟½on-based alternaï؟½ves for a beï؟½er understanding of the nutriï؟½onal effects of integraï؟½ng them into diets. Culï؟½vated meat is expected to have a similar nutriï؟½onal profile as convenï؟½onal meat and might therefore be similarly associated with colorectal cancer and other diet-related diseases. P97 The recommended consumpï؟½on levels of processed meat alternaï؟½ves remain similar to those of convenï؟½onal meat in Table 1.

Jusï؟½ce Implicaï؟½ons There is less understanding of the role of emerging meat alternaï؟½ves on socio-economic consequences including food and nutriï؟½on security. Price is a key driver of purchase and consumpï؟½on. While unprocessed legumes are least expensive, PBAs are currently o en more expensive than the products they aim to replace. P97 Culï؟½vated meats are currently not widely available, but despite high uncertainï؟½es, the exisï؟½ng peer-reviewed assessments not related to industry interests suggest it will not be a cost-compeï؟½ï؟½ve product even with substanï؟½al investment and improved producï؟½on methods. P99â€“101 The alternaï؟½ve meat industry could have addiï؟½onal implicaï؟½ons for the labour market,

e.g., by generaï؟½ng and shi ing employment from rural areas to ciï؟½es.

2531 (1) establish context-specific bundles of acï؟½ons and policies; (2) set and track targets that enable 2532 collecï؟½ve acï؟½on and transparent accountability; (3) build coaliï؟½ons of diverse actors; (4) idenï؟½fy and 2533 address barriers to change; and (5) unlock financial resources for transformaï؟½on. These steps broadly 161,367,459,460 2534 align with prior work to develop implementaï؟½on roadmaps for food systems, climate, and 27 2535 biodiversity. In the secï؟½ons that follow, we elaborate on each step, highlighï؟½ng its raï؟½onale and the 2536 enabling condiï؟½ons for its success. 2537 2538 Establish context-specific bundles of acï؟½ons and policies 2539 All soluï؟½ons idenï؟½fied earlier (Figure 13) contribute to a specific goal (health, sustainability, or jusï؟½ce), 2540 with potenï؟½al posiï؟½ve or negaï؟½ve addiï؟½onal contribuï؟½ons to other goals. For example, fiscal policies 2541 aimed at promoï؟½ng healthy diets (Acï؟½on 1) can yield co-benefits for both public health and 2542 environmental sustainability. However, such policies may generate trade-offs with jusï؟½ce unless 2543 accompanied by public investments in social protecï؟½on that increase purchasing power for low- 2544 income households (Acï؟½on 3, 15 and 22-23). While these intervenï؟½ons may independently contribute 2545 to shi s in producï؟½on management and consumpï؟½on paï؟½erns toward more sustainable and equitable 2546 outcomes, their transformaï؟½ve potenï؟½al is fully realized only when implemented as contextually 2547 appropriate bundles of mutually reinforcing acï؟½ons. 2548 2549 Bundling is the implementaï؟½on of mulï؟½ple acï؟½ons simultaneously or in an intenï؟½onally staggered 2550 manner. We refer to bundles as â€کa coherent set of intervenï؟½ons designed explicitly to achieve a single 9 2551 or mulï؟½ple objecï؟½ves of interestâ€™. An example of an acï؟½on bundle is the UK iniï؟½aï؟½ve â€کA blue print to 2552 halve obesityâ€™ in five years, which comprises more than 30 intenï؟½onally integrated policies spanning 462 2553 different domains. Bundling has been proposed as a necessary condiï؟½on for food systems 27,461 2554 transformaï؟½on. 2555 2556 Idenï؟½fying and bundling acï؟½ons is an inherently social process that requires cooperaï؟½on and 461 2557 coordinaï؟½on beyond the implementaï؟½on of singular policy or technical measures. It extends beyond 2558 policy coherence by addressing both short- and long-term objecï؟½ves, demanding aï؟½enï؟½on to the 463 2559 governance of transiï؟½on. Resistance to change, o en mobilized by actors adversely affected by 461 2560 specific measures, can constrain implementaï؟½on. Systemaï؟½c bundling offers a means to miï؟½gate 27,464 2561 trade-offs between poliï؟½cal feasibility and policy effecï؟½veness. It can also increase the 2562 acceptability of measures by, for example, combining acï؟½ons that increase costs for consumers (e.g., 464 2563 taxes on meat), with those that offer discounts on low-emission food alternaï؟½ves. Integraï؟½ng social 2564 protecï؟½on mechanisms or retraining opportuniï؟½es with prioriï؟½zed intervenï؟½ons can help miï؟½gate 461 2565 resistance by addressing short-term vulnerabiliï؟½es associated with structural transiï؟½ons. For 2566 instance, combining acï؟½ons to shi dietary paï؟½erns (Soluï؟½on 1) and improve sustainable and 2567 ecological intensificaï؟½on of healthy food groups (Soluï؟½on 3) with the provision of training in skills to 2568 support redeployment (Acï؟½on 16) can support the broader goal of securing decent work (Soluï؟½on 6). 2569 465 2570 Well-sequenced and gradually enacted policies can generate posiï؟½ve ï؟½pping points, whereby early 2571 successful intervenï؟½ons build public trust and facilitate broader acceptance of subsequent reforms. In 2572 contexts where intervenï؟½ons are met with scepï؟½cism or bias, combining intervenï؟½ons with robust 2573 evaluaï؟½ons can foster support over ï؟½me, allowing ciï؟½zens to engage with the intervenï؟½ons directly and observe its effects. Careful policy sequencing is therefore criï؟½cal: poorly ï؟½med or misaligned reforms can erode confidence in government abiliï؟½es, undermining legiï؟½macy for future iniï؟½aï؟½ves.

2576 For example, changes in agricultural management require ï؟½me to build the necessary skills and 2577 condiï؟½ons to adopt sustainable and ecological pracï؟½ces. 484 2734 2735 Corporate interests used against public interests 2736 The second major barrier is the exercise of corporate power in ways that undermine public interests. 2737 The high degree of corporate concentraï؟½on across food systems remains an intractable governance 333,498 2738 issue. This is partly due to the out-sized influence of large transnaï؟½onal food and beverage 17 2739 companies who have considerable power and resources at their disposal to block iniï؟½aï؟½ves. 2740 Concentrated market structures, with a few dominant firms, are common in food systems from 2741 producï؟½on (agricultural inputs), trade (commodity trade), and consumpï؟½on (food processing and 14,499 2742 retail), that impact peopleâ€™s access to food e.g. through manipulaï؟½ve pricing, as well as adverï؟½sing 500 2743 and packaging that shapes consumersâ€™ preferences. Corporate actors employ tacï؟½cs such as direct 2744 lobbying of governmental officials to undermine poliï؟½cal prioriï؟½es, including dietary guidelines and 501 2745 regulatory intervenï؟½ons. Another avenue of influence by corporate interests is in the sponsorship of 2746 scienï؟½fic studies that aligns with commercial interests and the disseminaï؟½on of misinformaï؟½on aimed 2747 at discrediï؟½ng independent scienï؟½fic evidenceâ€”such as in cases involving meat industry-sponsored 502,503 2748 scienï؟½sts. Furthermore, public-private partnerships are increasingly co-opted by corporate actors 2749 to shape discourses, parï؟½cularly in the area of sustainability, in ways that reinforce their legiï؟½macy 504,505 2750 and market power. 2751 Weak and fragmented demand for acï؟½on

2753 A perceived lack of effecï؟½ve public demand and acceptance for transformaï؟½ve change is frequently 2754 cited by policymakers and private sector actors as jusï؟½ficaï؟½on for deprioriï؟½sing ambiï؟½ous reforms. 2755 Ciï؟½zens may resist policies that ulï؟½mately serve the public due to the difficulty of weighing short-term 466,486 2756 costs, parï؟½cularly in terms of employment in affected sectors, against long-term benefits. 2757 2758 While grassroot actors play a criï؟½cal role in building public engagement and poliï؟½cal momentum for 506 2759 more equitable and sustainable food systems, their efforts are o en fragmented and constrained by 499,507 2760 entrenched power dynamics and the structural inequiï؟½es of food systems. In some repressive 2761 poliï؟½cal contexts, civil society and grassroot organisaï؟½ons are deliberately excluded or marginalized 2762 by authoritarian regimes, further silencing calls for change. In other contexts, grassroot mobilisaï؟½on 2763 can itself become a source of polarisaï؟½on with some backing policies that might not promote public 2764 good over the long term. 2765 2766 Fragmentaï؟½on in public demand can also stem from a lack of consensus on prioriï؟½es and insufficient 2767 space for dialogueâ€”parï؟½cularly facilitated, inclusive processes that can clarify areas of disagreement 477 2768 and forge common ground. These challenges are especially pronounced with global commitment 2769 mechanismsâ€”such as the Paris Agreement or the Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity Frameworkâ€” 21 2770 into local or naï؟½onal acï؟½ons. Misinformaï؟½on further compounds public disengagement, especially 2771 when systemic challenges like public health are framed as maï؟½ers of individual responsibility. This 2772 framing distorts public understanding of the structural determinants and diminishes support for 508 2773 necessary reforms. The absence of transparent monitoring systems exacerbates the problem by 2774 undermining public trust in transformaï؟½onal policies and fostering uncertainty or misconcepï؟½ons 509 2775 about their potenï؟½al for delivering broad-based benefits. 2776 2777 Unlock financial resources for transformaï؟½on 2778 It has been suggested that financial resources, averaging US$500 billion each year between now and 9 2779 2050, are needed to enable necessary food systems transformaï؟½ons. However, benefits could amount 9 2780 to more than 5 trillion a year. Importantly, not all acï؟½ons require large amounts of financial resources 2781 to be successful. Some can be implemented at relaï؟½vely low cost or can even be cost neutral. However, 2782 they require willingness to reprioriï؟½ze current resource spending. Here, we first look at the potenï؟½al 2783 to unlock resources within the food system, primarily through bundling of different acï؟½viï؟½es that 2784 already happens in the food system. We then explore how to unlock resources by bundling with 2785 broader policy agendas. 2786 2787 Repurposing subsidies, and other food systems investments 2788 Subsidies to the agriculture sector represent a significant resource esï؟½mated at roughly US$851 billion 2789 in the 2020-2022 period (for the 54 countries that report to the Organizaï؟½on for Economic Cooperaï؟½on 2790 and Development). The Food Systems Economic Commission has esï؟½mated that the cost of food 9 2791 systems transformaï؟½on is between US$200-500 billion per year. Without changing the degree of 2792 financing provided to food producï؟½on, seï؟½ng clear condiï؟½ons for the use of these currently earmarked 2793 public funds in favour of public goods such as public health, and environmental security would be a 417 2794 major first step towards transformaï؟½on. At least a third of agricultural subsidies have no public 510 benefit and could be repurposed. In the EU, 82% of the European Unionâ€™s agricultural subsidies favour animal-based agriculture. Farm sector subsidies could be used to incenï؟½vize or invest in

2797 sustainable/ecological intensificaï؟½on, focusing on management of opportunity crops that are currently 427 2798 neglected in relaï؟½on to healthy diet, while promoï؟½ng dietary change. Fisheries subsidies of around 2799 US$35 billion each year, could be used to support sustainable aquaï؟½c producï؟½on pracï؟½ces rather than 2800 contribute to falling fish stocks and declining profitability, which in turn fuels more over-fishing as well 511 2801 as environmentally damaging forms land-based aquaculture. 2802 2803 Shi s in agricultural and other subsidies, or value-added tax (VAT) can reduce health costs and increase 380 2804 health benefits, while lowering climate impacts. Health directed taxaï؟½on of meat products could 2805 yield a 9% decrease in premature deaths associated with red and processed meat consumpï؟½on, with 2806 an esï؟½mated 14% decrease in aï؟½ributable health costs globally, notably in high and middle-income 380 2807 countries. 2808 2809 Measures that seek to distribute income more equitably (via e.g. social protecï؟½on measures or 2810 progressive tax policies), combined with a serious public and private sector commitment to reducing 2811 food loss and waste along all food value chains, can together improve the relaï؟½ve affordability of 2812 healthy dietary paï؟½erns. When these and other measures were combined in a previous modelling 2813 effort, the cost of Planetary Health Diet aligned diets fell by 25-29% in low-income to lower middle- 8 2814 income countries. However, the substanï؟½al restructuring of any sector, including the â€کrepurposingâ€™ of 2815 any kind of subsidy should be handled with care, since the potenï؟½al unintended effects of shi ing 512 2816 resources from one targeted purpose to another can be as significant as the intended effects. 2817 2818 Alignment of investments with the climate-, nature/biodiversity-, and economic agendas 2819 Another way to unlock resources for food systems transformaï؟½on is to align approaches and financial 2820 prioriï؟½es with other criï؟½cally and mutually interdependent policy and investment agendas. A share of 2821 climate or biodiversity finance funding could be turned towards food systems transformaï؟½on. For 2822 example, at the Conference of Parï؟½es (COP) climate summit in Egypt in 2022 (COP27), the COP 2823 Presidency and World Health Organizaï؟½on launched the Iniï؟½aï؟½ve on Climate Acï؟½on and Nutriï؟½on (I- 2824 CAN), to beï؟½er integrate the global delivery of climate change adaptaï؟½on and miï؟½gaï؟½on goals and the 513 2825 policy acï؟½on necessary to meet nutriï؟½on and sustainable food systems goals. This alignment requires 2826 future summit agendas, focused on climate, fossil fuels, renewable energy, employment growth, food 2827 systems, and more, to work towards integraï؟½on of targets and investment modaliï؟½es. However, many 2828 iniï؟½aï؟½ves currently lack coherence and alignment with agriculture and other policies at different levels. 2829 This is the case, for instance, of the many iniï؟½aï؟½ves on tree planï؟½ng which, if showing considerable 2830 benefits for food security, nutriï؟½on and the environment, remain largely uncoordinated (Vansant 514,515 2831 2024. By ramping up these efforts, governments can work more strategically to integrate and 2832 coordinate these mulï؟½ple agendas to repurpose fiscal incenï؟½ves away from economic â€œbadsâ€‌ (e.g. 2833 carbon emissions, biodiversity destrucï؟½on, consumpï؟½on of excessive sugar, salt or trans-fats, etc.) 516 2834 towards public goods. 2835 2836 In 2022, subsidies to the energy sector (currently driven by fossil fuels) were esï؟½mated to be around 517 2837 US$7 trillion. Ending subsidy support to polluï؟½ng energy producï؟½on would allow for a repurposing 518 2838 of resources to alternaï؟½ve public sector investments. The laï؟½er would include regeneraï؟½ve energy 2839 to support diet quality through, for example, solar-powered drying and chilling of perishable foods, and other innovaï؟½ons in cold chain technologies that significantly reduce nutrient-rich food loss and waste. However, project-level climate financing for food systems stands at only 4.3%, or US$28 billion, of global

2842 2843 2844 2845 2846 2847 2848 2849 2850 2851 2852 2853 2854 2855 2856 2857 2858 2859 2860 2861 2862 2863 2864 2865 2866 2867 2868 2869 2870 2871 2872 2873 2874 2875 2876 2877 2878 2879 2880 2881 2882 2883 2884

climate finance for miï؟½gaï؟½on and adaptaï؟½on in all sectors, with specific miï؟½gaï؟½on finance in the food sector being only 2.2% of total climate finance. 473 In their â€œRecipes for a Liveable Planet Reportâ€‌, the World Bank esï؟½mates that annual investments in reducing food systems emissions will need to increase by 18 ï؟½mes, to US$260 billion, to reduce current food system emissions by half by 2030. 473

Different ways to unlock finance for food systems transformaï؟½on could work if banks, investors, and large businesses adopt and report against the Taskforce on Climate Related Financial Disclosures (TCFD), help develop the Taskforce on Nature-related Financial Disclosure (TNFD), and set Science- Based Targets (SBTs), targeï؟½ng Paris-aligned/net zero porï؟½olios. 519 Through these agendas, financial insï؟½tuï؟½ons can commit to a variety of porï؟½olios to shi towards more sustainable food systems, such as deforestaï؟½on-free porï؟½olios (e.g., deforestaï؟½on-free pension funds). Public spending should also create mechanisms for private investment in food systems transformaï؟½on. Central banks can for example introduce stress tesï؟½ng for financial insï؟½tuï؟½ons based on their viability in a future economy that fully accounts for negaï؟½ve externaliï؟½es.

The cost of food systems transformaï؟½on for lower-income groups and countries are beyond their current financing capacity. Li ing their financing constraints is criï؟½cal to unlock the global benefits of transformaï؟½on. 473 Countries with limited fiscal capacity need internaï؟½onal support to invest in food systems sustainability. 517 Wealthy countries and other donors should increase Overseas Development Assistance (ODA) from current low levels (relaï؟½ve to their actual and projected naï؟½onal wealth), as well as climate finance for food systems adaptaï؟½on through mulï؟½lateral development finance insï؟½tuï؟½ons. Wealthy governments can introduce targeted debt relief to facilitate the flow of capital to food systems transformaï؟½ons, including debt for food swaps. This could be supported by innovaï؟½ve financial instruments, such as guarantees for a new type of perpetual/long-dated social and environmental bond, with capped adjustable rates.

## Conclusions: acceleraï؟½ng meaningful acï؟½on

The Commission calls for an urgent, comprehensive approach to food systems transformaï؟½on, centred on the development of context-specific roadmaps that lay out viable evidence-based soluï؟½on sets. Such roadmaps should focus on bundling acï؟½ons, seï؟½ng science-based targets, building inclusive coaliï؟½ons, establishing and building on already exisï؟½ng monitoring and accountability mechanisms, and mobilising financial resources at scale. At the core of the Commission's framework are three foundaï؟½onal elements: (1) recommended food group intake values for the Planetary Health Diet to advance human health; (2) food system planetary boundaries to ensure achieve environmental sustainability; and (3) social foundaï؟½ons to guide the creaï؟½on of just food systems. These values, boundaries, and foundaï؟½ons can provide the guide for target-seï؟½ng and collecï؟½ve acï؟½on, adaptable across geographies and sectors.

The Commission has idenï؟½fied eight soluï؟½ons and 23 acï؟½ons to enable food systems transformaï؟½on. These can be organized into coherent â€کbundlesâ€™ of intervenï؟½ons that simultaneously advance health, environmental and jusï؟½ce goals. Bundling enhances poliï؟½cal feasibility and policy effecï؟½veness, parï؟½cularly when designed to prioriï؟½se the needs of marginalised and disadvantaged populaï؟½ons. The most suitable and effecï؟½ve bundles will vary by context and should be tailored to the specific challenges and opportuniï؟½es of each region and sector.

2887 2888 2889 2890 2891 2892 2893 2894 2895 2896 2897 2898 2899 2900 2901 2902 2903 2904 2905 2906 2907 2908 2909 2910

Cross-sectoral coaliï؟½onsâ€”including actors from public insï؟½tuï؟½ons, private sector, and civil societyâ€” are essenï؟½al to achieve synergisï؟½c governance. These coaliï؟½ons must align with exisï؟½ng and emerging global frameworks, such as the Paris Agreement, the Convenï؟½on on Biological Diversity, and the post- 2030 SDG agenda. Ensuring accountability within such coaliï؟½ons is criï؟½cal. Mechanisms must be established to insulate policymaking from undue corporate influence, while civil society and social movements play a vital role in promoï؟½ng transparency and oversight.

The Commission also underscores the need for acï؟½ons beyond the food system. Its modelling indicates that the PHD can only be achieved within GHG and land-use planetary boundaries if miï؟½gaï؟½on efforts in other sectors are also scaled up. Similarly, shi ing diets among low-income and vulnerable groups requires coupling dietary intervenï؟½ons with broader social protecï؟½on measures. Affordability must be achieved not only by lowering food prices but also by addressing structural poverty and inequiï؟½es. Financing for food systems transformaï؟½on must draw from broader funding streamsâ€”such as those targeï؟½ng climate miï؟½gaï؟½on and biodiversity conservaï؟½on and protecï؟½onâ€”rather than relying solely on current food systems financing.

Finally, the Commission posiï؟½ons jusï؟½ce as both a central goal and a driving force for food systems transformaï؟½on. Food systems cannot be just without ensuring the PHD is affordable and accessible to all, and without significantly reducing transgressions of planetary boundaries. At the same ï؟½me, jusï؟½ce is necessary to overcome the deeply entrenched structural barriers that currently impede transformaï؟½ve change. In this sense, jusï؟½ce is not only an outcome of food systems transformaï؟½on, but a prerequisite for enabling it.

Panel 11: School meals School meals are increasingly recognised as a cost-efficient investment for governments to advance mulï؟½ple policy objecï؟½ves, including health and nutriï؟½on, educaï؟½on, social protecï؟½on, and agriculture. P105 Well-designed and strategically implemented school meal programmes throughout the next 7,000 days â€“ the period of development between the first 1,000 days of life and adulthood â€“ can make a lasï؟½ng impact, parï؟½cularly on the most vulnerable children and adolescents, by improving aï؟½endance and academic performance, and reducing dropout rates. P106

Moreover, school meals programmes make up one of the largest outputs of the global food system, feeding 420 million children daily. Sustainable school meals coupled with consistent and acï؟½on- oriented food educaï؟½on can empower future generaï؟½ons by fostering healthier and more sustainable food habits at a criï؟½cal age when life-long dietary preferences and social aï؟½tudes are formed and carried into adulthood.

In 2021, in recogniï؟½on of the unique potenï؟½al of school meals for improving child health, wellbeing, and educaï؟½on following the COVID-19 pandemic, naï؟½onal governments formed the global School Meals Coaliï؟½on (SMC), declaring their commitment to scaling up and strengthening their naï؟½onal school meals programmes to ensure every child has access to a nutriï؟½ous meal at school by 2030. To support the (now) 105 SMC member states to achieve this ambiï؟½ous mission, the independent Research Consorï؟½um for School Health and Nutriï؟½on, with the input of 160 global experts from more

than 80 organisaï؟½ons, idenï؟½fied evidence-based policy approaches to support the delivery of diverse, balanced, and sustainably produced school meals.

Since these programmes are overwhelmingly domesï؟½cally financed and managed, the policy levers lie in the hands of naï؟½onal governments, allowing for significant change to be made at pace. Incorporaï؟½ng sustainable school meal policies can lower environmental impact, improve climate resilience and leverage food system transformaï؟½on in both high- and low-income seï؟½ngs. Key policy strategies to support posiï؟½ve change in this area include providing nutrient-rich menus, that are predominantly plant-based; sourcing producï؟½on from local farms using sustainable and ecological intensificaï؟½on pracï؟½ces that support agrobiodiversity and climate resilience, as well as structured demand and stable markets to smallholder farmers and processors; shi ing to efficient cooking soluï؟½ons to reduce negaï؟½ve health and environmental impacts; reducing food loss and waste; and ensuring acï؟½on- oriented food and climate educaï؟½on to enable future generaï؟½ons to make sustainable choices. With increased awareness, children also serve as powerful agents of change among their families and broader communiï؟½es. P107

Countries and regional bodies are already demonstraï؟½ng that these acï؟½ons are possible. Europe, for example, now requires a minimum share of organic foods in school menus, P108 and approximately 40% of naï؟½onal school meals programmes currently have agriculture policy objecï؟½ves such as agrobiodiversity, food sovereignty, and climate-smart foods. P109 The naï؟½onal school meals programme in Brazil legislates that at least 30% of the food used in the preparaï؟½on of school meals is locally sourced from family farms. P110 Like Brazil, many countries in Africa and Asia have implemented a food systems approach to school meals, o en involving public-private partnerships.

While financing is o en the barrier to scale-up, food-related costs of providing healthy and sustainable school meals to every child could amount to up to 1% of GDP in low-income countries where school meal coverage is low. P111 When weighing the long-term costs of inacï؟½onâ€”such as the treatment of diet-related health issues and the impact of greenhouse gas emissionsâ€”the case for invesï؟½ng in school meals becomes undeniably compelling. The study idenï؟½fied savings of US$ 120-200 billion in treaï؟½ng diet-related illnesses (including US$ 7-13 billion in low-income countries), and a reducï؟½on in climate change costs of US$ 18-70 billion (including US$ 1-5 billion in low-income countries). The savings were greatest, in each case, for meal composiï؟½ons in line with recommendaï؟½ons for healthy and sustainable dietary paï؟½erns.

In summary, sustainable school meals policies can posiï؟½vely influence dietary preferences of the next generaï؟½on, and promote the adopï؟½on of sustainable producï؟½on pracï؟½ces, broaden the local food basket and sï؟½mulate crop diversity, along with other social and economic development outcomes. The inclusion of school meals on the climate finance agenda, as well as in Naï؟½onally Determined Contribuï؟½ons for both health and the environment are criï؟½cal steps to more resilient, sustainable, and equitable food systems. 2911 2912

Panel 12: 40 Ciï؟½es Iniï؟½aï؟½ve to achieve the Planetary Health Diet for All by 2030 Launched in October 2019 by C40, a global network of almost 100 mayors of leading ciï؟½es commiï؟½ed to addressing climate change, 16 ciï؟½es signed up to an ambiï؟½ous iniï؟½aï؟½ve aiming to achieve the Planetary Health Diet for all by 2030 in ways that are â€œreflecï؟½ve of the culture, geography, and demography of their residentsâ€‌. Seven of the ciï؟½es are in Europe, five in North America, one in South America, two in East Asia and one in South-East Asia.

The ciï؟½es signing up pledged to implement four important measures by 2030:

- Aligning their food procurement to the Planetary Health Diet.
- Supporï؟½ng an overall increase of healthy plant-based food consumpï؟½on by shi ing away from unsustainable, unhealthy diets.
- Reducing food loss and waste by 50% from a 2015 baseline.
- Working with residents, businesses, public insï؟½tuï؟½ons and other organizaï؟½ons to develop a joint strategy for implemenï؟½ng these measures and achieving these goals inclusively and equitably and incorporaï؟½ng this strategy into Climate Acï؟½on Plans, including incorporaï؟½on into naï؟½onally determined contribuï؟½ons to climate goals. Interim evaluaï؟½on In September 2023, 15 of the 16 parï؟½cipaï؟½ng ciï؟½es submiï؟½ed interim reports on their progress. Sufficient data were provided by seven ciï؟½es to quanï؟½fy progress towards the first two measures. Between them, these seven ciï؟½es serve over 400 million meals a year for different groups and establishments including schools and hospitals. Measured against different baseline years from between 2015 and 2022, ciï؟½es reported an increase in their purchasing of plant-sourced protein of 44% (from a mean of 6,356 to 8,667 tonnes) and a decline in their meat purchasing of 19% (from a mean of 16,648 to 12,765 tonnes). In recogniï؟½on of its significant potenï؟½al in shi ing millions of meals in major ciï؟½es to be healthier and more sustainable, this iniï؟½aï؟½ve won the presï؟½gious US$2-million Food Planet Prize awarded by The Curt Bergfors Foundaï؟½on, in June 2024. Many examples of progress towards all four measures were described by ciï؟½es in the interim evaluaï؟½on in 2023 including engaging with ciï؟½zens, restaurants, store owners and town planners to change food environments so healthier and sustainable opï؟½ons are readily available and offering training for businesses on how to reduce food waste. But while excellent examples abound, the extent of progress for each of the four measures across all 16 ciï؟½es remains unknown at the ï؟½me of wriï؟½ng because monitoring systems using similar robust metrics across all ciï؟½es have yet to be put in place. Barriers to progress reported by ciï؟½es Two barriers to progress were commonly reported by parï؟½cipaï؟½ng ciï؟½es: Misaligned naï؟½onal procurement standards: Procurement standards donâ€™t include consideraï؟½on of the environmental impact of foods purchased. To achieve the PHD for all, ciï؟½es are having to make the case for, and then implement this change throughout the enï؟½re public food supply chain on their own.

Insufficient resources for evaluaï؟½on: Budgetary restricï؟½ons, coupled with increasing food prices are curtailing ciï؟½esâ€™ capacity to gather data on their progress towards each of the four aims of this iniï؟½aï؟½ve.

Maximising the impact of the C40 Good Food Ciï؟½es Iniï؟½aï؟½ve Providing addiï؟½onal resources to C40 Good Food Ciï؟½es at this juncture is vital to maximise its impact and value as a global exemplar of transiï؟½oning food systems, by achieving the following:

- Data collecï؟½on: seï؟½ng up and running monitoring mechanisms to assess progress and adjust if off-track for achieving its 2030 goal.
- Coaliï؟½ons: seï؟½ng up mulï؟½-level plaï؟½orms to engage different authoriï؟½es and ciï؟½zens to create a shared vision.
- Advocacy: support for campaigns and other forms of advocacy to inspire naï؟½onal governments and internaï؟½onal partners to increase ambiï؟½on and acï؟½on at the scale needed to achieve PHD for all.
Lessons from C40 all iniï؟½aï؟½ves needed to achieve the Planetary Health Diet for all Mayors from 16 ciï؟½es around the world are leading the way in transforming the food in their ciï؟½es. Similar ambiï؟½ous iniï؟½aï؟½ves are now needed in every city, town and village on our planet to achieve the Planetary Health Diet for all. But these pioneering ciï؟½es highlight that realizing these ambiï؟½ons will require each iniï؟½aï؟½ve to be matched-at its incepï؟½on-by sufficient resources for two core acï؟½viï؟½es:

- implemenï؟½ng a programme of intervenï؟½ons built on exisï؟½ng evidence;
- Robust evaluaï؟½on, built in from incepï؟½on as part of the implementaï؟½on plan, so that progress is monitored in real ï؟½me. This will allow intervenï؟½ons showing promise to be opï؟½mized and those that are ineffecï؟½ve to be stopped, and others started.
